The author of this book says: When I have completed for you, O my sons, this book, which is a part of the science of medicine in its completeness, and have reached the utmost in its clarity and explanation, I saw fit to complete it for you with this treatise, which is a part of the practical work.
Because practical work is completely lacking in our country and in our time, so much so that its knowledge is almost extinct and its effect has ceased. And there remain only a few traces of it in the books of the ancients, which have been copied by hands and marred by error and confusion, until its meanings have become obscure and its usefulness has been diminished.
Therefore, I saw fit to revive it and compose this treatise on it in a way that is explanatory, clear, and concise, and to include pictures of cauterizing irons and other surgical instruments, since this adds to the clarity and facilitates what is needed.
The reason why there is no skilled craftsman in our time is that the craft of medicine is lengthy, and its practitioner must beforehand practice in the science of anatomy, as described by Galen. until he understands the functions, forms, temperaments, connections, and separations of the organs, and knows the bones, nerves, and muscles, their number, origins, and the pulsating and non-pulsating vessels, and the locations of their origins.
Therefore, Hippocrates said that physicians are many in name but few in deed, especially in the craft of surgery, and we have mentioned a part of this in the introduction to this book.
For he who is not knowledgeable in what we have mentioned concerning anatomy will inevitably make mistakes that kill people, as I have often witnessed among those who have imagined themselves skilled in this science and claimed it without knowledge or study.
And that is because I saw an ignorant physician who incised a scrofulous tumor in a woman's neck, and severed some of the arteries of the neck, so the woman bled until she fell dead before him.
And I saw another physician who proceeded to remove a stone from a man who had a fistula, and the stone was large, so he acted rashly and removed it with a piece of the bladder substance. So the man died after about three days, and I had been called to remove it, and I saw from the size of the stone and the state of the patient that I could have done it.
And I saw another physician who earned his living practicing medicine with some of the pimps of our city.
Then it happened to a black boy who had a fracture in his leg near the heel with a wound, and the physician, in his ignorance, hastened and bound the fracture over the wound with bandages and splints very tightly, and did not leave the wound any space to breathe.
Then he indulged his desires, then he left him for days and ordered him not to untie the bandage until his leg and foot swelled and he was near death.
Then I was called to him, and I hastened to untie the bandage, so he gained relief and recovered from his pains, except that the corruption had become entrenched in the limb, and I was not able to stop it.
Then the corruption continued to spread in the limb until he died.
And I saw another physician cauterize a cancerous tumor, and it ulcerated after days until the affliction of its owner became great, and that is because if the cancer is purely of black humor, then it should not be exposed to iron at all.
Except it be in a limb that can be entirely excised.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual work is divided into two parts: work accompanied by safety, and work accompanied by harm in most cases.
And I have warned in every place that comes in this book against work which contains risk and fear, and you should avoid and reject it.
Lest the ignorant find a way to speak and criticize, so take for yourselves caution and circumspection, and for your patients gentleness and deliberation.
And use the best method leading to safety and a good outcome, and avoid dangerous diseases difficult to cure.
And purify yourselves from that which you fear might cast doubt on your religion and your worldly affairs, for it is more enduring for your prestige and higher in this world and the next for your destinies.
For Galen said in some of his precepts, “Do not treat a bad disease, lest you become bad physicians.”
This chapter is divided into three sections.
The first section.
Concerning cautery with fire and cautery with strong medicine, arranged in order from head to foot, with illustrations of the instruments and cautery irons, and everything needed for the work by hand.
And the second section.
Concerning incision, puncture, bloodletting, cupping, wounds, and the extraction of arrows and the like, all arranged in order with illustrations of the instruments.
And the third section.
Concerning setting and reduction, and the treatment of dislocations and the like, arranged in order from head to foot, with illustrations of the instruments.
The first section.
Concerning cauterization.
And before we mention its use, it is necessary to mention its benefits and harms, and in what temperament it is used.
And I say that discussion of the benefits and harms of cauterization is a lengthy discourse, a precise science, and a hidden secret, and a number of wise men have spoken of it.
And they differed in it, and I have shortened their speech for fear of lengthening [the text].
And I say that cauterization generally benefits every ill temperament that is with matter and without matter, except for two temperaments: the hot temperament without matter and the dry temperament without matter.
As for the dry temperament with matter, they differed concerning it. Some said that cauterization is beneficial in it, and others said the opposite: that cauterization is not suitable in an illness that is from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
And it is impossible to cure a hot, dry illness with a hot, dry medicine. And he who says the opposite says that cauterization with fire may benefit from a hot, dry illness that occurs in people's bodies. Because when you add the human body and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you make the human body cold.
And I say as he says, because experience has revealed that to me many times, except that one should not imagine that matter except someone who has practiced and trained in the art of cauterization to a great extent. And he has observed the differences in people's temperaments and the state of illnesses in themselves, their causes, symptoms, and duration.
As for the other temperaments, there is no fear from them, especially cold, wet illnesses, for all physicians have agreed on them and have not differed in the benefit of cauterization in them.
And know, my sons, that among the secrets of treatment is cauterization with fire and its advantage over cauterization with a burning drug, because fire is a simple element whose effect does not exceed the member that is cauterized, and it does not harm another connected member except slightly.
And cauterization with a burning drug may extend its effect to other members, and may cause in the member an illness difficult to treat, and may even kill.  Fire, because of its nobility and the excellence of its substance, does not do that except if one exceeds.
This has become clear to us through experience, long service, attention to the craft, and understanding the realities of the matter.
For this reason I have dispensed with lengthy discussion. If it were not unbecoming of this book of mine, I would have presented to you concerning fire a hidden secret and the manner of its action in bodies and its expulsion of illnesses, with philosophical, demonstrative discourse too subtle for your understanding.
And know, my sons, that they have differed concerning the time suitable for cauterization, and they have considered the best time to be springtime.
And I say that cauterization is suitable at any time, because the harm resulting from the time is consumed by the benefit obtained through cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is for strong, necessary, urgent pains which do not tolerate delay, because of the fear that what follows will be a calamity greater than the slight harm caused by the time.
And let it not occur to you, my sons, what the common people and ignorant physicians imagine, that cauterization which cures a certain illness will never return, and that you should consider it a guarantee.
And the matter is not as they suppose, because cauterization is only like a medicine which alters the temperament and dries the humors which are the cause of the pains.
Except that cauterization is preferred to medicine in the speed of its success, the strength of its action, and the intensity of its effect.
And it is possible that the illness may return after a time, according to the temperament of the patient, the strength of his illness, and the accumulation of humors in his body, and his negligence in acquiring them from foods and similar causes.
Except that the illness in which cauterization is used is a mild illness and in a member with few humors and moisture. Like cauterizing a tooth for pain and the like, for it is possible that the pain may not return in it, and this is in the least.
And as for the common people's saying also that cauterization is the last of medicine, it is a correct saying, not to what they go to, for they believe that no treatment benefits with medicine or without it after the occurrence of cauterization, and the matter is otherwise.
And the meaning of cauterization being the last of medicine is that when we use types of treatment in an illness and those medicines do not work, then we use cauterization as the last thing, and it works, then from here it is said that cauterization is the last of medicine. Not in the meaning to which the common people and many ignorant physicians went.
The ancients mentioned that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they said that because of the moderation of gold and the nobility of its essence, and they said that the place of cauterization does not suppurate, and this is not absolute.
Because I have tried it and found that it only does that in some bodies, not others, and cauterization with it is better and more excellent than iron, as they said, except that if you heat the cautery iron in the fire, made of gold, you will not know when it is heated to the degree you want because of the redness of the gold.
And because it quickly gets cold, and if you increase its heat in the fire it melts and flows, so the artisan is troubled by that. Therefore, cauterization with iron is, for us, faster and nearer to correctness in practice, God willing.
And I have arranged this chapter on cauterization into sections that I have organized from head to foot, to make it easier for the student to find what he wants, God willing.
The first chapter.
On cauterizing the head, one cauterization.
This cauterization benefits from the excess of moisture and coldness in the brain, which are the causes of headache and frequent catarrhs from the head to the region of the eyes and ears, excessive sleep, and toothache and throat pains. And in short, for every illness that appears from coldness, like paralysis, epilepsy, and apoplexy, and similar illnesses.
The image of this cauterization is that you first order the patient to vomit with a purgative medicine that cleanses the head for three or four nights, according to the strength, age, and habit of the patient.
Then you order him to shave his head with a razor, then you seat him before you cross-legged, having placed his hands on his chest.
Then you place the base of your hand on the root of his nose between his eyes; where your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-shaped cautery, whose image is this.
Expose the bone the size of a clove or a carob seed. Then lift your hand; otherwise, if it cools, repeat the process with the same or another instrument. Until you see what I have described of the bone. Then take some salt, dissolve it in water, and soak a piece of cotton in it. Place this on the area and leave it for three days.
Then apply a cotton swab soaked in butter and leave it there until the eschar from the cautery separates. Then treat it with the Quadruple Ointment until it heals, God willing.
And they have said that the longer a wound remains open, the more pus it discharges, and that this is better and more effective.
Some have mentioned cauterizing the skin down to the bone, holding the cautery until some of the bone thickness is burned, then removing the burned bone and treating it afterwards.
Others said that one should be excessive with the cautery, so that it strongly affects the bone, until it falls away from the bone like a small carat or flake. They claimed that this vents the vapors of the head from that place, and the wound should be left open for a long time.
Then treat it until it heals. I do not see these two types of cauterization at all except in some people, and in a haphazard way. In my opinion, leaving it alone is better and safer, because if the natural connection is broken, the head is weakened, as we have seen in other members.
Especially if the patient's head is naturally weak.  The first type of cauterization is safer and better in my opinion, and I use it, so use it and you will be safe, God willing.
Chapter Two.
On cauterizing the head also.
If a chronic pain occurs in the head and persists in the patient, and applications, gargles, snuffs, ointments, and dressings have been used, especially if a single cauterization, as we described, has been done and nothing has helped, then look. If the patient's head is naturally strong and not weak, and he feels intense cold, cauterize him again a little above that. Then cauterize each temple of his head until the thickness of the skin is gone and the amount of bone we described is exposed. And cauterize him at the back of his head in the place known as the hatchet, and lighten your hand in this, and do not expose the bone, for the patient feels a severe pain from this, unlike the pain of all the other cauterizations of the head, and I will mention this cauterization in its place.
The cautery used for the temples and the back of the head should be gentler than the cautery used for the middle of the head, and this is its image.
Chapter Three.
On cauterizing the non-chronic migraine.
If pain occurs in a fissure of the head with headache, and the pain extends to the eye, then let the patient vomit with purgative medicines for the head, and use all the treatment that I mentioned in the divisions of diseases; if that does not work, then cauterization is done in two ways. Either cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine or with iron.
As for cauterization with a burning medicine, take one clove of garlic, peel it and cut its ends from both sides, then incise the place of pain in the temple with a wide scalpel until there is a space under the skin large enough for the clove.
Then insert it under the skin until it disappears, then bind it tightly with bandages, and leave it for about fifteen hours; then unbind it, remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, then dress it.
Dress it with cotton soaked in butter until the place suppurates, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.  If you wish, do this with some of the burning medicines which I gave in the eighteenth chapter on burning medicines.
As for cauterization with iron, it is in this manner: heat the cautery, which is of this form:
It is called the nail-cautery because its head is like the head of a nail, with some pitting and a small projection in the middle.
Then place it on the painful area and hold your hand while you rotate the iron little by little, and the amount you burn of the skin's thickness should be about half of it. Raise your hand so as not to burn the artery underneath and cause bleeding, then soak cotton in salt water and apply it to the area and leave it for three days. Then dress the cotton with ointment, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize this migraine with the knife-like end of the cautery, and be careful not to cut the artery in this chronic migraine in particular.
Chapter Four.
In chronic migraine.
If you treat the migraine with what we mentioned of the aforementioned treatment and what we mentioned in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment does not work, and you see a cause for which what we mentioned of the first cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire is not sufficient. Then it is necessary to heat the knife-like cautery until it turns white, after you have marked the painful area with a line half a finger's length or so, and you should pull your hand once while tightening it until you cut the artery and reach the bone.
Except that you should be careful not to touch the jaw, which moves when chewing, so that you burn the muscle or the nerve that moves it, which would cause spasm.
And be cautious and watchful of the bleeding from the artery that you cut, for there is danger in cutting it. Especially for one who is ignorant of what he is doing and has not been a trained and experienced person; and leaving the work is better.
And the treatment of bleeding that occurs from the artery will be mentioned in its place in the book, God willing, if you see something in the illness that this cauterization cannot treat.
And you saw the patient's body enduring, cauterize in the middle of the head as we described, and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, use the cauterization that we mentioned in the chapter on the incision of the artery with the two-bladed cautery; for it is a better and more effective cauterization than this.
Chapter Five.
On cauterizing earaches.
If pain occurs in the ear due to cold, and it is treated with laxatives and all the treatments that we mentioned in the classification, and the pain does not go away, then heat the cautery which is called the point, whose image is this.
Then, after heating it, apply it with points around the entire ear, or around both if the pain is in both. And keep the cauterization a little distance from the base of the ear, after marking the spot with ink.  The cauterization should be about ten points in each ear, or thereabouts. Then treat the areas until they heal, God willing.
Chapter Six.
Cauterization of the tonsils.
The tonsils treated by cauterization are of the type caused by phlegm, as I mentioned in the classifications of diseases.
Cauterization of the type caused by dryness and nerve tension should be avoided.
If you have treated this type of tonsil with applications, snuffs, and gargles, and your treatment has not been effective, then the patient should be cauterized with three cauterizations. One at the base of the ear, a second slightly below his temple, and a third at the junction of the lips.
Make your cauterization on the opposite side of the diseased area, because relaxation occurs on the apparently healthy side.
The cauterization should be done with a cautery opposite the upper tip of the ear, slightly below the temple, and another in the temple, its length being equal to the length of the thumb.
Lower your hand with the cautery until you burn about half the thickness of the skin. This is the image of the cautery; it is a type of scalpel, the image of which has preceded this, but it is slightly gentler, as you see. The scalpel should be slightly thicker. Then treat the area as previously mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
Cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
If the apoplexy becomes chronic and you treat it with what we have mentioned, and your treatment is ineffective, and the patient does not have a fever, cauterize him in four places. One cauterization on each temple, one cauterization in the middle of the head as previously mentioned, and one cauterization at the back of the head as previously mentioned.
The application of the cautery is as previously described.  It may also be cauterized at the mouth of the stomach, which will be more effective, and then treat it as before.
Chapter Eight.
On cauterizing forgetfulness.
If it is due to phlegm, the patient should first be given large purgatives and pills that cleanse the brain.
Then his entire head should be shaved, and a mustard poultice, as described in the chapter on poultices, should be applied to the back of his head repeatedly, for this is a type of cauterization.
Do this in the same manner as I have described there.  If he is cured by this, well and good; otherwise, cauterize three times at the back of his head, the cauterizations aligned from the top of the head to the nape of the neck.
Make the distance between each cauterization the width of a finger. Then treat the cauterization as described above. If you wish to increase [the treatment] and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle again.
If you wish to increase [the treatment], cauterize the temples, then treat it until it heals. The cautery should be olive-shaped, as previously described.
Chapter Nine.
On cauterizing paralysis and general body relaxation.
You should first purify the head with purgatives and as we have mentioned. Then shave the patient's head, and cauterize: one cauterization in the middle of the head, one on each temple, and one at the back of the head.
And three on the vertebrae of the neck. If, in the case of relaxation of the body, you need more than that, and the patient can tolerate it and the disease is strong and persistent, cauterize with four cauterizations on the vertebrae of his back. And continue cauterizing until you burn most of the skin, then lift your hand, and treat him as previously mentioned until he is healed, God willing. The cautery should be olive-shaped.
Chapter Ten.
On cauterizing epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is caused by phlegm should be cauterized.  First, his brain should be purged with strong purgatives and with the other treatments we mentioned in the classification, if the patient is an adult and able to take medication.
But if he is a child who cannot tolerate medication, then use gargles and masticatories that cleanse the brain for many days beforehand, along with improving his diet.
Then shave his head, and cauterize him with one cautery in the middle of the head, as previously described, and another cautery at the back of the head. And one cautery on each temple of his head. If the patient is strong and able to tolerate it, cauterize him with the cauteries mentioned for the paralytic and the person with relaxation of the body, on the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back; and the cautery should be olive-shaped, as previously described.
If the patient is a child, make the cautery gentle in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
On cauterizing melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is corrupt humors and thick phlegm, cauterize with the cauteries we mentioned for the paralytic.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess inclined to black bile and the patient's body is moist, purge his brain as was explained previously in the classification.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tight cake of linen like a circle, then place it in the middle of his head, while the patient sits cross-legged holding it from every side, then take one pound of old sheep fat.
Then heat it over a fire to a moderate heat, as much as the finger can tolerate when inserted into it, then pour it into the middle of his head in the circle and leave it until it cools. Do this for the patient once a week along with other good treatment until he is cured, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize it with many small punctures without holding your hand to the cautery, but rather let it be a fumigation, for this type of cauterization moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply to it cotton soaked in butter or chicken fat.
Chapter Twelve.
On cauterizing the water that descends into the eye.
If the descending water in the eye becomes apparent to you through the signs which I mentioned in the classification, then act quickly and purge the patient of what fouls his head and cleanse him of all humors and sweat him in the bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and cauterize him in the middle of the head, then cauterize him twice on the temples if the descent of the water began in both eyes, or on one side if it is in one eye.
Then cut with the cautery all the veins and arteries which are under the skin, and let the cauterizations be lengthwise across the width of the temples, and guard against bleeding.
If you see some of it, cut it on the spot with whatever treatment is possible for you, and we will come to the wisdom in cauterizing and cutting the arteries and guarding against bleeding.
And he may be cauterized in the nape of the neck under the two bones with two deep cauterizations.
Chapter Thirteen.
On cauterizing chronic tears.
If the tears of the eye are chronic and persistent, and are from the veins and arteries on the surface of the head, from outside the head, and you are certain that it is from cold, thick, phlegmatic excess, then cauterize it with the cautery that I described in its place at the beginning of the descending water. A cauterization in the middle of the head and two cauterizations on the temples and two cauterizations in the nape of the neck under the two bones. And if you need more, then cauterize one cauterization on each side of the tail of the eye at the end of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter Fourteen.
On cauterizing the foul smell of the nose.
If you treat it with what we mentioned in the section and the treatment does not work, then quickly purge the patient with colocynth for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle with the olive-shaped cautery. Then cauterize with the nail-shaped cautery two cauterizations above the eyebrows, a little below the hair, and be careful of the artery, do not cut it. And this is the image of the nail-shaped cautery.
Chapter Fifteen.
On cauterizing the relaxation of the eyelid.
If the eyelid relaxes from illness or moisture, cauterize the eyelid with one cauterization with this crescent-shaped cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize above the eyebrows a little, two cauterizations on each side, and keep them distant from the temples, and the length of each cauterization should be the length of the eyebrow.
And do not exaggerate with the cauterization, but only to the extent that a third of the skin burns, and the shape of the cautery should be in this manner.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If its margins turn inward, so that the eye is inverted, then cauterization is of two types. Either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a burning drug. As for cauterization with fire, you should order the patient beforehand to leave his eyelashes, if he is one who plucks them, until they grow long and even.
For if you cauterize it while they are growing, then bind his eyes with a bandage lest they move until they grow.  Then when they have grown and become even, place the patient's head in your lap, then mark a sign on the eyelid with ink in the shape of an "as" leaf.
The beginning of the mark should be near the margins. Then place under the eyelid a cotton swab soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a lizard. Then heat a cautery iron, this is its form.
Then cauterize according to the mark little by little in many times until the surface of the skin, which is in the shape of the "as" leaf, is entirely burnt, especially its surface. A sign of the correctness of your work is that you see the eyelid curled up and the hair raised from the flesh of the eye.
Then lift your hand and leave it for three days, then apply a cotton swab with ointment until the scab is removed, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If some of the hair returns after a time and the eyelid relaxes.
Then repeat the cauterization on that place as you did before. If the hair is in the lower eyelid, pull it out until it returns to its natural place and does not prick the eye. As for cauterization with a burning medicine, it is that you instruct the patient to leave the margins until they grow long and even.
Then make from paper the shape of an as leaf. Then take from the usual soap and from unslaked lime, from each one a weight of a dirham or so, and grind them both well together, and do this quickly lest it dry. Then spread some of it on the paper which you made in the shape of the as leaf and place it on one or both eyelids and place under the eye cotton soaked in egg white, and the patient's head in your lap.
And place your index finger above the medicine and press it slightly while you move it whenever the patient feels the sting of the medicine, because he will feel a sting like fire. As long as he feels the sting, leave the medicine and move it with your finger. When the sting subsides, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And look; if you see the eyelid has risen as it rises when cauterized with fire or with cutting, otherwise repeat applying the medicine to the place where the medicine did not affect it and did not blacken, until you complete your work and the eyelid is cauterized. Then put cotton with butter on it until the burning subsides.
Then treat it with palm ointment or another until it heals.  It is necessary for you during the work to be extremely careful that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye. If the eyelid relaxes after a few days and we fear something like hair in the eye.
Then reapply the medicine to that place of the eyelid which has relaxed, specifically as you did first, then treat it until it heals. And this is the image of the as leaf.
Know that people's eyes differ in size, so let your cauterization be according to that. The correct method is not hidden from one who is experienced in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cauterizing the cancerous sore which appears in the corner of the eye.
If you treat the cancerous sore with what we mentioned in the classification of diseases and your treatment is not effective, then it should be cauterized in this manner.
You should order the patient to place his head in your lap, and a servant should hold his head between your hands in such a way that it does not move or shake.
Then you place a cotton swab moistened with egg white or the saliva of a frog on his eyes, then you heat the cautery iron, whose image is this.
It should be hollow like the quill of an eagle's feather at the one end which performs the cauterization; and if you wish it to be perforated at the other end, and if you wish it to be solid like an awl, but this hollow one is better for your work, God willing.
Then, squeeze the sore if it is open and remove the pus and dry it; or if it is not open, open it and remove its pus.  Then apply the cautery iron to it while it is very hot, holding it with your hand until it reaches the bone.
And move your hand slightly during cauterization, away from the eye toward the nose, lest your hand err or the patient be disturbed, causing the cautery iron to fall onto the white of the eye and damage it.  If you reach the bone on the first cauterization, well and good; otherwise, reapply the cautery iron a second time if necessary.
Leave it for three days, then apply cotton soaked in butter and treat it with drying ointment until it heals. If forty days pass and it does not heal, then apply a strong, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and denuded, as will be mentioned in its chapter, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing a fistula, mentioned by some of the ancients:  You approach the location of the fistula, incise it, then place a thin cone in the incision itself; this is its image.
Then pour into it the weight of a dirham of molten lead, holding the cone firmly and securely; the patient must not move at all, lest the molten lead flow into his eye. It is necessary to place cotton soaked in egg white or water on the patient's eye.
For the lead will burn the fistula and cure it wonderfully. If the fistula heals with the cauterization and treatment we have mentioned, well and good; otherwise, it is necessary to use the perforation of the nose and return the fistula to the nasal passage, as will be detailed in its appropriate place.
Chapter Eighteen.
On cauterizing fissures of the lip.
A fissure often occurs in the lip, called a hair, especially in the lips of children; it occurs frequently. If you treat this fissure with what we mentioned in the section and the treatment is not effective, heat a small, knife-shaped cautery of this shape.
Let its cavity be like the thinness of a knife, then apply it, heated quickly, into the fissure itself until the cauterization reaches the depth of the fissure; then treat it with the kyrioty until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
On cauterizing fistulas occurring in the mouth.
If a swelling appears at the root of the gums, or in the palate, or at the roots of the molars, then it suppurates and bursts, and from the flow of pus a fistula results, and you treat it but the treatment is not effective, then you should heat a cautery to the extent that it fits into the fistula.
Then insert it, heated, into the opening of the fistula, and hold your hand until the heated iron reaches its depth and end. Do this once or twice, then treat it afterwards with what we have mentioned of treatment until it heals, God willing.
If the material ceases and it heals, well and good; otherwise, it is necessary to examine the place and remove the decayed bone according to what will come in its chapter, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On cauterizing loose molars and gums.
If the gums are relaxed from moisture and the teeth are loose, and you have treated them with medicine and it has not worked, then place the patient's head in your lap, then heat the cautery iron, the image of which comes after this, after you have placed the tube on the tooth.
And insert the hot cautery iron quickly, and hold your hand a little until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth, then raise your hand, then return the cautery iron several times as you wish, then let the patient fill his mouth with salt water.
And let him hold it for an hour and then spit it out; for the loose tooth will be fixed, and the relaxed gum will tighten, and the corrupt moisture will dry.
Chapter twenty-one.
On cauterizing a toothache.
If the toothache is from cold, or if there are worms in it, and treatment with medicines has not been effective, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with butter, or cauterization with fire.
As for cauterization with butter, you take cow's butter and boil it in an iron spoon or in a shell, then take cotton and wrap it around the end of a probe, then dip it in the boiled butter and quickly place it on the aching tooth and hold it until it cools.
Then repeat it several times until the heat of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.  And if you wish, you may dip wool or cotton in cold butter and place it on the aching tooth, and place a heated iron on top of it until the fire reaches the bottom of the tooth.
As for cauterization with fire, take a brass or iron tube, and let it have some thickness so that the heat of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth. Then heat the cautery iron which has the described shape, and place it on the tooth itself, and hold your hand until the cautery iron cools; do this repeatedly.
Then the pain will go away, either that same day or the next day. After this cauterization, it is necessary that the patient fill his mouth with good butter and hold it for an hour, then spit it out.  This is the image of the cautery iron.
Cauterize with whichever end you wish, and according to what is possible. This is the image of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On cauterizing fistulas.
If fistulas are due to phlegm and cold humors and do not respond to ripening with medication, and you desire a quick ripening, then heat a hollow cautery iron, which has this image, perforated at both ends.
So that the smoke exits at the other end during cauterization, place it carefully on the fistula itself, once and twice if necessary, until you reach the depth of the fistula. If the fistula is small, make the cautery iron the size of the fistula.
Then leave it for three days and apply a cotton swab soaked in ointment until what was burned by the fire is gone. Then treat it with ointment and tents until it heals, God willing.
Chapter twenty-three.
On cauterization for hoarseness and shortness of breath.
If humors prevail in the trachea, especially if this is accompanied by coldness of temperament, then the patient should first be purged with purgative medicines, then cauterize with a cautery in the hollow of the throat at the base of the larynx in the lower position.
Beware of reaching the larynx with the cautery and do not burn more than half of the skin. Then apply another cautery at the joint of the neck at the end of its last vertebra, and let the cautery be nail-shaped in the manner previously described.
Then treat him with what has been previously mentioned until he recovers, God willing.
Chapter twenty-four.
On cauterizing lung disease and cough.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold humors, and the patient has no fever or consumption, and the disease is chronic, then apply two cauteries above the clavicles in the low, soft, empty places, and another cautery in the middle of the chest between the breasts.
And the cautery should be nail-shaped, in the manner previously described. And if you wish, let your cautery be by puncturing with the cautery called the point, whose image has been previously shown in puncturing earache, and let the punctures be from thirty points or thereabouts.
Then treat him with what has been previously mentioned until he recovers.  You may also make a cautery with three branches in this manner, and it hastens the cauterization because you cauterize with it three places at once. This is the image of the cautery.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus is dislocated due to slippery humors, or if it does not remain fixed when it is reduced from its dislocation, so that this becomes a habit to relapse and then dislocate at the slightest movement, as we have witnessed, then it is necessary to first reduce the dislocation. Then the patient lies on his back or on his healthy side, and then the skin inside the armpit is raised upwards with the fingers of your left hand if the joint is dislocated inwards.
Then heat the cautery iron with two heads, which is of this form.
Then cauterize the skin with it until it penetrates to the other side, and the form of the cauterization will be four cauterizations. It may be cauterized with a cautery iron with three heads, in which case the form of the cauterization will then be six cauterizations, and the heads will be on the thinness of a rose petal.
And this is the image of the cautery iron with three heads.
And this number may be increased by one, so that the cauterizations are eight. Then apply to the cauterization the pounded balls with salt, and then let the patient rest and not move the limb for a time until it strengthens, and if the dislocation is upwards, and that is rarely the case.
Cauterize above the shoulder with one good, nail-shaped cauterization or many cauterizations in a dotted pattern, for the joint will strengthen, and the moisture will leave, and the patient will heal.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In cauterizing the stomach.
If coldness and excessive moisture occur in the stomach, to the extent that it is disturbed from its temperament, and frequent catarrhs occur in it, and it is treated with various treatments, but they are not effective, then the patient should lie on his back and extend his legs and arms, then cauterize three cauterizations. One cauterization below the xiphoid process, the size of a finger, with a nail-shaped cautery, and two cauterizations lower down on either side of the first cauterization, so that the shape of the cauterizations is triangular, and spaced apart so that they do not join if they suppurate, and the depth of the cauterization is about two-thirds the thickness of the skin, and this is the shape of the cauterizations.
In this manner and to this extent, no more.
And if you wish, cauterize it with one large cauterization in the middle of the stomach, in this manner.
And the cautery with which you cauterize this single cauterization is to be in the shape of a circle, in this manner.
And the stomach may be cauterized by puncturing for whoever shrinks from this cauterization; and that is that you mark points on the stomach with ink to the extent that you want, then cauterize it with a punctate cautery, then treat it with the treatment which has preceded until it heals.
Chapter twenty-seven.
In the cold cauterization of the liver.
If there arises from the liver a pain due to coldness and dampness, or due to a subtle wind, so that it has departed from its natural temperament an excessive departure, and the patient has been treated with what we mentioned in the classification, and that has not availed. Then it is necessary that the patient lie on his back, and three cauterizations are marked with ink in this shape and this exact amount on the liver, below the ribs where the human elbow ends.
And after each cauterization, a cauterization should be the thickness of a finger, and the cauterization should be along the body straight, and do not press down firmly with the cautery iron.
And let the amount of thickness of the skin that you burn be about half, no more, and let the patient stand on his feet; and if he is not standing, let him be lying down with his legs extended and his arms raised.  And this is the image of the cautery iron.
And you may be able to cauterize these cauterizations with the knife-like cautery iron if you have gentleness and skill in the craft, and be careful lest you overdo the cauterization and burn all the skin, and burn the abdomen and reach the intestines, for the skin there is thin, so know that.
Chapter twenty-eight.
In cauterizing a swelling of the liver.
If an abscess appears in the liver and you wish to know if that swelling is in the flesh of the liver or in its peritoneum, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient will find heaviness and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the peritoneum of the liver, there will be intense sharpness with the pain, and you see that the physicians have been unable to treat it, then it is necessary that the patient lie on his back, then mark the swollen area with ink.
Then heat the cautery in the fire; it is the cautery that resembles a needle, and this is its image. And cauterize it with one cauterization until you burn all the skin and the cauterization reaches the peritoneum until all the matter comes out, then treat it with the treatment of abscesses until it heals.
And this type of cauterization should not be used except by someone whose training in the art of medicine has been long and who has experienced these diseases many times by practice; then he may undertake such work, but leaving it is better in my opinion.
Chapter twenty-nine.
On cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients mentioned cauterization with the roots of rhubarb for a cold fistula in this way: that you take one long, dry root of rhubarb, the longest you can find, and it should be as thick as a finger, then dip it in oil and heat it in the fire. Then you cauterize with one cauterization between the connection of the clavicle and the neck, and two small cauterizations a little below the jugular veins, leaning towards the side under the chin, and two large cauterizations above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
And two cauterizations also between the fifth and sixth ribs, leaning slightly backward, and another cauterization in the middle of the chest and another above the stomach, and three cauterizations from behind: one between the shoulders and two on the sides of the spine, lower than the cauterization which is between the shoulders.
And you should not deepen your hand with the cautery, but it should be a superficial burning of the skin.  Some of the ancients mentioned that some people used an iron cautery like a file, heating it and inserting it between the ribs until it reaches the tumor itself.
And the pus is discharged as we mentioned in liver tumors.  In this method of cauterization there is great danger; either the patient dies immediately or a fistula forms at the site which does not heal.
Chapter Thirty.
On cauterizing the spleen.
If you treat a spleen ailment with the treatments mentioned in the sections and your treatment is not effective, then cauterization is appropriate in three ways, all correct: one is to cauterize it with three or four cauterizations aligned along the length of the spleen.
in the shape of the liver cauterizations whose shape we have described before, and between each cauterization and the next should be the width of a finger or a little more. The nature of the cautery should be as we described in the cauterization of the liver, and do not deepen your hand with the cauterization; the patient's position is lying on his back.
The other method is in the flank, and you lift the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left flank ends, and you lift the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left flank ends, and your lifting of the skin should be across the body so that the cauterizations fall along the body.
Then you insert two cauteries, very carefully protected, until you pierce the skin on the other side, then you remove the cautery, so there will be four cauterizations; and if you wish, you may cauterize with the other cautery with three points.
Then you treat the cauterized area after you let it discharge pus for many days; this is more effective than all the treatments mentioned before.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On cauterizing dropsy.
Cauterization is only beneficial in ascitic dropsy specifically. If you treat the patient with dropsy using the various treatments we mentioned in the classification and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize four times around the navel and once on the stomach.
Another cauterization on the liver, one on the spleen, and two on his back between the vertebrae, one opposite his chest and another opposite his stomach. The depth of the cauterization should be approximately the thickness of the skin. Then you leave the cauterization open to discharge pus for a long time.
And do not neglect the patient's treatment after cauterization with what is necessary to gather the humors for him, so that healing will hasten. The shape of the cautery with which the back is cauterized is nail-shaped, as its image was previously shown, and that with which the abdomen is cauterized is olive-shaped.
Chapter thirty-two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.
If they swell in dropsy and fill with yellow water, then it is necessary to cauterize on the back of the foot in the depression between the little finger and the ring finger.
And place your hand with the cautery and do not bend it, then raise your hand and do not return it at all, for the yellow water will exude, and the cautery is of this shape.
Then cauterize on the legs, two cauterizations on each leg, and the cauterization is done with the sharp end of the cautery, and the cauterization is along the length of the leg, one below the knee and another below it towards the middle of the leg, and on each thigh two cauterizations.
And leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time, so that the water may exude from it, then treat it with all that we have mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On cauterization for diarrhea.
If the diarrhea is from cold and dampness, so that it weakens the retaining and digestive powers in the stomach and intestines, and this has been treated with various treatments and it has not healed, and you see that the patient can tolerate cauterization and is strong, then cauterize him with a large cauterization on his stomach. As was previously described in cauterizing the stomach with the circular cautery iron, and four cauterizations around the navel with the gentle nail-shaped cautery iron, and one or two large cauterizations on the coccyx.
If you see that the dampness is abundant and the patient can tolerate it, then cauterize him on the pubis and on each flank, and perhaps add two small cauterizations on the stomach near the large one, for it is a successful treatment whose benefit does not fail, God willing.
Chapter thirty-four.
On cauterizing hemorrhoids of the anus.
If there are chronic, numerous, or single hemorrhoids in the anus, and they are from thick, cold humors or putrid dampness, and they have been treated with what we mentioned in the section, but the treatment has not worked, then cauterize the patient with three cauterizations on the lower part of the sacrum, slightly below the coccyx, in a triangular arrangement.
And a cauterization below the navel by the width of two fingers. And if you estimate that his stomach has become cold and his food is not digested, and you see his face swollen, then cauterize him on the stomach with a large cauterization as described before, and a cauterization on his liver. And another on his spleen with a nail-shaped cautery.  Leave the cauterization open for a time, then treat him until he heals, God willing.
Chapter thirty-five.
On cauterizing warts.
After cutting it, if the wart is cut, heat the cautery which resembles a needle, then insert it, hot, into the same cut wart and deepen your hand until the cautery reaches the mouth of the vein from which the blood flows. Do this once or twice.
If the warts are numerous, cauterize each one as we have described, then treat them with ointments suitable for them until they heal. And if you cauterize it with a large cautery also on cotton, it was more effective in benefit.
Chapter thirty-six.
On cauterizing the fistula which is in the anus and its surroundings.
If the patient does not respond to the incision and the procedure we described in its place and shrinks from that, then perhaps he will heal with cauterization. If a fistula occurs and the pus and corrupt humors flow from it for a long time, then the first thing you should do is measure it with a thin probe.
Then know its depth with the probe, then heat the cautery which resembles a needle, then insert it hot into the fistula itself, in a straight line along the depth of the fistula and the extent to which the probe entered, and repeat the cauterization until all those corrupt bodies are burned, once or twice or three times, according to your need.
And be careful not to burn a nerve if it is there, or a large vein, and if the fistula opens into the body of the bladder or into the body of the intestine.
Guard against all these places.  You do this only if the fistula is in a fleshy place and you have estimated that it is not penetrating. Then treat the place until it heals. If the place closes and the matter ceases from it and remains so for a time, then know that it has completely healed.
And if the matter does not cease from it, then know that it is penetrating, or that in its depth there is a corrupt bone or something similar, which will be mentioned in its place.
Chapter thirty-seven.
On cauterizing the kidneys.
If pain occurs in the kidneys from cold or a violent wind, and because of that the patient's sexual function decreases, then it is necessary to cauterize him on the loins, on the kidneys themselves.  Cauterize each kidney with the nail-shaped cautery mentioned previously, and perhaps you may cauterize a third time in the same session, so that there are three cauterizations in a row, which will be more effective.
Chapter thirty-eight.
On cauterizing the bladder.
If weakness and relaxation occur in the bladder from cold and moisture, so that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize him with one cauterization below the navel on the bladder where the pubic hair begins, and one cauterization to the right of the navel and another to the left.
And the distance after cauterization on each side should be the width of a thumb's breadth, and cauterize with one cauterization in the lower back, or two if needed. The cautery should be nail-shaped, as previously mentioned.
Chapter thirty-nine.
On cauterizing the uterus.
If a substance from cold and moisture occurs in the uterus, so that it is prevented from conception, and its menstrual flow is corrupted and its course is difficult, or pain occurs to it upon its arrival, then it should be cauterized with three cauterizations around the navel, as we mentioned in cauterizing the bladder. And one cauterization on cotton in the lower back, or two, and the cautery should be nail-shaped.
Chapter forty.
On cauterizing a dislocated hip.
Sometimes errant moist substances gather in the hip joint, and this becomes a reason for its displacement. Its sign is that the leg is longer than the other when one compares them to each other, and one finds emptiness in the place of the dislocation.  Then one should cauterize the patient on the hip joint itself with a semi-circular cauterization.
After you have drawn with ink around the joint as it rotates, so that the joint itself falls in the middle of the circle. And the cautery, whose image is presented in cauterizing the stomach; if you do not have this cautery, then cauterize with three cauterizations using the large olive-shaped cautery.
And let the cauterization be deep enough to encompass the whole thickness of the skin, then treat him until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter forty-one.
On cauterizing sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint, and the cause of this is cold and moisture, and you have treated the patient with what we mentioned in the section, and the treatment has not worked, and this has persisted, then it is necessary to evacuate the thick humors from the patient with the bitter apple or the prickly pear and the like.
Then cauterize it, and the cauterization is of two types: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire.  Cauterization with fire is done in many ways; one of them is to cauterize on the hip joint itself with three triangular cauterizations in this manner.
Make them of a suitable depth, and the distance between each cauterization and the next should be the width of a finger. The cautery iron should be olive-shaped.  You may also cauterize one in the middle, on the head of the hip itself, making four cauterizations. And if you wish, cauterize it with the circle which we mentioned previously in cauterizing a dislocated hip—a single cauterization.
So that the circle encompasses the entire hip and burns through the entire thickness of the skin. This is the manner of cauterization for sciatica if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If it extends to the thigh and leg, then cauterize two cauterizations on the thigh at the place where the patient indicates pain, and one cauterization above the heel, four fingers towards the lateral side slightly. The cautery iron should be knife-shaped, and the depth of the cauterization should be only the thickness of the skin.
And if he indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, cauterize where he indicates with a pointed cautery iron three or four times, or more if necessary. And if he indicates pain below the knee towards the leg, cauterize there with one knife-shaped cauterization.
And be careful in all your cauterizations not to cauterize to the point of burning a nerve or a large artery, lest you thereby cause the patient a serious injury or death.
And I have seen one and another who was cauterized above the heel, and the cauterization was excessive, so that the leg became inflamed until the inflammation reached the foot, and it became completely perforated, and the entire leg was corrupted, and then diarrhea and death followed. If the pain is in both sides, cauterize both in the same manner.
And some learned physicians have mentioned regarding the cauterization of the hip a cauterization of this type:  A cup-like instrument is made of iron, its diameter being half a span, and at its mouth it should be the thickness of a date pit, or a little less, and inside that cup is another cup, and a third cup.
And the distance between each cup will be the width of the thumb, and the cups will be open on both sides, and their height will be about a finger-breadth or two finger-breadths, and a handle of well-secured iron is made for them. And this is its image.
Then it is heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, then it is placed on the hip joint, the patient leaning on the healthy side, and three round cauterizations are performed at once. Then he is left for three days, and it is bandaged with butter, and the wound is left open for some days.
Many, then treat it with ointment until it heals.  The author of this book says that this type of cauterization is rarely used because of its ugliness and the horror of its appearance, and because few are found who can endure it, although it is among the best cauterizations for those who endure it and its application is correct.
And as for cauterization with burning medicines, it is that you make two cup-like things resembling the rings into which the hinges of a door descend, of copper or iron.
Their rims will have a height of about two finger-breadths or so, and the distance between them will be the thickness of a finger, and they will be open at the bottom and open at the top, and they will be fastened to each other in this manner.
Then lower them onto the hip joint, the patient lying on his healthy side, and firmly hold your hand, then pour between the two circles some strong water, which is warmed a little by fire, and hold it for the duration of an hourglass.  The patient will endure its stinging and burning, for he will feel a burning like fire.
Until the burning subsides, then raise your hand with the instrument and wipe the strong water from the whole hip with fresh water, then leave it for three days and apply butter to it until the blackness of the burn disappears, and leave it for days while pus flows from it, for that is more effective.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make a mold for it to hold the water, in the same way as you made for the hip. This is the description of the strong water, and I have described it in the chapter on the preparation of medicines.
Take one part of alkali salt and one part of unslaked lime, crush them, and place them in a new pot which has a small hole pierced in its bottom, just large enough for a probe to enter, and place another glazed pot underneath.
And pour fresh water over the alkali and lime to cover them by a finger's breadth, after you have pressed them well with your hand, and leave the pot until the strong water descends into the glazed pot below, then collect all that water and pour it onto more fresh lime and alkali.
For then it will be very strong, useful in many medical operations and in cauterizing various members, because it acts exactly like fire.  There are also other drugs which cauterize the hip, such as thapsia, bdellium honey, and lime with soap, ground together.
Galen claimed, recounting it from an ancient man, a treatment for hip pain and sciatica, and greatly magnified its importance, claiming that no other treatment is needed and that it cures in a single application.
To the point that he might enter the bath carried and leave it cured. This is done by taking green verdigris; if green is not available, take fresh dry verdigris, finely grind it with some fat, and apply it to the hip where the pain is, or to the leg, or to the thigh.
It is bound on and left for three hours, or as long as the patient feels the burning subside, then he is taken to the bath. When his body is moistened, he is put into the bath; the pain will go away and he will be cured, God willing. If he is not cured, reapply the poultice after ten days.
Then, God willing, he will be cured. Know that no one should use cautery with these medicines except after evacuating the body. Dioscorides mentioned that if goat hair is used to cauterize sciatica, it is beneficial.
The cautery is done in this manner: wool is taken and soaked in old oil and placed on the deep place between the thumb of the hand and the forearm, closer to the forearm. Then take dry goat hair.
Heat it in the fire until it becomes a coal, then place it on the wool and leave it until it goes out. Then take another and continue doing this until the sensation reaches from the middle of the arm to the hip, and the pain subsides, God willing. This type of cautery is called goat-hair cautery.
Chapter forty-two.
On cauterizing back pain.
Pain may occur in the back from many causes, either from a fall, a blow, excessive vomiting, and the like. It may be from the effusion of a cold, moist substance. Cauterization is only done in this type alone.
That which is from the effusion of a cold substance, it is necessary, after evacuating the patient with hellebore and the like, to cauterize on his back where the pain is, three rows across the width of the table itself, after marking the spot with ink. In each row, five cauterizations or more, according to what you see of the patient's tolerance and strength.
The cauterization should be with a pointed cautery.  And if you wish, cauterize it three or four times with a medium-sized nail cautery, in this manner.
Chapter forty-three.
On cauterizing the beginning of a hump.
This ailment often affects small children.  Its initial sign in a child is shortness of breath when standing and moving, and you find at the end of his spinal vertebrae a bead that has emerged, protruding over the other beads.
If you see that and want to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery, in this manner. Let the cauterization take hold on each side of the vertebra evenly.  And if you wish, cauterize around the vertebra with a pointed cautery, in two or three rows, and let the points be close to one another.  Then treat the area until it heals with what we have mentioned.
And be cautious about using cautery in a hump that is from a spasm of the nerve.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On cauterizing gout and joint pains.
If joint pains are from cold humors that settle in any member of the body, and if the pain occurs in the legs, then it is the custom of physicians to call that specifically gout.
If gout caused by cold is treated with the types of treatment we mentioned in the classification and the pain does not go away, then cautery will remove it.  This is done by cauterizing around the joint of the legs with many cauterizations after evacuation. The cautery iron should be olive-shaped and moderate, in this manner.
And if you need to puncture the surface of the leg, do so with a pointed cautery iron. If the pain ascends to the knees or to the other joints, which often happens, cauterize each knee three or four times on each side with this same olive-shaped cautery iron.
If you need more cauterization than this, cauterize, but do not deepen your hand with the cautery; rather, it should be about the thickness of the skin only. If the pain ascends to the hips or to the back, use what we mentioned of cauterization in its chapter.
If the pains are only in the hands, puncture around the forearms as if circling two rows. If the pains remain in the fingers, puncture each joint with a puncture and the metacarpus. If the pain ascends after days to the elbows or shoulders,
cauterize them on each side.  The patient should not lack good management and the taking of medicines, for if he improves his diet and evacuates the phlegm, he will be cured with this cauterization, God willing.
Chapter forty-five.
On cauterizing hernias.
If a hernia appears in the groin and some of the intestines and fat descend to the testicle, and this is recent and early, then you should order the patient to abstain from food that day and to use laxatives that will evacuate his bowels.
Then he should lie on his back in front of you, and you should tell him to restrain himself until the fat or intestine protrudes, then you should return it with your finger. Then you should mark with ink below the hernia on the pubic bone a sign resembling a semicircle, its ends towards the upper part of the body.
Then you heat a cautery of this shape.
Until it becomes white and throws off sparks. Then you return the intestine or fat to the abdomen. Then a servant places his hand on the place so that the intestine does not protrude.  And you have spread the patient’s legs apart and placed a pillow underneath, and another servant ties his legs.
And another on his chest holds his hands. Then you lower the cautery onto the same mark, and your hand with the cautery should be upright and straight, and you hold it until it reaches the bone, or you repeat it again if it did not reach the bone the first time.
And you should exert yourself to prevent the intestine from protruding while you are cauterizing, lest you burn it, which would cause the patient either death or a great calamity. And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your work will not succeed. And the cautery should be gentle for children according to their size, and for adults according to their size.
Then you treat the cauterized area after three days with butter until the scab from the fire goes away. Then you treat it with other ointments until it heals. And let the patient lie on his back for forty days until the wound is sealed.
And you should make his food during his treatment such that it softens his bowels, lest the intestine protrude during straining and defecation. Then, if he wants to get up after forty days, you use a tight bandage and keep it on for another forty days. And he should reduce exertion, overeating and drinking, and loud crying.
For if this procedure is used in this way, he will recover completely, God willing. And I will mention the treatment of hernias by incision in its chapter, God willing.  And as for the hernia that occurs in the rest of the abdomen and is incipient, if you want it not to increase, cauterize a round mark on it according to its size. And let what you burn of the skin be about two-thirds of it. Then treat it as we have mentioned, for it will not increase, God willing. And some of the ancients mentioned that the hernia should be cauterized with a triangular cautery in this manner.
After you mark the hernia with ink, and make the horizontal line in the upper part of the hernia and the other line downwards, and you place one nail-like cautery in the middle, the first cautery is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter forty-six.
In cauterizing the fistula.
If pain occurs in some of the limbs from a fall or a blow, and that pain lasts a long time and medication does not alleviate it, then it should be cauterized with a pointed cautery. The cauterization of each limb should be according to its size, smallness, weakness, and strength.
And if the pain and fistula persist, then if it heals from the first cauterization, well and good; otherwise, repeat the cauterization upon it, because it is the nature of these pains to spread from the limb to what is near it.  Therefore, you should pursue them with cauterization until the patient is healed.
Chapter forty-seven.
In cauterizing leprosy.
As for those with leprosy, they may benefit greatly from cauterization, especially the type of leprosy that comes from the putrefaction of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize, observe whether the leprosy is beginning, and if you have treated it with what we mentioned in the classification, and it has not diminished nor stopped, and you fear that corruption will overcome the entire constitution of the patient, then cauterize on the head, five cauterizations. One in the middle of the head, as is customary; the second below it towards the forehead at the end of the hair; two on the temples; and one at the back on the nape of the neck.  And press your hand with the cautery a little until it slightly affects the bone.
And scales will peel off from it, so that the thick vapors may more easily escape from it.  And cauterize it also on the spleen in the same way as mentioned before.  But if the leprosy has persisted in the patient and has appeared clearly, then you should cauterize with these cauterizations which we mentioned on the head. And a cauterization on the tip of the nose; two cauterizations on the cheeks; two cauterizations on the vertebrae of the neck; six on the vertebrae of the back; one large cauterization on the coccyx at the end of the spine; another above it in the same region; and two on the hips, one on each hip. And two on the knees, one on each knee; and two on the shoulders; and two on the elbows; and two on the breasts; and cauterize on each joint of the joints of his fingers and toes, one cauterization; and on each heel of his feet and forearms.
And be careful of the nerve which is on the back of the heels, lest you burn them.  And it may be cauterized, one cauterization on the pubic bone, and another on the stomach, and another on the liver.  And know that the more cauterizations you do, the more beneficial and effective it will be.
And know that the patient does not feel the pain of cauterization as much as a healthy person, because his body is numb.  And the cauteries should be of various sizes, large and small, according to the organs and joints, as mentioned before concerning the descriptions of the cauteries.
Then treat the cauterization with finely ground cress mixed with honey and other treatments until it heals, God willing.
Chapter forty-eight.
Concerning the cauterization of numbness: When a limb becomes numb and is treated with medicines, ointments, and dressings, but does not heal, cauterize the numb limb with cauteries according to the size of the limb, whether large or small. Let your cauterization be deep, penetrating the thickness of the skin slightly.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  And sometimes cauterization is used for some numbness that appears in the hand and foot, in the vertebrae of the back, at the exit of the nerve that moves that limb, and the numbness will go away.
And let no one undertake this except one who is knowledgeable about the anatomy of the limbs and the exits of the nerves that move the body.
Chapter forty-nine.
Concerning the cauterization of vitiligo.
If vitiligo is longstanding and no medical remedy has been effective, then cauterize it slightly deeply, to the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour mixed with rose oil,  plantain leaves, and pigeon's blood or partridge blood; one part of each. Mix everything together, apply it to a cloth, and bind it to the area until it heals.
Chapter fifty.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
If the cancer is incipient and you wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it goes round. Some wise men have mentioned that it should be cauterized intensely in its middle, but I do not see that, because I expect it to ulcerate, and I have witnessed that many times.
Therefore, the correct method is to cauterize around it in a circle, as we said, or with many cauterizations.
Chapter fifty-one.
Concerning the cauterization of the fistula.
If a fistula occurs and is slow to mature, either because of the potency of its cause, or because of the patient's age if he is an elderly person with little blood, or because of time, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the fistula, cauterize around it with many small cauterizations, punctually.
Then leave it, for it will hasten maturation.  If you wish to slow it with cauterization, heat the cautery iron, whose image is this.
And insert it into the middle of the fistula until it penetrates the skin. Let the cauterization be from what is below, so that the flow of pus is easy. Then treat it as is appropriate until it heals.
Chapter fifty-two.
On cauterizing the ulcer.
An ulcer is a corruption that spreads in the member, eating it as fire eats dry wood. If you see an ulcer in a place that can tolerate cauterization with fire, heat many small and large, nail-like cautery irons, according to what suits the place of the ulcer.
Then cauterize it from every side until all the corruption is eradicated and nothing remains at all. Then leave it for three days and apply to the cauterized areas ground sulfur with oil until all the eschar and all the corruption are removed.
Then treat it with ointments that generate flesh. If after three days you see that the flesh grows healthy without corruption, otherwise repeat the cauterization on what remains of the corrupted areas.  And the ulcer may be treated with a sharp medicine.
For it acts in place of fire, more quickly beneficial. And I have mentioned its treatment with the sharp medicine in the section; take it from there when you need it.
Chapter fifty-three.
On cauterizing inverted and non-inverted nails.
This condition frequently occurs in the soles of the feet; it is a rough thing that pains the man, and its cauterization is in two ways: either cauterization with fire or cauterization with sharp water.
As for cauterization with fire, it is that you heat a hollow cautery resembling an eagle's feather, made of iron to the extent that it surrounds the nail from every side, and let it be thin-edged, then you lower it, hot, onto the nail, then you rotate your hand with the cautery around the nail until the cautery reaches the depth of the nail.
And you leave it three days, it will suppurate with pus, then you apply a dressing of wild bread, pounded with salt, and leave the dressing on it overnight, then it will be removed from its roots, then you treat the site of the wound with a flesh-generating ointment until it heals, if the nails are not inverted.
And it frequently occurs on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet; so you should take a tube of copper or iron or of an eagle's feather and lower it onto the nail or wart, then you pour into the tube a small amount of sharp water.
And hold your hand as you rotate it, with a slight twitch of your hand, so that the edge of the tube affects the root of the nail, and the sharp water finds a way to penetrate to the root of the nail, and let the patient endure a little the stinging of the sharp water for a while.
Then you leave it, and the nail will be removed from its roots; thus you do it one by one until you come to all of them in the body, then you treat their locations after they are removed with whatever generates flesh from among the ointments.
Chapter fifty-four.
In cauterizing the scab.
If a scab occurs from cold in the nerve or from a quartan fever or something else, then it should be cauterized with four or five cauterizations on the vertebrae of the back, between each vertebra a cauterization, and on the chest a cauterization. And on his stomach a cauterization with the olive cautery, for the scab is calmed and the cold illness is hastened to ripen.
Chapter fifty-five.
In cauterizing the pustule occurring in the body.
Ugly pustules may erupt in the body, which are from cold, thick, corrupt materials.  At their first appearance, each pustule should be cauterized gently with a pointed stick whose end has been lit with fire, or with a long root of the ziraund, or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And abscesses may be cauterized at their first eruption in this manner; the active cause does not increase, and the illness dissipates and the patient recovers, except that this should be after the patient has been bled.
Chapter fifty-six.
In cauterizing the bleeding that occurs when an artery is cut.
Often, bleeding occurs from an artery that has been severed in a wound that occurs externally, or in the incision of a tumor, or the cauterization of a member, and the like, and its cutting is difficult.
If this happens to someone, quickly place your index finger on the mouth of the artery and press it firmly so that the blood is contained under your finger and nothing comes out of it. Then place several small and large olive-shaped cauteries in the fire and blow on them until they become very hot.
Then take one of them, either small or large, according to the wound and the place where the artery has burst, and apply the cautery to the same vein after quickly removing your finger, and hold the cautery until the bleeding stops.
If, when you lift your finger from the mouth of the artery, the bleeding starts again and the cautery is extinguished, quickly take another cautery from those in the prepared fire, and continue to do this one after another until the bleeding stops.
Be careful not to burn a nerve that is there, lest another affliction befall the patient. And know that if blood flows from an artery, it cannot be cut, especially if it is a large artery, except in one of four ways: either by cautery, as we have said; or by amputation, if it has not already been amputated; for if it is amputated, its ends contract and the bleeding stops; or it may be tightly bound with threads; or medications that stop blood flow may be applied to it, and it may be firmly bandaged with compresses.
But whoever tries to cut it with a ligature, or by tightening with rags, or by applying burning substances, and the like, will not stop it at all except rarely. If this happens to someone and a physician or medicine is not at hand, let him quickly place his index finger on the mouth of the wound itself, as we have described.
and press it very hard until the blood is constricted and flows away from above the wound and onto the artery, and let the finger not be removed from it, constantly applying very cold water until the blood clots and thickens and stops; and meanwhile, look for what is needed of cautery or medicine.
Chapter Two.
Concerning incisions, punctures, bloodletting, wounds, and the like.
Khalaf said: In the first chapter we mentioned every disease for which cauterization with fire and burning medicine is suitable, and its causes, reasons, instruments, and the forms of cauteries, and we made that into sections from head to foot.
And I follow in this chapter the same method, so that the student may easily find what he seeks.
Before I begin with that, you my sons should know that this chapter contains more dangers than the first chapter on cauterization.
And for that reason, caution should be greater in it, because the work in this chapter often involves the loss of blood upon which life depends when opening a vein or making an incision on a tumor or lancing an abscess or treating a wound or removing an arrow or incising a stone, and the like, all of which are accompanied by danger and fear, and in most of them, death occurs.
And I advise you against falling into what is doubtful for you, for there may come to you in this craft various kinds of people with various kinds of illnesses, some of whom are weary of their illness and death is easy for them.
Because of the severity of his illness and the length of his affliction, and the illness has reached a stage indicating death; and some of them will give you their money and enrich you with it in hope of health, and his disease is deadly.  Therefore, you should not assist anyone who comes to you with this condition at all.
