Let your caution be stronger than your desire and eagerness, and do not proceed with anything of that except after certain knowledge that is established with you of what leads to a good outcome. And use in all treatment of your patients, precedence of knowledge and warning as to what leads to safety.
For in that you have an aid to acquiring praise, glory, mention, and thanks. May God inspire you, my sons, with His guidance and not deprive you of correctness and success; indeed, that is in His hand. There is no god but He.
And I have arranged this chapter in sections, as preceded in the chapter on cauterization, from head to foot, to make easier for you what you wish from it, God willing.
The first chapter.
In the treatment of the water which gathers in the heads of children.
This ailment often occurs to infants at birth if the midwife presses the infant's head without gentleness, and it may also occur from a hidden cause which is not known.
And I have not seen this ailment in anyone other than infants, and all those whom I have seen of them have quickly died; therefore I have seen [it is best] to leave treatment of it. And I have seen an infant whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger each day. Until the infant could not sit up by himself because of the size of his head, and the moisture increased until he perished.
And this moisture either gathers between the skin and the bone, or it gathers under the bone on the membrane.  The treatment in this, if the moisture is between the skin and the bone and the swelling is small, is that you should make in the middle of the head a single incision across, and the length of the incision should be about two fingerbreadths, until the moisture flows out.  This is the image of the lancet.
If the moisture is greater and the swelling larger, then make two intersecting incisions in this manner:
And if the moisture is under the bone, and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on every side and the water sinks down if you press it with your hand inwards—and this is not hidden from you—then you should make three incisions in the middle of the head in this manner.
And after the incision, all the moisture comes out, then you bind the incisions with rags and lint, then you bathe it from above with syrup and oil until the fifth day, then you untie the bandage and treat the wound with lint and ointments, and do not neglect to bind the head moderately, and feed the patient with every dry food with little moisture.
Until the member strengthens and heals. Another description of the incision is that you look where the bone of the swelling and the gathering of water appear, for it may be in the back of the head more, or in its front, or on the right, or on the left.
Then you aim with the incision where the swelling and the fullness of the water appeared to you, so you incise it as you can, and be careful not to cut an artery, lest bleeding occur and the patient die from that bleeding along with the evacuation of the moisture.
Chapter Two.
Cutting the two arteries behind the ears, known as the mastoid arteries.
When someone is afflicted with acute catarrh in the eyes or chest, and this has lasted and treatment with medications has not been effective, then the most effective treatment in that is to cut these two arteries.
So, if you want to cut them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth so that the artery appears, then you tighten the patient's neck with the excess of his clothing, then you look where the pulse beats and their positions, the two low-lying positions behind the ears, and they rarely hide except in some people.
Then you mark them with ink, then you cut them with a sharp scalpel, cutting to the bone.  This should be done across the head, or if you wish, you insert the scalpel from under the artery and cut upwards. The length of the cut should be about two fingers’ breadth.
If the artery is cut, the blood will come out in a pulsating flow, leaping forward repeatedly. If the artery is not palpable, you should measure three fingers’ breadth from the ear, then mark it with ink and incise to the bone.  What should be...
...released from the blood is six ounces on average, and perhaps more or less will be released, all this depending on the strength of the patient and the fullness of his arteries.  Then look at the wound; if there remains any of the peritoneum on the bone, cut it away.
Lest a hot swelling appear, then bind the wound with a thread of linen rags, then treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
In ligating the two arteries in the temples.
If a person experiences chronic migraine or sudden attacks caused by sharp humors and heat in the muscles of the temples, or severe chronic headache and the like, and he is treated with various medical treatments without success, then we have tried in these diseases ligating the arteries of the temples or cauterizing them as we have described.
The method of ligating them is that the patient's hair in the temples should be shaved, and the artery apparent in the temple should be targeted, for it will be evident to you from its pulsation, and it rarely hides except in a few people.
Or in severe cold, if it is hidden from you, let the patient tighten his neck with the excess of his garment, then you rub the area with a cloth or compress the area with hot water until the artery appears clearly and evidently. Then take the scalpel, whose image is this. Then you incise the skin gently with it until you reach the artery, then you insert a hook into it and pull it upwards until you bring it out of the skin and free it from the membranes beneath it from every side; if the artery is thin, then twist it with the end of the hook. Then cut off a portion of it to the extent that its ends separate and contract, and do not cause bleeding, for if it is not severed and not cut, the blood will not flow at all; then evacuate from six ounces to three of blood.
If the artery is large, then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a strong doubled thread, and let the thread be either of silk or of lute strings, lest corruption quickly reach it before the wound heals, and bleeding occurs.
Then cut what is between the ligatures; do this at that time or after another time. And if you wish to cauterize it with a knife-cautery to the bone until its ends are severed, this will serve the same purpose or better, as we have said.
Unless the patient has a fever or a hot temperament, for cauterization is something that helps in eliminating humors, and thus it is more certain in its benefit. After removing the arteries, the place should be packed with old cotton and firm bandages applied. After healing, it is treated with dry medicines that grow flesh, and with bandages, until it heals, God willing.
If bleeding from the artery occurs during your work, hasten to cut it, either by cauterization or by filling the place with vitriol and tightening your hand until the blood stops. If you do not have any of that, put your finger there until the blood clots.
And wash the place with very cold water until the sharpness subsides and bind it appropriately.  What is easier and simpler than ligating the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cautery after marking the two places with ink.
And lower it, very hot, until it reaches the bone and the vein is severed in two places so that what is between its ends is separated, for it will not reunite with this cautery at all.  And this is the image of the cautery.
The blades should be sharp, resembling small scalpels, but much less sharp than a scalpel, because if they are as sharp as a scalpel, they cool more quickly and the flesh is not severed quickly. If they have some thickness, the heat of the fire is retained in them.
And the flesh is cut quickly. This method is better than any other, and lighter and easier. Let the distance between the blades be the width of a finger.
Chapter Four.
In the treatment of the constant flow of hot tears to the eyes.
If the tears are constant and their flow is from the veins on the scalp from the outside, and nothing from medical treatment with medication benefits, and you see the patient's face is red. And he feels in his forehead a crawling like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are sunken and moist, their eyelashes are eroded and their eyelids are clinging from the intensity of the tears.  The ancients in their treatment resorted to this procedure, and they call it treatment with the sword. It is that you order the patient to shave the hair from his forehead.
Then you make three parallel incisions in the forehead along the length of the forehead. The length of each incision is about two fingers, one incision parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second a little distance from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third from the other side.
And be careful not to cut the two arteries on the sides, and keep your hand away from the connection of the jaws.  Let the distance between each incision be about three closed fingers, and have with you prepared pieces of sponge or many dry cloths to wipe the blood.
Then you insert the double-edged scalpel, whose image is this. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, and with it you flay all the skin between the two incisions, together with the periosteum on the bone. Then you do this also from the middle incision to the third incision, then you remove this scalpel. And you also insert into the first incision another instrument called a scalpel, sharp on one side and smooth, not sharp, on the other side, and this is its image:
And you place its sharp side upwards towards the flesh attached to the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone, and you push it until it reaches the middle incision, and you cut with it all the vessels which descend from the head to the eyes, without the cut reaching the surface of the skin.
Then you do that in the middle incision to the other incision, and after a moderate amount of blood flows, you squeeze the places of the clotted blood, then you place in each incision a pledget of cotton, and you place on it a dressing moistened with syrup and oil, or vinegar and oil.
Lest a hot inflammation occur, and on the third day you loosen the bandage and use much irrigation with lukewarm water, then you treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and all that you treat wounds with, until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Five.
In the treatment of tears and catarrh to the eyes from the inside of the head.
When someone experiences many severe, sharp, persistent catarrhs, and you see his eyes weak, small, and his vision weakened, and his eyelids ulcerated, and the eyelashes falling out. And there is severe, painful pain in the depth of the head and consecutive sneezing, then know from these symptoms that those materials and catarrhs come from many deep veins, and the best treatment in them is this treatment, which is that you order the patient to shave his forehead, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is severe, painful pain in the depth of the head and consecutive sneezing, then know from these symptoms that those materials and catarrhs come from many deep veins, and the best treatment in them is this treatment, which is that you order the patient to shave his forehead, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead. Or a little higher horizontally, and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple, and the incision should be to the bone, and keep your hand away from the muscles of the temples that move when chewing, until the bone is exposed and all the blood is dried with a sponge.
Then separate the lips of the incision with old cotton or a linen thread, then bandage from above with pledgets, and soak the pledgets with syrup and oil, lest a hot swelling occur, and when you remove them and see that the hot swelling has subsided, then you should scrape the bone until the growth of flesh begins in it.
Then treat it with the drying treatment that grows flesh, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, and preparing an ointment from them, and using it in the growth of flesh in such wounds.
Chapter Six.
In the treatment of what falls into the ear.
Whatever falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic stone or a semi-stone like iron and glass; or a plant seed like chickpeas and kernels and the like; or a flowing thing like water and vinegar and the like.
Or an animal.  If a stone or a kind of stone that does not grow in the ear falls into the ear, then expose the ear to the sun. If you see the stone, then instill some violet oil or ceruse into it, then try to remove it by moving the head or sneezing with a handkerchief. And plug the nostrils when sneezing comes, after placing a ring of cloth or wool around the ear and extending the ear upwards. Often it comes out with this treatment. If it does not come out otherwise, then try to remove it with a gentle probe, whose image is this.
If it comes out with the probe, otherwise try to remove it with a gentle, slightly curved, blind hook. If it does not come out with that, then make a tube of copper and insert the end of the tube into the ear hole smoothly.
And seal around the tube with pitch softened with oil, lest there be a path for the air other than the tube, then draw it with your breath strongly. Often it comes out with that. If it does not come out with what we described, then take a little of the Nabataean mastic or of the prepared mastic with which birds are caught, and place it on the end of the probe.
After wrapping it tightly with cotton, then insert it gently into the ear hole after drying the ear of moisture. If it does not come out by all that we have described, then proceed to incision before a hot inflammation or a convulsion occurs.
The method of incision is that you first bleed the patient in the cephalic vein and remove blood from him according to his strength. Then you seat the patient before you and turn his ear upwards and make a small incision at the base of the ear near its lobe in its lower part.
And the incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone, then remove it with whatever instruments you can. Then sew up the incision quickly and treat it until it heals.
And if the thing that has fallen into the ear is one of the seeds that grow and swell, then try to remove it as we mentioned. If it does not respond to removal, then take a thin, delicate scalpel of this shape.
And try with it to cut that type of seed that has fallen into the ear.  You only do this if you are certain that the seed has become moistened by the vapor of the ear so that it becomes many small pieces, then remove them with a blind probe, or with a soft sponge, or by suction.
As we mentioned, then it is easy to remove them.  As for water entering the ear, the patient should first use sneezing with hellebore, having filled his ear with some old cotton while lying on the ear containing the water. If it comes out with that,
otherwise, take many smooth, flat pebbles the length of the fingers, warm them slightly by fire, and the patient inserts one into the opening of his ear, and he hops on one leg on that side, and strikes the pebble in the ear with another pebble.
He continues to do this with pebble after pebble until all the water comes out. The water may also come out by taking a piece of papyrus or a feather, inserting one end into the ear, and applying the other end to fire until most of it burns, then repeating with another feather.
Do this repeatedly until all the water comes out, or draw it out with the tube as was mentioned previously for the pebble. As for removing an animal that has entered, see if it is small in size like a flea, etc. then treat it as was mentioned in the section, and as for...
if its body is large enough to be felt, then try to remove it with forceps and hooks, and this is easier than anything that becomes lodged in the ear. As for removing worms that develop in the ear, if you treat it with what we mentioned in the section on fumigations and your treatment is not effective,
you should look into the ear in the sun. If you see anything of the worms, remove them with forceps or fine hooks. If you do not see anything, take a tube of this shape.
narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, and insert the thin end into the ear as far as the patient can tolerate, then suck strongly. Do this repeatedly until all the worms come out. If they do not respond to being pulled out, seal around the tube with wax as I mentioned to you with the pebble.
If they do not come out with what we have mentioned, use the fumigations which I mentioned and which the ancients have used to kill worms. You will find this in the section on fumigations.  Pouring oils and medicines into the ear is done with this instrument, and this is its image:
As you see, make it from silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, wide at the top. And if you wish, let the nozzle inside the tube be of tightly fitting copper.  And if you wish, take a reed and tightly wrap cotton around its end.
Then pour the oil or juice or whatever medicine you want into the tube while it is in the ear, then insert the reed with the cotton from above and squeeze it gently with your hand until the oil is forced into the cavity of the ear and it is felt.
The patient feels it going in. And let what you pour into the ear be warmed a little in the fire, and beware that what is dripped into it is neither too cold nor too hot, for the ear cannot tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
On the treatment of obstruction occurring in the ear.
Some children may be born with their ear openings not perforated.
Obstruction in the ear canal may also occur in some people due to a wound or newly grown flesh. This obstruction may be deep within the ear opening, invisible to the eye.
It may also appear slightly outside the opening and be visible. That which occurs deep within the ear and is invisible is, in most cases, difficult to cure. That which is visible should have the patient's ear exposed to the sun. And look into it; if you see the obstruction is visible, open it with a delicate scalpel, which is of this form:
Its tip should be slightly wide, its edges somewhat limited, and the rest of the scalpel smooth on both sides so as not to injure the ear. If the obstruction is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a delicate hook and cut it gently but firmly until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is deep within the ear, take a smooth, slender probe, warm it slightly in the fire, and insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the obstruction and the patient experiences hearing difficulty, remove it gently.
Be careful not to injure the eardrum. Then insert into the ear a wick, to fit the size of the opening, smeared with Egyptian ointment. Do this for several days until the healing of the wound is ensured. Or take a wick, moisten it with water, and sprinkle powdered vitriol on it.
Use this. If you see that the ear is swollen with a hot inflammation, it is necessary to remove the wick and replace it with another wick smeared with a plaster made with rose oil, until the hot inflammation subsides, then treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear. Use the other treatments for stopping bleeding mentioned in the chapter on cauterizations.
Chapter Eight.
On the treatment of warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts which appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry.  It is necessary to grasp them with forceps or a hook and cut them from their roots with a scalpel, then apply powdered verdigris to the area.
If it bleeds, cauterize it with a mild lentil-shaped cautery. Cauterization is preferable after cutting.  It often recurs if any of its roots remain; if cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and it does not return.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of the cold affecting the eyelids.
Something resembling a cold in its intensity and hardness often appears in the eyelids.
Therefore it is called a cold, which is a gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids. The procedure is as follows: If the cold is prominent on the surface of the eyelid and moves in every direction, then it is easy to treat; make a transverse incision.
Then gently peel it from all sides until it is removed, then hang it with a hook and cut it off. If you cannot cut it off until it penetrates the eyelid, this will not harm the patient. If the incision is large, join it with sutures and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, there is no harm, for the ointment will heal and join it. If the cold is inclined towards the inside of the eyelid, towards the inner surface, then turn the eyelid over and hang the cold with a hook without needing to make an incision, and cut it off from all sides.
If it penetrates the eyelid with the cut, that does no harm. Then wash the eye after removing the cold with salt water and treat the area with what joins it until the patient heals.
Chapter Ten.
In the treatment of the stye which appears in the eyelid.
A stye is a lump that forms in the layers of the upper eyelid, and this mostly occurs in children. It weighs down their eyes, and they experience colds in the mornings, and are unable to look at the sunlight. Because the tears rush to them.
Therefore, you see them always leaning on their faces, and on them they sleep, and their eyelids under their eyebrows are moist, with swelling upon them, and the stye is clearly visible. When you press the place with your fingers, you feel the stye between them. The procedure is that the patient should place his head in your lap.
Then you take a linen cloth and prepare a wick from it, and you make a circle from that wick to the extent that it surrounds the stye from every side, then you place it on it and press with your fingers from every side to collect the stye in the middle of the circle.
Then you make an incision in the middle of that moisture with a lancet, a transverse incision, and the incision should not be larger than the incision that is made in bloodletting. As for the depth, you should incise all the skin until you reach the stye.
Then, in most cases, the stye protrudes from the incision in the form of a white piece of fat. You pull it out with a cloth that you have wrapped between your fingers, while you turn your hand to the right and left until it heals. And beware of increasing the incision, lest the lancet reach the eye and harm it.
If the stye does not appear to you in the first incision, then you should increase the incision a little gently until the stye appears, then you pull it out as we said, then you dip a cloth in vinegar and water and place it on the place and bind it with a bandage. Some people crush salt.
And they place it in the hollow of the incision to dissolve what remains of that moisture, then treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs in the place, then treat it with soothing compresses until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the types of styes of the eye.
If there grow on the eyelid extra membranes, other than the natural course, under the natural membranes and eyelashes, then they harm the eye and cause various diseases, such as constant tearing, relaxation of the eyelids, whiteness, and thickening, until it becomes a cause of blindness in the eye.
And the stye of the eye is of four types: either by cauterization with fire, or by a caustic medicine as was previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, or the stye may be by cutting and suturing, or by a reed, as I shall mention.
It is necessary that you place the head of the patient in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand; if it turns over, otherwise, insert a needle with a thread from below the eyelid and pass the needle with the thread from above, and that should be near the hair itself, and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid.
And turn it over with a probe, then incise in the inner part of the eyelid without the superfluous hair with a sharp scalpel from the larger canthus to the smaller canthus, then remove the thread and place under the eyelid a small pledget of cotton or cloth, then mark on the eyelid with ink.
Like the shape of an ivy leaf, except that the shape should be according to how much you want to raise the eyelid, because people differ in this; some need you to cut from the eyelid an amount suitable to the degree to which the eyelid has relaxed.
And some need less cutting; all that is according to the relaxation of the eyelid. Then incise with the scalpel on the two lines which you marked, and begin from the larger canthus to the smaller canthus, and let each incision be near the natural hair, about the thickness of a probe.
Then insert the hook into one corner of the skin, then flay it all, then gather with stitching the lips with a needle and thin woolen thread, and wipe away the blood, and stick what remains of the threads to the eyebrows with some adhesive substance.
If you wish to do that, otherwise it doesn't matter. Then leave the stitching and threads for about three or four days, then treat it. And if you wish, leave the wound without stitching and treat it with what dries and constricts, for the eyelid rises when the wound closes and unites, and stitching is better.
This method of blepharoplasty was mentioned by the ancients, except that it is burdensome for the patient; it is a good operation and there is no danger in it. Another method of blepharoplasty is also to mark on the eyelid a shape like the shape of an ivy leaf, as we described. Then raise the eyelid with three hooks, either separate or grouped in this manner:
Then cut off the excess eyelid with small scissors in this manner, cutting evenly.
If you cannot hold the hooks and they do not straighten for you, then take a needle with thread and insert it into the middle of the shape, and insert another thread near the larger canthus and a third thread near the smaller canthus, and gather the threads between your fingers evenly, then lift it with your hand with a moderate lift.
Lift the marked skin completely as we described, then join the edges of the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals. If a hot inflammation occurs when you cut or incise, then soothe that inflammation with qirūṭī and similar soothing ointments.
The lower eyelid may also have its margins inverted; in this case you should use what we have mentioned of cutting, suturing and cauterization with fire or with a caustic medicine; cauterization with fire or with a caustic medicine is more effective and gentler on the patient than cutting and suturing, and do cutting and suturing in both.
Cauterization with a reed is done in this way: you invert the eyelid and make an incision on the inside as described, then make two thin reeds or pieces of wood, their length the length of the eyelid and their width less than the width of a scalpel.
And you have carved notches at their ends on both sides where the threads are fastened, then you gently gather them over what remains of the eyelid and tightly pull the two reeds from both sides and leave it for some days; the stretched skin dies, blackens and decays.
Until it falls off by itself; if it is slow, cut it with scissors, then treat it until it heals.  When it heals, the eyelid rises and the margins of the eye do not invert. This is an image of the two reeds, make them in this shape and this size in length and width exactly:
Chapter Twelve.
On raising ingrown eyelashes from the eye with a needle.
If there is one or two hairs, the procedure is to take a thin needle and thread it with a smooth, thin silk thread, then gather its ends and tie a very gentle knot, and let the length of the thread be about a span.
Then attach another thin thread to the needle, shorter than the first, and tie its ends if you wish. Then have the patient place his head in your lap, and let it be near the sun so that the work may be clear, for it is delicate work. Then insert the needle with the thread into the root of the ingrown hair.
And quickly pass it through the eyelid until you bring it out above the natural hair. Then pull the thread upward with the needle and draw into the needle that hair, whether it be one, two, or three, but no more.
Then pull it with your hand until the hair comes out with the needle in the eyelid along with the natural hair.  If you pull the needle and the hair does not come out with it, pull the needle downwards with the thread you attached to it until the needle comes out of the upper opening.
Then return the hair into it and pull them; do this until it rises into the upper opening and you see it clearly. Then remove the needle and the thread and take them out, and bind the eyelid and leave it bound for two or three days until the hair settles in its place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, either add to it a long hair from the natural hair so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer after a while; then raise it.
Chapter Thirteen.
In the treatment of the sty that occurs in the upper eyelid.
That which this sty is exposed to is called a harelip, and this sty is either natural or accidental. The accidental one is from the healing of a wound or a cut or cauterization and the like.
And the method of treating it is to incise that adhesion and separate its lips, and place a linen wick between them and bind it until it heals.  And it is not appropriate to use in its treatment things that dry and constrict.
For if you do that, the sty will return worse than it was. Rather, use in it things that relax, such as fenugreek and lint with water in which althaea and flaxseed have been boiled, and a diachylon ointment melted with some fats, and smear it with the wick and treat it with that.
And the key to its treatment is to strive by every means that it not adhere in the way it was initially. And this treatment that we have mentioned is only some repair of the sty, not that the appearance of the place returns to exactly as it was at all.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the treatment of the sty which occurs in the lower eyelid.
This sty which occurs in the lower eyelid is that which is truly called a sty, and it is natural and it is accidental.  The accidental one is from a wound or incision or cauterization and the like.
And the method of working in it is that you take a needle with doubled thread and pierce it into the flesh and pass it from the left canthus to the right canthus until the thread is at the two ends of the flesh, then you stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a wide scalpel.
Then if the shape of the eyelid returns to what it should be, otherwise you take a probe and place it on the site of the incision and turn the eyelid with it and make two incisions in the inner side of the eyelid, and the ends of the two incisions are from the corners of the cut which you cut until they meet, so that an angle is formed from them. Until, when they meet, its shape becomes similar to this shape:
Which is the Greek letter lambda, then you remove that flesh to the extent that its sharp side is lower than what is below the eyelid, then you gather the separated parts with two stitches, sewing it with a woolen thread, then you treat it with what we mentioned of emollient medicines and bandages until it heals.
And if the fistula appeared from an incision or suture or cauterization, it is necessary to make a simple incision without the hair of the labia as well, as mentioned before, then separate the lips with a bandage as we mentioned.
In short, in the treatment of fistula, whether from above or below, you should act according to what is possible for you in terms of the appearance of the fistula, for it may differ greatly in form, and the skillful surgeon devises a means in whatever way he can.
Until the shape returns to its natural form or approximates it, and the disease itself indicates the appropriate treatment and instrument in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may occur to many people, such that the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye, preventing it from easy movement and hindering its natural functions.  It is necessary to insert the point of a probe under the eyelid.
And lift it upwards or stretch it with a hook, then cut the adhesion with a delicate scalpel, not sharply like cutting scalpels, but rather slightly blunt, lest the patient be disturbed during the procedure and the scalpel slips, injuring the eye with its sharpness.  Your cutting should be as if you are peeling off that adhesion.
Wait until the eyelid returns to its natural state and the adhesion is completely healed, then pour salt water into the eye to wash it, or instill a solution of vitriol in water, then separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen wick.
And place on top of the eye wool moistened with egg white. After the third day, use collyria with acacia until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the excision of pterygium and the fleshy excrescences of the conjunctiva.
Pterygium is of two kinds: either nervous, resembling a thin, hard membrane; or non-nervous, resembling a thick, white moisture which, if touched with an iron instrument or attempted to be grasped with a hook, will tear.
And a hook cannot be fixed in it. Both kinds begin from the greater canthus until they gradually spread until they cover the pupil and obstruct vision and the movement of the eye. The method of excising them is that the patient should place his head in your lap.
Then he opens his eyes, and you raise the eyelid with your hand, then you grasp the pterygium with a slightly curved hook and stretch it upwards, then you take a needle and thread it with a horsehair or oxhair and strong thread, and you bend the point of the needle slightly and pierce it into the middle of the pterygium.
And you pass the needle through and tie the pterygium with the thread and stretch it upwards and dissect with the hair the side of the pterygium next to the pupil, as if you were dissecting it with the hair to its end, then you cut the remainder at the root of the canthus with a sharp scalpel or small scissors, and you leave the natural flesh of the canthus.
So that constant lacrimation does not result from its excision.  The pterygium may be stretched with the hook alone or with the thread as we said, then the smooth scalpel dissects the end, the picture of which is this:
And be careful that the scalpel does not touch the cornea, lest it cause a perforation and injure the pupil. Then after the excision, put something of powdered salt or zinc sulfate in the eye and leave it until the next day, then treat it as is appropriate until it heals.
If the pterygium is not vascular and you cannot insert a needle into it or fix a hook to it, this type of pterygium is often treated with medication and heals as described in the classification.  If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a fine, smooth scalpel with a weak edge, like this image: And dissect the pterygium from above gently with it. If you see that it is loosening and breaking up and the dissection is affecting it, then instill in the eye from your instrument zinc sulfate or red sulfate or powdered salt, and bandage the eye until the next day.
Then resume working on it until it is all gone, unless a hot inflammation hinders you in the eye; then leave it and treat the hot inflammation until it heals, then resume working on it by dissection until it heals. As for the fleshy excrescence of the caruncle…
If the excrescence harms the eye excessively, then hook that excrescence with a hook and cut some of it off, but do not overdo the cutting lest it cause tearing, then instill in the caruncle red sulfate or zinc sulfate until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cutting the pterygium and what grows of excess flesh in the eye.
In the eyes of some people, red flesh may grow, accumulating until it covers the pupil or comes close to it or overflows onto the eyelids; and perhaps the eyelids are turned outward, and it resembles a wild rose.
The procedure is that the patient places his head in your lap, then opens his eyes, and you grasp all that red flesh with hooks suitable for that.
Or you grasp it with a rasp or forceps, then you cut the flesh, piece by piece, until you remove all of it by cutting, and you protect the eye so as not to harm it during the procedure.  Your cutting may be with the scalpel that we described in cutting the pterygium, or with small scissors. Like those with which one plucks hairs, as its image comes in the chapter following this chapter.
When your cutting is completed and all the growth is gone, fill the eye with crushed salt or drop into it some red vitriol or a similar caustic medicine, and apply to the eye externally a cotton pledget with egg white to prevent hot inflammation.
If some of the growth remains and the bleeding overcomes you and you fear hot inflammation, leave the eye and treat it with what calms the inflammation, then return to the procedure until it heals.  Do likewise with the excess flesh that appears in the eye of this type.
Except that it is necessary for you to avoid treating any eye weak by nature or by circumstance with the iron. And do not put your hand to any of these procedures until you see that the member is able to withstand that procedure.
Chapter Eighteen.
On removing pterygia from the eye.
Pterygia are red vessels which grow over the eye, preventing its function and weakening the eye over time.  First you should look; if the eye in which the pterygia is found is strong and has no other disease besides the pterygia,
then removal is performed as follows: you should tell the patient to place his head in your lap, then you should hook those vessels with one or two hooks, according to your skill.  The hooks should be delicately curved in this manner:
Or they may be two hooks joined together in one piece, in this manner:
Then, with a delicate pair of scissors, gently remove those vessels, wiping away the blood from time to time, until you see that the eye is free of those vessels and the bleeding has stopped. Be careful not to injure the eye with the tips of the scissors, and let your work be done in the middle of the day, facing the sun.
And be very careful in your work, so that you do not cut anything other than those vessels.  When you have finished, instill green or red vitriol into the eye, so that its sharpness may consume what remains of the pterygia. If you cannot remove it all at that time, then bandage the eye with something that soothes the pain of the eye.
And leave it for several days until the pain subsides and the inflammation is safe, then repeat the procedure in the same way until it is healed. This is the picture of the appropriate scissors.
And leave it [and let the patient's head be in the lap of the pupil, and another wipes away the tears and checks the flow of tears in the front and back of the eye, and you] the principle in this operation is that you open the eye with fixed or triangular openers that the pupil holds.
Sharp, and let the patient's head be in the lap of the pupil, and another wipes away the tears and checks the flow of tears in the front and back of the eye, and you have the choice of beginning to remove it from the top of the eye or from the bottom. It is best to remove two strands on the upper side, one at the end of the cornea and the other at the beginning.
And grasp them between the middle finger and the index finger, then hook them from below opposite them, then grasp them between the little finger and the ring finger, and lift your hand gently until you see all of the pterygium raised. Then cut it from the smaller side, if you wish, with scissors, or if you wish, with a delicate scalpel.
And insert the thread onto a needle and lift all the pterygium until it is raised on the conjunctiva, and do not cut the thread, for the tears and blood will make it difficult for you.
Then cut with scissors what extends beyond the cornea from above and below so that nothing remains attached except to the cornea.  If you can lift it from the cornea by pulling the thread, that is best, and it may be possible in some people.
And when you have cut it, let its place be cauterized for several days to soothe its pain and prevent hot swelling, then resume the treatment.
Chapter Nineteen.
On returning the nose to its place.
Physicians call a nasal polyp a fistula. If you treat it with cautery or with a sharp, burning medication as previously described, and it does not heal, then there is no remedy except to incise the swelling when it is ripe and extract all the moisture or pus within it.
Until the bone is exposed.  If the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then scrape it with an instrument of this shape:
The rasp, which is made of Indian iron, is called the head. Its head is round like a button, finely engraved with the pattern of a file or a rasp. You place it on the corrupted part of the bone, then rotate it between your fingers, applying slight pressure with your hand until you know that the corruption has been scraped away.
Do this repeatedly, then dress the wound with drying, astringent medications. If the wound closes and flesh grows in it, and the discharge stops, and forty days pass without recurrence, suppuration, or any incident,
know that it has healed. Otherwise, there is no remedy except to return the fistula to the nasal cavity in this manner:  again expose the bone with an iron instrument or a sharp medication. When the bone is exposed, take a probe of this shape:
The tip of the instrument is triangular, and its shaft is conical, tapering to the tip, as you see. Then place it on the bone itself, near the ethmoid and a little further from the eye. Then rotate your hand with the probe until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
He will feel air escaping from it if he holds his hand over his nose. Then dress the wound with the drying astringent medications as I have taught you. If flesh grows and the place hardens, then the matter which used to flow outwards will return to the nose; this will be easier for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On returning the protrusion of the eye.
If the eye protrudes in its entirety, and no damage or impairment occurs in the vision, then you should ease the patient, and then bleed him in the cephalic vein.  Then apply a cupping glass to the nape of the neck without restriction, and gently suck.
Then apply a poultice made from acacia, aloes, frankincense, and bdellium. Then bind the eye firmly over the poultice with many pledgets. The method of making the pledgets is to take many soft lint, and fold each piece into four layers, making many of them.
Then place them one over the other, as much as the eye needs. Then bind it firmly with a strong cloth. Repeat the cupping with gentle suction, then remove the cupping glass and leave the eye bandaged for a day and a night.
If the bandage loosens during that time, it should be tightened gently. Then remove the bandage after a day and a night, as we said. If you see that the eye has returned, otherwise, repeat the poultice, pledgets, binding, and cupping until it returns.
Chapter twenty-one.
On cutting the uvula.
If a hernia occurs in the uvular layer, then it protrudes and emerges outside the eyelids like a grape, and the person's appearance is disfigured because of it, and you wish to cut it, then the procedure is as I describe.
It is that you insert a needle into the base of the uvula from below upwards, then insert another needle with a doubled thread from the side of the palate, and pass it through, and leave the first needle as it is.
Then you cut the place where the doubled thread bends and tie some of the uvula around the needle with it and pull it firmly, then you remove the needle and place on the eye wool moistened with egg white and leave it until the threads fall off with the uvula, then you treat the eye with what strengthens it.
Until the wound heals.  You may pull the uvula from each side with a circle of cloth until the eye is further drawn in firmly, then incise with a fine scalpel reaching to the depth of the eye so that the white moisture flows, and the eye descends and withers in place, then you pull it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-two.
In the treatment of the uvula.
This ailment, which is called kamanah, is a moisture that collects in the eye resembling descending water, but it is not. The method of treatment is that you seat the patient on a chair upright, then take his head in your hands from both sides.
And move it until you see the moisture descend to the bottom with your eye, then it stabilizes and does not move, and the light departs and the patient sees things as he used to see them. And if it does not descend to the bottom, we know that it is water.  If the descent of the moisture does not occur as mentioned,
then seat the patient between your hands, then take a thin scalpel and incise it slightly above the cornea at the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the ciliary region until the substance comes out.
Then, once it has come out, instill in the eye warm water in which honey has been mixed, or water in which fenugreek and honey have been boiled, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have already mentioned the types of water in the classification and which of them is suitable for cupping, in a detailed and explained statement, so take it from there correctly. Then you should seat the patient between your hands, sitting cross-legged facing the light near the sun.
And you should bind his healthy eye and tie it tightly, then lift the eyelid of his eye with your left hand if the eye with the water is the left eye, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then you place the tip of the cupping instrument near the cornea, with the thickness of a probe, in the same white of the eye on the side of the smaller canthus, then you push the cupping instrument forcefully while rotating it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel with the cupping instrument that it has reached something empty.
And it should be that the depth to which the cupping instrument goes is the distance from the pupil to the end of the blackness which is the cornea, for the copper will be seen by the examiner himself directly due to the clarity of the corneal membrane, then you move the cupping instrument upwards to the place where the water is.
Then press it downwards again and again, and if the water descends immediately, then the patient will see what his sight has been opened to immediately with the cupping instrument in his eye, then it will rest a little. If the water rises, then lower it again without removing the cupping instrument.
If it settles and does not rise, then remove the cupping instrument gently while twisting it with your hand little by little, then dissolve in the water a little clean Andarani salt and wash the eye with it from the inside, then apply to the outside of the whole eye a compress or wet wool.
with rose oil and egg white and you bandage it along with the healthy eye. As for us and the people of our time, we place crushed cumin with egg white on it. If the cupping instrument does not succeed in entering the eye due to its hardness, because some people have very hard eyes, Then you should take the scalpel called the *barīd*, whose image is this:
Then pierce only the adherent part with it and do not overdo the piercing, but it is only to make a delicate place for the cupping instrument, then insert the cupping instrument as we have mentioned. When your work is complete, prepare for the patient a firm lying-down place where he sleeps on his back in a dark room.
And he abstains from all movements and from coughing, and you make his food that which softens his nature, and he does not move his head to the right or left at all. The bandage remains in place until the third day, then you untie it in that dark room and test his sight.
Show him things, then replace the bandage until the seventh day.  It is not appropriate to do this during treatment or after cupping at your hour, but you should avoid that before the water ascends quickly with intense vision.
If a hot swelling appears, then you should untie the eye before the seventh day and treat that with what calms the swelling, until when it is calmed, then at that time you release his sight and place a veil on his face, training his sight from under it for days while he is in that dark room, then...
He gradually leaves the house and manages his affairs. And know that in cupping, the learner cannot dispense with observation many times, then he proceeds to the work. And it has reached me from some Iraqis that he mentioned that he makes in Iraq a cupping instrument that is drawn out, with which he sucks out the water.
And I have not seen anyone in our country make that, nor read it in any book of the ancients. It may be that this is recent. And this is an image of types of cupping instruments for those who are ignorant of them:
It is made especially of copper, and its end is of this very thinness, triangular-ended and sharp.
Chapter twenty-four.
In the treatment of fleshy growths in the nose.
Various fleshy growths may grow in the nose, some resembling a centipede, some being cancerous flesh, hardened and dark in color, and some being soft flesh, not dark in color.
If any of these fleshy growths are soft, not malignant nor cancerous, then you should seat the patient before you facing the sun, open his nostril, and cast the snare into that flesh, then pull it outwards, and then cut what it has grasped with a fine, sharp scalpel from one side.
Until you know that all the flesh has gone. If any remains that you could not cut, then remove it with one of the delicate instruments gently, so that nothing remains. If the bleeding overcomes you or a hot inflammation appears,
then treat it appropriately. If it is one of the malignant tumors, then act quickly to cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is gone, then place in the nose after cutting, vinegar and water or a drink. If the nose opens and moisture passes from it to the throat,
know that he is healed. If the moisture does not pass as it should, then know that inside there is flesh growing on the top of the porous bones that the instrument did not reach with the cutting. Then it is necessary that you take a linen thread of some thickness.
and tie many knots in it, and make between each knot the space of a finger or less.  Let the patient skillfully insert one end of the thread into his nose with a probe or whatever is possible for him, after making it like a button, and draw his breath until it reaches the pharynx.
and it comes out at his throat.  Children often do this in school, and it is easy for those who want to do it. Then gather the ends of the thread, the one end that came out at the mouth and the other that remained in the nose.
Then use the knots in the thread to saw the flesh, doing so until you know that the flesh has been cut by the knots of the thread. Then remove the thread and put in the nose, after wiping away the blood, a wick that has been soaked in Egyptian ointment. Do this for three days or more.
until the ointment eats away all that remains of the flesh. Then, as a last thing, put a lead tube in the nose for days until it heals. If it needs a drying treatment, use that.
And this is the image of the syringe with which ointments and medicines are dropped into the nose:
It is made of silver or copper, resembling a small, open oil lamp, and its tube as well.
And if you wish, make the tube closed like a reed, and the nozzle of the syringe flat.
It has a handle at its end, as you see, to hold it when you heat the ointment or whatever you want of juices or flowing things in it.
Chapter twenty-five.
On warts growing on the tip of the nose.
Often a wart grows on the tip of the nose, and it increases and grows larger with the days until its appearance becomes ugly.
Therefore, you should cut it off at its first appearance and excise all of it, then apply to the place either cautery or a burning medicine which takes the place of cautery. If you miss cutting it until it becomes large, then look: if it is hardened, solid, dark-colored, and insensitive.
Then do not use iron on it, for it is a cancerous tumor, and I have often seen those who cut this tumor suffer a great calamity from it. But if the tumor is soft to the touch, not dark-colored, and you see that excision is possible in its entirety. Then let the patient vomit and cut it without hesitation or delay, and treat the place with what dries and constricts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On suturing the nose, lip, and ear. If their connection is separated by a wound or the like, know that when a separation of connection occurs in one of these cartilages, rarely does treatment work except in some people.
Then, if any of this happens to someone, see if the wound is fresh with its blood; gather the edges of the wound with sutures, then treat it until it heals. And if the separation of the connection has separated its edges and each part is whole, then it is necessary to scrape each part to the thickness of its outer skin until it bleeds.
Then gather the edges with sutures and tighten them, and sprinkle on them mastic and frankincense, powdered, and place on top a plaster of palm-tree ointment or another of the agglutinative ointments, and leave it tied for two or three days, then untie it.
And change the medication and leave it until the threads separate by themselves, then treat it with ointment until it heals.  The method of suturing is to gather the separated connection, either with needles as we described in suturing the abdomen, or with thread as we showed you there.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On removing the knots which appear in the lips.
Many people develop small, hard tumors inside their lips; some resemble a barleycorn, others are smaller or larger. You should turn back the lip and incise each nodule and hang it with a hook.
And cut it from each side, then pack the area after cutting with powdered alum until the bleeding stops, then rinse with vinegar and salt and treat the area with something astringent until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On cutting away excess flesh in the gums.
Often, excess flesh grows on the gums, which the ancients called epulis. You should hook it with a hook or grasp it with forceps and cut it at its base, and let the serum or blood flow, then apply powdered alum to the area.
Or one of the astringent drying powders. If that flesh returns after treatment—for it often does return—cut it again and cauterize it, for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On extracting teeth with iron.
Rough, unsightly crusts may gather on the surfaces of the teeth, inside and out, and between the gums.  These may blacken, yellow, or green, so that the decay reaches the gums and disfigures the teeth.
The patient should sit before you with his head in your lap.  Clean the tooth or teeth where you see the crusts and the gritty substance until nothing remains.  Do likewise with the black, green, and yellow discolorations and anything else, until you have cleaned it.
If the crusts are removed at the first cleaning, well and good. Otherwise, repeat the cleaning on another day, and then again, and again until you achieve your goal. Know that a tooth may require many different shapes of instruments, according to what is suitable for the task.
For the instrument used to clean the inside of a tooth is not the same as that used for the outside, nor that used between the teeth, which is of yet another form.  These are several forms of instruments, all of which you should have ready:
Chapter Thirty.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
You should treat a toothache by every means possible and delay extraction, for there is no replacement for a tooth once it is extracted, because it is a noble substance, unless extraction is absolutely necessary.
If the patient is determined to have it extracted, you should ascertain that the pain is in the tooth itself. For often the patient is deceived by the pain and thinks it is in a sound tooth, and extracts it, then the pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is extracted.  We have seen this done repeatedly by barbers.
If you ascertain that the pain is in that tooth itself, then you should incise around the tooth with a strong scalpel, so as to loosen the gum on all sides, then move it slightly with your fingers or with delicate forceps, little by little, until you loosen it.
Then firmly grasp it with the large forceps, and hold the patient's head between your knees, having steadied him so that he does not move; then pull the tooth straight out, so as not to break it. If it does not come out otherwise, take one of those instruments and gently insert it under the tooth from all sides, and proceed to move it as before.
And if the tooth is perforated or decayed, you should fill the hole with a rag and pack it tightly with the end of a thin probe, lest it crumble when you pull on it with the forceps; and you should thoroughly incise around the gum on every side.
And take care lest you break it, so that part of it remains, and the patient suffers a worse affliction than his initial pain. And beware of doing what the ignorant barbers do, in their haste and boldness in extracting it without using what we have described.
For they often inflict great harm on people, the least of which is that the tooth breaks and all or part of its roots remain. Or else it may extract some of the bone of the jaw, as we have witnessed many times.  Then, after its extraction, let him rinse his mouth with a drink or vinegar and salt. If bleeding occurs from the place—and this often happens—then crush some vitriol and pack the place with it.
Otherwise, cauterize it if the vitriol does not help. The image of the delicate forceps with which you first move the tooth: they should be long-pronged, short-handled, and thick, lest they bend when you grasp the tooth with them. And this is the image of the large forceps:
They should be thick-handled, as you see, so that when you grasp them they do not yield nor bend; short-pronged. And let them be of Indian iron or of well-tempered steel, with sharpened points, and at their tips, teeth that interlock, so that they grasp firmly and tightly.
And the points may be made like a file, so that they also hold strongly.
Chapter Thirty-one.
On extracting the roots of molars and removing fractured jawbones.
If, when extracting a molar, a root remains broken off, you should apply a cotton pledget with fat to the area for a day or two, until the area relaxes, then introduce the forceps or hooks. whose points resemble the beak of the bird called the “bulrāja,” and this is an image of the hooks:
They should be made like a file on the inside or like a rasp. If you are not able to extract it with these hooks, you should drill into the root and expose all the flesh with the scalpel, then introduce the instrument resembling a small lever, which is depicted thus:
short at the end, somewhat thick, and not pointed, lest it break. If the root is extracted by this, well and good; otherwise, use these other instruments, which are depicted thus: the first has a triangular point, somewhat thick:
Another fine triangular image:
And we may also use this instrument with two branches, which is also depicted thus:
And other instruments and tools previously mentioned in the extraction of teeth.  We may also use this instrument which resembles a large hook, which is depicted thus:
Triangular, with a curved point, somewhat thick, lest it break; and it should not be pointed. Know that the instruments for teeth are numerous, and so are all the other instruments; they are almost innumerable.  A skillful and expert craftsman may invent for himself instruments according to what the work and the diseases themselves indicate.
For there are diseases for which the ancients did not mention instruments, due to the variety of their types. If a bone of the jaw or one of the bones of the mouth is broken or decayed, then search for it in its place with one of these instruments and hooks which were mentioned in the extraction of roots.
And we use a forceps, its image is this:
It should be somewhat thick to hold the bone so that it does not slip until the bone is removed. And we treat the area with appropriate medicines. If the bone has a little decay, cleanse it of its decay and blackness until it is clean, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
On the extraction of molars growing on top of others.
If molars grow other than in their natural course, it is unsightly, especially if this happens in women and slaves.  It should be examined; if the molar has grown behind another tooth,
and its extraction or pushing back is impossible, then extract it. And if it is attached to another tooth, cut it with this instrument, whose image is this:
It resembles a small beak and should be made of sharp Indian iron, very sharp at the tip. Your cutting should be done over many days because of the tooth's hardness, so as not to shake other teeth. But if it is firmly grown, so you can file it down, then file it down with a file from India, whose image is this.
It should be entirely of Indian [steel], and its point should be very finely engraved, like the file with which needles are made. File the tooth down little by little over many days gently, so as not to shake the tooth and cause it to fall out. Then smooth it down further and remove it with some forceps.
And if a tooth is broken, and it hurts the tongue when speaking, then it should also be filed down until the roughness of that fracture is gone, and it becomes even and smooth, and does not hurt the tongue or impair speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three
On binding loose teeth with silver or gold threads.
If the front teeth become loosened and move due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on anything edible for fear that they will fall out, and you have treated them with astringent medications but the treatment has not worked.
The solution is to tie them with a gold or silver thread; gold is better because silver tarnishes and decays after a few days, while gold remains unchanged forever, and this does not happen to it. The thread should be of moderate fineness and thickness, according to what fits between the teeth.
The method of binding is to take the thread and insert its loop between the healthy teeth, then weave the ends of the thread between the loose teeth, whether one or more, until you reach the healthy tooth on the other side by weaving.
Then weave back to the side where you began, and pull gently and skillfully so that they do not move at all.  Tighten the thread at the roots of the teeth so that it does not come loose, then cut the excess ends of the thread with scissors, gather them, twist them with the pliers, and hide them between the healthy tooth and the loose tooth.
So that it does not injure the tongue. Then leave it tied as it is for as long as it remains. If it becomes loose or breaks, tie it again with another thread.  In this way, he will enjoy it for his whole life. This is an image of the teeth and how the binding is done to two healthy teeth and two loose teeth, as you see:
A single tooth or two may be returned to their place after falling out, and bound as we have described, and they will remain. This is done by a skilled craftsman. He may carve a bone from cattle bone and shape it like a tooth, and place it in the place from which the tooth has gone. And it is tightened as we have said, and it remains, and he enjoys it.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On cutting the ligament which appears under the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament which appears under the tongue may be either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from a wound which has healed.
And the procedure is that you open the mouth of the patient and place his head in your lap and raise his tongue, then cut that nervous ligament transversely until the tongue is released from its restraint. If there is some stiffness and complexity, and this is from the healing of a wound, then insert a hook into it and split it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knot is loosened.
And beware that the incision is not deep into the flesh, lest you cut an artery there and bleeding results. Then, after the cutting, let him gargle with rose water or with vinegar and cold water, then place under the tongue a pledget of linen which the patient holds in place each night.
Lest it unite again.  If bleeding occurs, place powdered alum on the place. If the bleeding overcomes you, cauterize the place with a lentil-shaped cautery suitable for this. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On removing a frog generated under the tongue.
A tumor resembling a small frog may occur under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function, and it may even grow large enough to fill the mouth. The procedure is to open the patient's mouth towards the sun and look at the tumor.
If you see it dark-colored or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, do not interfere with it, for it is a cancer. But if it is whitish and moist, insert a hook into it and dissect it with a fine scalpel, freeing it from all sides.
If bleeding overwhelms you during the procedure, apply powdered vitriol until the bleeding stops, then resume your work until you remove it completely. Then, let the patient rinse the mouth with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with other appropriate remedies until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
In the treatment of tonsil tumors.
And what grows in the throat from other tumors; there may appear inside the throat glands resembling those that appear outside, called tonsils. If you treat them with what was mentioned in the section and they do not heal,
then look: if the tumor is hard, dark in color, and insensitive, do not approach it with an instrument. And if it is red in color and its base is thick, do not approach it with an instrument either, for fear of bleeding; rather, leave it until it matures, then either you will lance it or it will break open of its own accord.
And if it is white in color, round, and its base is thin, this is what should be cut.  The procedure is that you should look before operating whether its hot inflammation has completely subsided or lessened somewhat.
Then seat the patient facing the sun, his head in your lap, and open his mouth; and take an assistant with you who presses his tongue downwards with an instrument of this shape:
It is made of silver or copper, thin like a knife. When you press the tongue down with it and the tumor becomes visible to you, take a hook and pierce it into the tonsil and pull it outwards as much as possible, without pulling anything of the membranes along with it.
Then cut it with an instrument of this shape: it resembles scissors, except that its two ends are curved, and each of them is very sharp at its edge next to the other; it is made of Indian iron or Damascene steel:
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a scalpel of this shape:
sharp on one side and not sharp on the other. After you cut one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner. Then, after cutting, gargle the patient with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate peel or myrtle leaves or similar astringents have been boiled, until the bleeding stops. Then treat it until it heals. Other tumors may grow in the throat besides the tonsils; cut them as described for the tonsils.
I treated a woman who had a tumor that had grown inside her throat, resembling a polyp, somewhat insensitive, which almost blocked her throat. The woman breathed through a narrow passage and was prevented from eating and drinking water.
She was on the verge of death if she remained one or two days. Two branches extended from the tumor, reaching her nostrils. I acted quickly, inserted a hook into one of them, and pulled; a suitable piece was extracted.
Then I cut it where I reached it from the nostril. Then I did the same with what protruded from the other nostril. Then I opened her mouth, pressed down her tongue, inserted the hook into the tumor itself, and cut some of it; only a little blood flowed.
The woman's throat opened, and she immediately began to drink water and eat. We continued to cut away parts of the tumor repeatedly for a long time, the tumor replacing what was cut, until it was prolonged for both of us.
Then I devised a plan and cauterized the tumor inside the throat. It stopped growing, then she traveled away, and I did not know what God did with her afterward.
Chapter Thirty-Seven
On cutting a uvular tumor.
That which is called ‘anba’ if it descends as a growth to the uvula and swells, and is white and oblong; it is called ‘‘amud’ if it is thick below and round, it is called ‘anba’. If it is treated with what we mentioned in the section and the treatment is not effective,
and I saw that the hot tumor had subsided from it and it was thin; then it should be cut, and what is gathered together and round, and does not have length, and is bloody or the color of pus, or black, or without sensation, then it should be avoided cutting.
For there is danger to the patient. Therefore, if you see it in the manner that I described, of its whiteness and length, then seat the patient facing the sun and compress his tongue with the instrument whose description I presented earlier, then pierce the ‘anba’ with the hook.
And pull it downwards and cut it with one of the two instruments which I mentioned in cutting the tonsils.  And it is necessary that you cut only that which exceeds the natural state without excess, for if you cut more of it you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after cutting, use what we described in cutting the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient is afraid of its being cut, then it is necessary to use a method of cauterizing it without fear or caution. The method of cauterization is with a sharp medicine, and that is that you place the patient's head in your lap.
Then compress his tongue with the instrument that we mentioned, then take of the sharp water which I mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and mix it with unquenched lime, and make it neither thick nor thin, and fill the concavity of this instrument with it, and this is its image:
Its end, in which you place the medicine, has a concavity like the concavity of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself, and the patient lying on his side so that the saliva flows from his mouth from inside his throat so that nothing descends from it into his throat to harm him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine while you press it against the uvula for about half an hour until you see that it has blackened and the stinging of the medicine has ceased. And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of a probe and wet the cotton in the medicine.
And insert the probe with the cotton into the tube from above until you stick the cotton to the uvula. Do this repeatedly until you achieve what you want from cauterizing the uvula. Then leave it; it will wither and fall after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, reapply it.
After cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in butter and wipe away the medicine around it. Then gargle with cold water and treat from the outside with lotions and from the inside with gargles until it heals. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and further from fear.
The uvula may also be treated with something gentler than cauterization and cutting; it is treated with fumigation in this way: take futhanga, zofu, savory, sedab, wormwood, chamomile, qisum, and similar herbs. Gather them all or some of them in a pot and immerse them in vinegar.
Boil it, the pot being plastered with wise mud, and there should be a hole in the middle of the lid onto which a hollow instrument is fitted, made in this way, of silver or copper.
Insert the end containing the pomegranate into the patient's mouth until the steam rises to the uvula through the tube until the uvula is well compressed. Then repeat it several times until it withers. And beware of performing this treatment at the first occurrence of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling.  Rather, it should be done when its hot swelling subsides. If you do not have this instrument, take a reed and fit an eggshell to its end, lest the patient's mouth be burned.
Because the eggshell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth, and this is among the good treatments, with its safety.
Chapter thirty-eight.
On removing thorns and other things that lodge in the throat.
Often a fish bone or thorn or something else lodges in the throat.  Then you should remove what is visible to the eye, after compressing the tongue with the instrument, at the time of the sun.
So that what is in the throat becomes clear to you. And what does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, then it is necessary that you induce vomiting in the patient before his food is digested in his stomach, for perhaps the lodged object will come out with the vomit; or the patient may swallow a piece of turnip or the root of a lettuce.
Or he may swallow a piece of dry bread, or you may take a piece of soft sea sponge and tie it to a string, then he swallows it; and when it reaches the place of the thorn, pull the string quickly. Do this repeatedly.
Often the thorn or bone will stick to it and be removed. If it is not removed by what we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument of this shape:
It should be slightly thicker than a probe, and at its end a hook. The patient inserts it into his throat gently, raising his head upwards, and being careful not to touch his throat lest it cause coughing, and pushes the bone or thorn with it; or the physician inserts it with his hand.
And the patient's insertion of it is better, because he knows the position of the lodged object, and pushes downwards or pulls upwards with the instrument, all this as much as he is able, until it comes out.
Chapter thirty-nine.
On removing leeches lodged in the throat.
If you treat the leech with what we mentioned in the section on treatment with medication, and it is not effective, then look then into the patient's throat in the sunlight, after you have pressed down his tongue with the instrument which I described to you.
If your sight falls upon the leech, then draw it out with a small hook or with a fine, tightly-made forceps; and if you are not able to do so otherwise, then take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near to the leech, then insert into the hollow of the tube a piece of iron heated in the fire.
Do this repeatedly, and let the patient abstain from water all that day; then let him take a vessel full of cold water and open his mouth into it and gargle with it, and let him not swallow a drop of it, and let him move the water from time to time with his hand.
Then the leech will fall to the bottom if it feels the water; and if it does not come out by what we have described, then fumigate the throat with urine and with asafoetida with the instrument which was described in the fumigation of the tonsils; do this repeatedly, for it will fall.
The method of operation in fumigation is that you take a pot containing embers heated in the fire, and the pot is covered with a lid in the middle of which is a hole; then you fit into that hole the end of the instrument, then you throw in the fumigant, and the patient puts his mouth to the end of the tube and closes his mouth so that the fumigant does not escape until he knows that the fumigant has reached his throat.
Then the leech will fall to the bottom; and if it does not fall, then let the fumigation be repeated, and let the patient endure thirst and eat salty things and garlic, and let him not drink water; then it must come out by this treatment. And this is a picture of an instrument by which the leech is drawn from the throat if it is seen.
And it resembles hooks, as you see, except that it has this curvature which enters the throat, and its ends resemble the mouth of a bird; in them is the roughness of a file, so if it seizes anything it does not leave it.
And another instrument, with which the tongue is pressed down; it is a wide ring of iron, as much as the mouth can contain above the tongue; it is inserted into the mouth standing on its side, so that the mouth remains open without the will of the patient, and he cannot remove it at all until it is removed.
The description of the instrument with which the mouth is opened in this very way.
Chapter Forty.
It will then speak of cauterizing tumors and incising them.
Tumors are numerous and varied in kind, as will be mentioned one by one in this book; they differ in their cauterization and incision in two respects: one, the type of tumor itself and what it contains of humors.
The second type is because of the locations in which they occur in the body, for a tumor occurring in the head is not the same as a tumor occurring in the rectum, and a tumor occurring in a fleshy location is not the same as a tumor occurring in a joint; each has its own rule of treatment.
Some tumors should not be cauterized except after the pus in them has matured and become complete, and some should be cauterized while they are raw and have not fully matured, such as tumors that are near the joints.
For if a tumor occurs near a joint and its condition persists until it corrupts what is around it, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, and this may cause paralysis of that limb, or the tumor may be near a vital organ.
Because if you delay its cauterization until it matures, you will harm that vital organ; or it may be near the rectum, so cauterize it raw.  It is necessary to cauterize a raw tumor that is not fully mature which is near the rectum, lest it corrupt the depths and penetrate into the rectum and become a fistula.
And it becomes something that does not heal.  You should know the time to cauterize tumors that have fully matured, which is when the pain of the tumor subsides, the fever disappears, the redness and throbbing decrease, the head of the tumor becomes pitted, and all the other signs [are present].
The cauterization should be applied to the lowest possible part of the tumor if that is possible, so that the flow of matter is easier downwards, or in the thinnest and most prominent part of the tumor. The cauterization should follow the length of the body if the tumors are in the hands or feet.
And the places of the muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries, and in short, in all flat places that do not bend.  As for those that bend, the cauterization should follow the contour of that place. As for tumors in fleshy places, it is best to leave them uncauterized.
Until its maturation is complete, as we have said, for if you cauterize it before that, the flow of pus from it will be prolonged, and it will be very harmful and dirty, and perhaps its lips and depths will harden. Some tumors may be cauterized across the body when necessary, or according to the needs of the affected area.
