In small tumors, one cauterization should be used; in large tumors, a wide cauterization or many incisions should be made, according to the size of the tumor.  Some tumors may need to have the skin scraped and cut if it has become like a rag.
And it has reached the point where it has died, as happens in many abscesses and hidden collections. Some of them are incised with a three-sided incision, and some are cut in the shape of an ivy leaf, like the inguinal tumor.
Some use a circular incision, a crescent-shaped incision, and similar incisions.  If it does not have a head, like smooth, flat tumors, it should only be cauterized simply. If the tumor is large, has been present for a long time, and you cauterize it, you should not rush to remove all the pus at once.
Rather, remove some of it, then dress the tumor until the next day, then remove some more pus; do this repeatedly gradually until you have removed all of it.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old man, for the vital spirit often dissolves with the sudden removal of pus, and the patient may die without your noticing. Therefore, be extremely cautious in this matter.
After cauterizing these tumors, you should wipe the wound and examine it. If the wound of the tumor is small, or the incision is simple, use lint or old cotton for packing. If the tumor is large and the cauterization incisions are numerous,
you should insert a tenton into each incision until they meet. If some of the skin of the tumor has been cut or eroded, you should pack it with old cotton or linen lint without moisture, and dress it until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during the procedure, use cold water and vinegar, after soaking a linen cloth in them, and apply it repeatedly to the bleeding area.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the methods and treatments and styptics that we have described in many places in this book and in the treatise. If it is winter and the tumor is in a highly nervous area, you should moisten the bandages with a warm potion and oil.
Apply them to the area. If it is summer and the area is fleshy, you should apply bandages soaked in cold water and oil, or in a cold potion and oil.  Then, as we said, on the third day you should unwrap the tumor and wipe it.
And use similar treatments until it heals. This is what you need to know about the general treatment of tumors. As for the detailed method, I have described the treatment of each tumor individually, summarized and categorized.
Chapter Forty-One.
On the incision of tumors that appear on the scalp.
Small tumors appear on the scalp; they are a type of boil contained within membranes, similar in appearance to a chicken's pouch.  There are many types, some fatty, and some containing a moisture resembling mud.
And some contain a moisture resembling pus and broth, and so on, and some are hardened and solid.  There is no danger in incising and removing all of them, unless you encounter an artery during the incision.
The procedure for their incision is to first probe them with the instrument whose image follows, which is called the 'muddass', so that you know what they contain. If what they contain is moisture, then incise them lengthwise with a simple incision in this manner:
Begin the incision from line B to line C. When the moisture has drained, dissect and remove the entire sac that contained that moisture, leaving absolutely nothing behind, for it often returns if anything remains.
Then, soak a cotton swab in yellow ointment if available, otherwise in salt water, and fill the wound with it. Leave it until the next day, for it will consume what remains of the sac. Then, apply the cotton swab with the yellow ointment again and again, if necessary.
Until you are certain that nothing of the sac remains. Then treat the wound with ointments until it heals. And if the swelling contains fatty material, incise it in this manner:
Make a firm incision and place hooks in the edges of the wound.  Dissect it from every side and do your best to remove the membrane containing it. If an artery obstructs you, do as I have described for you, and do likewise for the swelling if it is hardened by the incision.
The treatment is the same as I have described. The incision on a hardened swelling is easier because it contains little blood and moisture.  Some of these swellings in the head in some people contain absolutely no moisture at all.  And so I incised a swelling in...
the head of an old woman, and I found the swelling like hard, rough, white stone, impossible to break; and if someone threw it, it would cut him.  And it was not like other swellings occurring in the head, except for those that appear in the heads of children.
And at the roots of the ears, incise them all with a simple incision and make their bottom from their lower parts so that the flow of matter to the bottom is easier; then treat them with whatever treatment suits them.
Chapter Forty-Two.
Concerning the incision on the swellings which often appear in the neck. These swellings often appear in the neck, under the armpits, and in the groins; and they may appear in the rest of the body. That which appears in the neck may be one or many, and some develop from others.
And each swelling of them is within a special sac, as is the case in boils and the swellings of the head as we have described. And the types of these swellings are many: some are calcified, some contain fluids, and some are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
So I saw one of them in good condition to the touch, and its appearance was close to the color of the skin; it moved in every direction and was not attached to a nerve of the neck, nor to a vein, nor to an artery, nor was it deep-seated. Then it should be incised with a simple incision from above to below the body, in this manner, from line J to line B.
And you should dissect it from every side and stretch the edges of the skin with a hook or two hooks or many hooks if you need that, as we said in the swellings of the head.  Remove it little by little and be careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and let the scalpel not be very sharp.
Lest your hand increase the cutting or the patient be disturbed, and you cut what you do not need to cut. If you cut a vein or artery and it hinders your work, then place in the wound powdered verdigris or some of the styptics that stop blood.
And tighten the wound and leave it until the acuteness of the swelling subsides and the wound relaxes and begins to suppurate, for the bleeding will then stop. Then return to your work until you finish, then examine with your index finger if there are any other small tumors remaining, so cut them and clean them.
If there is a large vein at the root of the tumor, then it should not be cut from its root, but it should be tied with a double, tight thread and left until it falls off by itself without harm. Then stuff the wound with old cotton that has been dipped in Egyptian ointment, then treat it.
And if you cut the tumor completely, then you should bring together the edges of the wound and stitch it immediately after you ascertain that no residue remains. If you see that some residue of the bone of the tumor remains,
then you should aim to cut to its root and base and use stitching and what we have mentioned. And as for tumors containing fluids, lance them simply where their ripening appears, and make the incision from below as we said.
Then, after lancing, use a dressing with Egyptian ointment or similar to consume what remains of the corruption, until when you see that the wound has been cleansed, treat it with ointments that promote granulation until it heals.
Chapter forty-three.
Concerning the incision of the throat for a tumor that occurs inside the throat.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the throat, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it. This is the account of their words. As for those with angina, they should avoid the incision of the throat, since they will not benefit from it.
Because all the veins and the lung will be diseased. But those with a hot tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if there is no illness in the windpipe, then it is necessary to use the incision of the throat to escape the harm which results from suffocation.
Then, if we want to do this, we should incise the throat below three or four rings of the windpipe, a small transverse incision between two rings, to the extent that the incision is in the membrane, not in the cartilage. This place is suitable for the incision.
Because it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are distant from it. If the physician is timid, then he should stretch the skin of the throat with a hook, then incise the skin until he reaches the windpipe, avoiding the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should incise the membrane that we described.  The incision of the windpipe is indicated by the phlegm which comes out of it along with what is excised, and by the interruption of the voice. The wound is left open for a time. Then, when the time in which suffocation was feared has passed, the edges of the wound are brought together from the skin.
And its outline alone, without the cartilage. Then use medications which cause the growth of flesh until it heals. The author of this book said: The explanation of this statement which we have mentioned is only if they see the patient's throat obstructed by one of these tumors which were mentioned.
And the patient was close to death and his life was about to end. They proceeded to incise the throat so that the patient might breathe somewhat at the site of the wound and be saved from death.  They were ordered to leave the wound open until the intensity of the illness subsides, and its intensity is about three days or so.
Then they were ordered to suture the wound and treat it until it heals. What I witnessed myself is that a maidservant took a knife and passed it across her throat, cutting some of the lung windpipe. I was called to treat her, and I found her weakening as a slaughtered animal weakens. So I uncovered the wound.
Then I found the blood which came out of the wound to be slight, and I was certain that she had not cut a vein nor a jugular vein. Air was exiting from the wound. I immediately outlined the wound and treated it until it healed.  Nothing happened to the maidservant except a slight hoarseness in the voice, nothing more.
And after days she returned to her best condition. From this, we say that incising the throat is not dangerous.
Chapter forty-four.
Concerning the incision of the tumor which appears in the throat from the outside and is called the throat elephantiasis.
This tumor, which is called throat elephantiasis, is a large tumor of the color of the body. It is frequent in women and is of two types.
Either it is natural or accidental. As for the natural, there is no remedy for it; as for the accidental, it is of two kinds: one resembling fatty swellings, and the other resembling the tumor which results from the complication of an artery; and in its incision there is danger. Therefore, one should not at all apply iron to it, except what is small of it; if you examine and investigate it with a probe and find it to resemble a fatty swelling, and it is not attached to anything of the veins, then incise it as you incise fatty swellings. And remove it with its containing sac, if it is in a sac; otherwise, remove all of it, then treat the place with what is appropriate of treatment.
Chapter forty-five.
On the incision of various swellings.
Swellings are numerous in their types, and I have mentioned all their types in the classification. It is necessary to explain here the difference between a swelling and an abscess, since it is difficult.  So I say that with an abscess there is heat, fever, and severe pain until the boiling of the matter subsides. And the suppuration is completed; then the fever and intensity subside.  A swelling is not accompanied by heat, fever, or pain, and it is contained in a membranous sac, which is a special covering for it. It is the color of the body, and its beginning is like a lentil, and it becomes like a blister, larger or smaller.
And they are of two types: either fatty, or containing moisture. The colors of the moisture are many, as I have mentioned in the classification. Therefore, when you come to treat a swelling, you should first probe and examine it with the instrument called the *muddass*, whose image is given in the chapter which follows this.
Assess the size of the swelling, then remove the probe and look at what comes out after it. If a fluid of any color comes out, then make a simple incision as I have mentioned in the case of other swellings. If no fluid comes out after the probe, then know that it is fatty.
Then make a firm incision on it in this manner, as I have taught you, and hook it with hooks.  Peel the skin from every side gently, and preserve the sac if you can, so that you may remove it whole with the swelling.
If the sac is torn during the procedure and you cannot remove it whole—and this often happens—then remove it piece by piece until nothing remains. For if any part, however small or large, remains, the swelling will most often return.
If it overcomes you and a little remains, cauterize the wound when you have finished with some sharp cauterizing agents.  Tighten the wound and place on top of it something that soothes hot swelling and treat it with other treatments until it heals. If the swelling is large, then suture its lips and treat it with what unites [the tissues].
If you encounter a superficial or deep vein and bleeding occurs, quickly cauterize the area with powdered vitriol and leave it compressed for two or three days until the wound becomes inflamed and the flow of blood stops. Then return to removing what remains of the swelling.

Chapter forty-seven.
On the treatment of men's breasts.
The breasts of some people may swell at the age of puberty until they resemble the breasts of women, remaining swollen and unsightly. Whoever dislikes this should make a crescent-shaped incision on the breast in this manner.
From line B to line C. Then remove all the fat, then fill the wound with cauterizing medicine, then bring the edges of the wound together with sutures and treat it until it heals.  If the breast droops downwards and becomes flaccid due to its size, as happens to women,
it should be incised in its upper sides with two crescent-shaped incisions, each connecting to the other at their ends, so that the larger line encircles the smaller one in this manner: From line B to line C, then remove the skin between the incisions and remove the fat, and use what we have mentioned of sutures and dressings and whatever treatment is needed until it heals.
If you cannot remove all that needs to be cut away because of the patient's anxiety or because of bleeding, then you should pack the wound with cotton soaked in corrosive ointment and leave it until it consumes the remaining fat, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter forty-eight.
In the treatment of tumors that appear under the armpit.
These tumors that appear under the armpit are a kind of hard, cystic swellings, some of which contain fluids.  Those that contain fluids should be incised with a crescent-shaped incision in this manner.
From line B to line C. If all the fluid in the tumor is evacuated, pack it with old cotton and leave it until the next day, then remove the cotton.
And treat with a suitable ointment until it heals. If the tumor is of the type of nodular swellings, incise it as we mentioned in the incision for swellings. If the wound continues to suppurate, cauterize it as we previously mentioned.
Chapter forty-nine.
On the incision of a tumor that appears near an artery or vein, called aneurysma.
If an artery is injured and the skin over it heals, a tumor often results; similarly, a vein may also develop a swelling and tumor.
The signs by which one knows whether the tumor and swelling are from an artery or from a vein are: if the tumor is from an artery, it is oblong, deep-seated in the body; and if you press the tumor with your finger, you feel as if it has a throbbing.
That which is from a vein, the tumor is round, on the surface of the body. Incision of these tumors is dangerous, especially those in the armpit, groin, and neck, and in many other places in the body, and if they are very large; it is necessary to avoid treating them with iron.
And those also in the extremities or in the head should be avoided. As for those that are a swelling of the mouth of an artery, make a longitudinal incision in the skin, then open the incision with hooks, then dissect the artery and free it from the membranes.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle under it and pass it to the other side, and tie the artery with a doubled thread in two places, as I have shown you in the ligation of the two arteries in the temples; then incise with a scalpel the place between the ligatures until all the blood in it comes out.
Then the tumor will resolve, and then you use treatment that produces pus, until the ligatures fall off, then treat it with suitable ointments until it heals.
If the tumor is from a vein incision, then you should grasp with your hand as much of the tumor as possible, together with the skin, then insert a needle below the place that you grasped with your hand, and pass it with a doubled thread until you bring it out from the other side. Then tie the tumor tightly with it, according to what I have mentioned to you in tying the uvula from both sides with the ends of the thread; if you fear that the threads will slip, then insert another needle with another thread under the whole tumor at the intersection of the first needle.
And tighten your threads in four places, then incise the tumor in its middle until what is in it comes out; then cut off the excess skin and leave what was tied, then apply a pledget that you have soaked in a potion and oil. Then use treatment with cautery and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
On the tumor that occurs from the twisting of a nerve.
Just as a tumor occurs in an artery and vein, so too does a tumor occur in a nerve if a blow occurs to it or it occurs from excessive fatigue or the like.  This is most common in the areas of the wrists and heels.
And in every place where the joints move, it is a subtle swelling, resembling in color the rest of the body. In most cases, it is without pain, and if pressed firmly, the patient feels a kind of numbness. The swelling is not concentrated deep within the body.
Rather, it is under the skin and moves in every direction, neither forward nor backward.  As for that which is in the joints, it should not be exposed to iron, for it may cause harm. And as for that which is in the head or forehead,
make an incision in the skin with a scalpel. If the tumor is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it from its base. If it is large, hook it with a hook and peel it off, then remove it and close the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals.
Chapter fifty-one.
On cutting warts which appear in the abdomen.
Many people develop warts on their abdomens and other parts of their bodies. These are called  "fungal" because of their resemblance to mushrooms. Their base is thin and their head is thick; their lips may be inverted. Some are small, others very large.
I once saw a man who had two warts on his abdomen, resembling mushrooms.  They were white, thin at the base, their lips inverted and cracked, and constantly discharging moisture. I cut them off; one weighed about eighteen ounces, the other about six.
In cutting them, observe: if the patient is moist, and the wart is white, moist, and thin at the base, cut it with a broad scalpel. Have cautery irons ready at hand, for often much blood flows when they are cut.
If the bleeding is profuse, cauterize immediately. If you find the patient cowardly and afraid of being cut with iron, take a strong lead thread, and tie it tightly around the wart of this kind. Leave it for two days, then tighten the lead further, continuing to tighten it.
Keep tightening until the wart is affected, until it separates and falls off by itself without further effort. If the wart has a thick base, there is some danger and uncertainty in cutting it, especially if it is in the abdomen; however, it may be possible to cut off half or part of it.
Then cauterize it to prevent recurrence, and treat it until the wound heals. Beware of cutting a wart that is dark in color, insensitive, and foul-looking; for it is a cancerous tumor, and we shall describe cancer later.
Chapter fifty-two.
In the treatment of umbilical hernias.
An umbilical hernia may result from several causes: either from a rupture of the peritoneum of the abdomen, from which fat or intestine protrudes, as happens in other hernias; or from blood flowing from a vein or artery, as mentioned before; or from wind that collects there.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and protrusion of fat, then the swelling will be similar in color to the body, soft, painless, and varying in position. If it is due to the protrusion of the intestine, its position will be, as we have described, more variable. If you press it with your fingers, it disappears and then returns; sometimes there is gurgling, and it increases greatly upon entering a bath and with strenuous exertion. If it is due to moisture, it will be soft.
It does not disappear when pressed with your hand, nor does it increase or decrease. If it is due to blood, then in addition to these signs, the swelling appears dark. If it is due to newly grown flesh, the swelling is firm, hard, and remains at a constant size.
If it is due to wind, it will be soft to the touch. The treatment is as follows: if the umbilical hernia is due to blood from an artery or vein, or to wind, then you should refrain from treating it, for there is danger and uncertainty, as I have explained to you in the chapter where I discussed swellings arising from arteries and veins.
If the umbilical hernia is due to the intestine or fat, then you should instruct the patient to hold himself and stand upright, then draw a line with ink around the entire umbilicus. Then you should order him to lie on his back before you, then incise with a wide scalpel around the umbilicus at the place marked with ink. Then extend the center of the swelling upwards with a large probe, and then tie the place of the incision with a strong thread or silk ligature tightly.
The knot of the ligature should be a loop. Then open the center of the tightened swelling above the ligature and insert your index finger. Search for the intestine; if you find it caught by the ligature, loosen the loop and push the intestine back into the abdomen. If you find fat, extend it with a probe.
And cut away its excess. If an artery or vein obstructs you, ligate it carefully and return to your work. Take two needles, and thread them with strong threads. Insert the needles into the incision you made around the swelling, crossing them, having passed them through. Then tighten the swelling at four points on the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the place until the tightened flesh putrefies and falls off by itself, or cut it if it softens and decays. Then treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If the umbilical hernia is from flesh that has grown there,
or from moisture, then it is necessary to incise the swelling as we said and evacuate the blood or moisture that you find in it, then treat it with what will close the wound.
Chapter fifty-three.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have already mentioned in the classification the types of cancer and how to treat it with medications, and warned against treating it with iron, lest it ulcerate.  We have also mentioned cancer that develops in the womb and warned against treating it.
The ancients stated that when the cancer is in a place that can be completely excised, like cancer that is in the breast or thigh, or similar accessible members, and especially if it is small and beginning, then it should be removed entirely.  However, if it is advanced and large, then it should not be touched.
For I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me achieve this.  If the cancer is accessible, as we said, then you should first purge the patient with black hellebore several times, and then bleed him if there is obvious fullness in the veins.
Then you should position the patient in a way that allows you to work, and then apply the appropriate hooks to the cancer, and then scrape it from every side, together with the skin, thoroughly, until nothing of its roots remains.  Let the blood flow and do not stop it quickly.
Rather, squeeze the areas and drain all the thick blood with your hand or with whatever instruments you can. If during the procedure you encounter profuse bleeding from a severed artery or vein, then tie off the vessel until the bleeding stops, and then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter fifty-four.
On the treatment of fistula.
We have already described in the classification the types of dropsy, how the accumulation of water occurs, and the signs of each type, and its treatment with medication.  That which is treated with cautery is only the ascitic type; the tympanitic type and the fleshy type are not treated with cautery at all.
For that is lethal, if you have treated this type of ascitic fistula with medication and your treatment has been ineffective, then look: if the patient is extremely weak, or has another disease besides the fistula, such as...
cough or diarrhea, and the like, then avoid treating him with cautery, for that is dangerous. If you see that the patient is strong, has no disease other than the fistula alone, and is neither a child nor an old man, then the procedure is that you make the patient stand before you. and an attendant behind him massages his abdomen with his hands, pushing the water downwards towards the pubic area. Then you take a lancet shaped like this:
pointed at both ends, long, and with a sharpened point like a lancet, except that it has a slight curve so that it does not penetrate the intestines during the procedure and injure them. Then look: if the fistula originated from the intestines…
then you should make the incision three fingers' breadth below the navel, directly above the pubic area. If the fistula originated from a liver disease, then let your incision be three fingers' breadth to the left of the navel. If its origin is a spleen disease, then let the incision be three fingers' breadth on the right side.
Nor should the incision be on the side on which the patient wishes to lie down, lest the fluids flow to that weak area. Then, pierce the entire skin with the instrument, then insert the instrument into that incision and raise your hand.
with the lancet between the skin and the peritoneum, as if you are flaying it, and the amount you flay should be about the size of a fingernail or so. Then pierce the peritoneum until the lancet reaches the empty space, which is the location of the water, then remove it and insert into the opening the instrument which looks like this:
It is like a small tube made of silver, copper, or smooth, polished lead, having a small opening at its lower end and three openings in its sides, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And one may sharpen its end in the form of a pen nib, like this image:
At its upper end is a ring; for when the instrument reaches the water, it will immediately descend on the instrument, and you will drain a moderate amount of water at once, for if you drain more than is necessary at once, the patient may die from the dissolution of his vital spirit.
Or he may suffer a faintness close to death. But drain according to his strength and what indicates to you the state of the patient's strength from the strength of his pulse and the good color, then remove the instrument and the water will be contained, as it will be contained immediately.
Because of the skin which holds the opening on the peritoneum which I told you to suture in that manner, then return the instrument another day if you see that the patient can bear it.
And remove also a small amount of water; do this for several days, and you are responsible and careful to avoid error, until only a little water remains.  If you fear for the patient and leave a large amount of water, treat it by burial in hot sand.
And profuse sweating in the bath and sun, and accustom him to thirst, and treat him with drying medicines until he recovers, and cauterize the stomach, liver, and spleen after removing the water as I described before.
Chapter fifty-five.
In the treatment of children who are born with their urinary passages unperforated, or with a narrow or misplaced opening.
Some boys may be born with their urethra imperforate; it is necessary to perforate it as soon as they are born.
With a very fine scalpel, in this manner:
Then place a thin lead nail in the opening, and bind and hold it for three or four days. When he wants to urinate, remove it and then replace it. If you do not use lead, it is possible that the urine passing through the area will not allow it to close.
As for him whose opening is narrow, treat him with lead as we have said, for many days until it widens. As for those whose opening is misplaced—that is, some are born with the opening at the end of the penis and cannot urinate forward—
until he raises the urethra upwards with his hand, and it is not born before that because the semen cannot reach the uterus in a straight line; this is a very ugly condition. The method of treatment is that the patient lies on his back.
Then stretch his penis with your left hand with a strong pull, and incise the tip of the urethra with a sharp blade or scalpel, the size of a pen, as if you are carving something so that its middle is prominent, resembling a penis, and the opening is in the middle as it should be.
And be careful during your work to avoid bleeding, for this often occurs; therefore stop the bleeding and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter fifty-six.
On the ulcer that appears in the foreskin and penis, and the blackness, corruption, and adhesion of the foreskin to the penis.
This ulcer often appears in the urethra; it is a fleshy, rough growth, and it is benign or malignant.  The benign one should be tied off with a fine ligature.
And cut it until you completely remove it, then apply a cotton swab soaked in Egyptian ointment, then treat it afterwards with palm ointment until it heals. But if the ulcer is malignant and dark in color, then you should cauterize it after cutting and removing it.
If the ulcer is in the foreskin of an uncircumcised man, and part of the ulcer is inside the foreskin and part is outside, then you should remove the ulcer inside first, until it heals; then treat the outside.
Because if you treat both at the same time, you risk perforating the foreskin.  Also, blackness and corruption can appear in the labia and foreskin; you should excise everything that is black and is about to corrupt or has already corrupted.
Then apply honey mixed with crushed and sifted pomegranate peel and acacia, then treat it with other treatments until it heals. If bleeding occurs, use cauterization with a crescent-shaped cautery iron of this shape:
Cauterization is beneficial for both conditions, I mean bleeding and the ulcer if it becomes corrupt. If the glans is eaten away and entirely destroyed at some point, then you should insert a lead tube into the urethra for the patient to urinate through. As for the adhesion of the foreskin to the glans...
This adhesion occurs in those whose foreskin is healthy and who have not been circumcised.  It may also occur due to a wound or swelling.  You should separate it with a blunt scalpel until the bond is broken and the glans is freed on all sides.
If it is difficult to distinguish them on close examination, you should remove some of the glans and some of the foreskin.  The foreskin is thin, and it may easily perforate due to its thinness. Then separate the foreskin from the glans with a thin linen cloth soaked in cold water so that they do not adhere again.
Then treat it with an astringent drink until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-Seven.
On the cleansing of boys and the treatment of what may befall them of ailments.
Circumcision is nothing other than the separation of a connection like other wounds, except that since it is done by our will and is especially used in boys, we must describe the best method of doing it.
And the easiest way leading to safety.  I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books because it was not used in their laws; rather, it is something we have acquired.
by experience. That is to say, I found the majority of surgeons and barbers use purification with the scalpel and with scissors, and they use the forceps and ligature with thread and cutting with the nail.  I have tried all these methods.
I found nothing better than purification with scissors and ligature with thread, because purification with the scalpel often causes the skin to retract, since the skin of the foreskin is two layers, so perhaps the upper layer is cut and the lower layer remains.
Then one is forced to make another cut and renewed pain. Purification with forceps does not prevent cutting the end of the urethra, because it may enter its hole. As for purification with the nail, the skin may slip and your work will be spoiled, or the boy's skin may be naturally short.
Therefore, they are often born like that and do not need purification. I have seen this. As for purification with scissors and ligature with thread, experience has revealed its excellence to me, because the cut of the scissors is proportionate, since the blade above is like the blade below.
So when you squeeze your hand with the proportion of the two blades, you cut with one measure and in one time. Thus, the control of the thread becomes like a wall for the skin of the urethra from all sides, with no mistake whatsoever. The way to proceed first is to reassure the boy.
Especially if he understands a little that you are only tying the thread in his urethra and leaving it until another day. Then cheer and please him in every way you can and that he accepts with his mind. Then make him stand upright before you.
And do not let him sit. Hide the scissors in your sleeve or under your foot, so that the boy's eye does not fall upon them at all, nor on any of the instruments. Then insert your hand into his urethra, inflate the skin, and lift it up until the head of the urethra comes out.
Then cleanse it of whatever dirt gathers there. Then tie the marked place with a doubled thread, then tie a second ligature a little below it. Then hold the lower ligature firmly with your thumb and forefinger and cut between the two ligatures. Then quickly raise the skin and bring out the head of the urethra. Then let the blood flow a little, for it is better and causes less swelling of the urethra. Then wipe it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some burnt ash of dried gourd; this is the best I have tried, or finely ground barley, which is also excellent.
Then quickly raise the skin and bring out the head of the urethra. Then let the blood flow a little, for it is better and causes less swelling of the urethra. Then wipe it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some burnt ash of dried gourd; this is the best I have tried, or finely ground barley, which is also excellent. Then apply a poultice of a cooked egg white in rose water, beaten with fresh, fine rose oil, and leave it on until the next day. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
And this is the image of the scissors suitable for circumcision:
It should be thin, sharp, without curve, and the nail should be well-fitted.  The length of the blades should be equal to the length of the handles. As for the error that occurs in circumcision, sometimes the inner skin is completely or partially inverted during the cutting, therefore it should be extended with your fingernail immediately, before the area swells.
Cut it evenly. If you cannot hold it with your fingernail, pull it with a hook and cut it. If three days have passed and what is under the head of the urethra remains swollen and inflamed, leave it until the acute inflammation subsides and then remove it gently.
Cut it as seems best to you, and be careful of the glans penis; for if a part of the glans penis is cut, it does no harm. Treat it with what heals the wound from the poultices which we described in the chapter on poultices.
And if more of the skin than necessary is cut and it retracts upwards, this also does little harm. Treat it as mentioned until it heals.
Chapter fifty-eight.
On the treatment of urine retention in the bladder.
Urine retention in the bladder is due to its obstruction by a stone, clotted blood, pus, a growth, or something similar. If you treat this with the types of treatment mentioned in the classification.
And if the urine does not flow, and you see that its retention is due to a stone that has lodged in the neck of the bladder, then the patient should be made to sit on his knees, then a man should mount his back and press his belly towards his back, and the patient should hold himself as much as he can.
Then the neck of the bladder will be forced to push the stone backwards, and the urine will flow. If it does not flow by what we have mentioned, and the matter becomes severe for the patient, then it is necessary to use its removal with the instrument called a catheter, whose image is this:
It is made of silver, and it is thin, smooth, hollow like a bird's feather quill in thinness, about a span and a half long, with a gentle funnel at its head. The way to draw urine with it is to take a doubled thread and tie to its end wool or cotton firmly.
And insert the end of the thread into the bottom of the catheter and cut with scissors if any of the wool is left over so that it enters the tube and closes it like a button. Then lubricate the catheter with oil, butter, or egg white, and seat the patient on a chair.
And cleanse his bladder and urethra with moist ointments or oil and lukewarm water. Then gently insert the catheter into the urethra until it reaches the root of the urethra, then bend the urethra upwards towards the navel, and then push the catheter inside.
Until the urethra is completely bent, then push the catheter inside until it reaches near the rectum; incline the penis downwards with the catheter inside, then push it until it reaches the bladder, and the patient feels that it has reached something empty.
This method is used because the passage through which urine flows is winding. Then pull the thread with the wool gently; the urine will follow the wool. Then remove it, and the urine will flow out.  Replace the catheter.
Continue doing this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.
Chapter fifty-nine.
How to inject the bladder with a syringe.
Illustrations of instruments suitable for this are made of silver or hollow ivory, with a long tube as thin as pus.  If you wish to instill water or medicine, this is done with an instrument called a syringe; this is its image.
It is made of silver or hollow ivory, with a long tube as thin as a millet stalk, hollow throughout except for the tip, which is solid and has three holes: two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And the hollow part in which the propellant is, should be just large enough to fit it without excess, so that when it draws up moisture, it is drawn up, and when it pushes, it pushes outwards, as does the syringe with which oil is thrown in sea battles.
If you wish to introduce fluids into the bladder, insert the tip of the syringe into the fluid and draw upwards on the piston; the fluid will be drawn into the body of the syringe. Then insert its tip into the urethra, as described for catheters, and then push the fluid with the piston.
Then that fluid will reach the bladder immediately, so that the patient feels it. This is also the image of a delicate clyster with which the bladder is injected.
It is made of silver or leather; its upper end resembles a small funnel, and below it is a groove in which the ligature is placed. Then take a camel's bladder and put in it the fluid you want to inject into the bladder; then tie it tightly between the two grooves with a doubled thread.
Warm the fluid slightly over a fire, then insert the tip of the clyster into the urethra, then squeeze the bladder containing the fluid firmly until the patient feels that the fluid has reached the bladder.
If you do not have a bladder, take a piece of thin material and make a circle from it; pierce it all around. Then insert a strong thread into these holes as they go around; then gather the circle as you would gather a tablecloth, after placing in it whatever fluids, ointments, and waters you desire.
Then tie it to the instrument and use it as you did with the bladder, squeezing with your hand until the fluid reaches the bladder.
Chapter Sixty.
On the extraction of stones.
We have already mentioned in the classification the types of stones and their treatment with medication, and described the difference between stones formed in the kidneys and those formed in the bladder, and the signs of each.  We have also described the stones which require manual intervention and incision.
These are the stones formed specifically in the bladder and those which lodge in the urethra, and I will describe this clearly and concisely.
I say that stones formed in the bladder most often affect boys, and one of their signs is that the urine passes from the bladder like water in its thinness.
Sand appears in it, and he scratches and plays with his penis; it often hangs down, then swells, and the anus protrudes in many of them.  It is easily expelled in boys until they reach fourteen years of age, and is difficult in old men; as for young men, it is intermediate between these two.
The larger the stone, the easier its treatment; the smaller, the opposite.  If we proceed to treatment, it is first necessary to administer an enema to the patient to evacuate all the faeces from his intestines, for it may prevent the detection of the stone.
Then the patient is taken by his legs, and shaken downwards so that the stone descends to the neck of the bladder, or he jumps from a high place several times. Then seat him between your hands upright, his hands under his thighs, so that the whole bladder inclines downwards, then examine him.
And feel it externally. If you feel the stone in the space, immediately begin to incise it.  If it does not fall under your touch at all, you should lubricate the index finger with oil from your left hand if the patient is a boy, or the middle finger.
If he is a fully developed boy, insert it into his anus and search for the stone until, when it is under your finger, you move it little by little to the neck of the bladder. Then press on it with your finger and push it outwards towards the place you wish to incise.
And you order a servant to press the bladder with his hand, and you order another servant to stretch with his right hand the scrotum upwards, and with his other hand to stretch the skin under the scrotum away from the place where the incision will be. Then take the lancet, the shape of which is this:
And you incise between the anus and the scrotum, not in the middle, but towards the left side of the perineum. The incision should be on the stone itself, with your finger in the rectum, and you press it outwards obliquely so that the incision is wide externally and narrow internally, to the extent that the stone can pass through.
Do not make it larger, for perhaps the finger in the rectum will press the stone during the incision, and it will come out without difficulty. And know that there may be stones which have angles and edges, making their removal difficult for that reason.
And some are smooth, resembling an acorn and round, so their removal is easy.  But if it has angles and edges, then enlarge the incision a little.  If it does not come out like this, then you should devise a way to grasp it, either with a tightly-fitting forceps whose end is like a rasp.
to secure the stone so that it does not escape, or else insert a delicate instrument with a hooked end under it. If you cannot remove it this way, enlarge the incision a little. If you are overcome by blood, stop it with alum.
If there is more than one, push the largest first towards the bladder opening, then incise upon it, then push the smaller ones afterwards.  Do the same if there are more than two. If it is very large, it is foolish to make a large incision upon it, because two things will result for the patient.
Either he will die, or he will experience constant urinary incontinence because the wound will not heal at all. But try to push it out, or devise a way to break it with forceps until you remove it in pieces.
When you have finished your work, cauterize the wound with  costmary, aloes, and myrrh, and bandage it. Place a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or rose oil and cold water, on top of it to soothe the hot inflammation. Then let the patient lie on his back, and do not untie the bandage until the third day.
When it is loosened, wash the area with plenty of water and oil, then treat it with palm unguent and basil unguent until it heals. If excessive hot inflammation, gangrene, or something similar occurs in the wound, such as blood clotting in the bladder and preventing urination—and this is known by the appearance of blood in the urine—
insert your finger into the wound and remove that blood, for if it remains, it will lead to corruption and putrefaction of the bladder. Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type with a similar treatment until it heals.
Throughout the treatment, it is necessary to bind and bring together the thighs to secure the medicines applied to the area. If the stone is small and has lodged in the urethra, preventing the passage of urine, treat it as I have described before it necessitates incision.
I have often dispensed with incision using this treatment, which I have tested.  It involves taking a steel probe, which looks like this:
The end is triangular, sharp, and fixed in a shaft. Then take a thread and tie the penis below the stone to prevent it from returning to the bladder, then gently insert the probe into the urethra until the probe reaches the stone itself.
And turn the probe in the stone little by little, aiming to pierce it until you pass through to the other side. Then the urine will flow immediately. Then press your hand on what remains of the stone outside the penis, for it will crumble and exit with the urine and the patient will recover.
If this treatment is not feasible due to some impediment, tie a thread below the stone and another thread above the stone. Then incise over the stone in the penis itself between the two threads and extract it.
Then untie the ligature and clean the clotted blood which has formed in the wound.  The reason for tying the thread below the stone is to prevent it from returning to the bladder, and the other ligature above is so that when the thread is untied after the stone is removed, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound.
Therefore, when you tie the upper thread, you should raise the skin upward so that it returns when you are finished and covers the wound, as we said.
Chapter sixty-one.
On the extraction of stones from women.
Stones rarely develop in women; if one should appear, its treatment is difficult and impossible for many reasons. One is that the woman may be a virgin, and another is that you will not find a woman who permits herself to a physician.
If she is chaste or a relative, and thirdly, you will not find a woman skilled in this craft, especially in working with the hands, and fourthly, the place of incision for stones in women is distant from the location of the stone, so a deep incision is needed.
And in that there is danger; if necessity demands it, then a skilled female physician should be sought, but they are rarely found.  If you do not find one, seek a chaste and gentle male physician, or get a skilled midwife experienced in women's matters, or a woman who knows something of this craft.
Then prepare her and instruct her to do everything you order, first examining for the stone.  That is, if the woman is a virgin, she should insert a finger into her rectum and search for the stone. If she finds it and presses it under her finger…
then you order her to incise it. If she is not a virgin but a widow, order the midwife to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone, after placing her left hand on the bladder and pressing it firmly.
If she finds it, she should move it from the mouth of the bladder downwards as far as she can until she reaches the groin, then incise it opposite the middle of the vagina at the groin, whichever side she feels the stone on.
And her finger should not leave the stone, pressing it underneath. Let the incision be small at first, then insert the probe into that small incision. If she feels the stone, enlarge the incision as much as is needed for the stone to come out. And know that there are many types of stones.
Some are small and some large, smooth and rough, long and round, and some branched.  Know their types so you can determine what you need. If the bleeding overwhelms you, sprinkle powdered verdigris on the area and hold it for an hour until the bleeding stops, then return to your work.
until the stone comes out. And prepare the instruments mentioned for removing stones from men to assist you in your work. If the bleeding overwhelms you and you know from the throbbing of the blood that it is from a severed artery, place the styptic on the area.
And bind it tightly with bandages and leave it; do not disturb it, and leave the stone; do not remove it, for the patient may die. Then treat the wound. When the severity of the bleeding subsides after a few days and the area becomes infected, return to your work until you remove the stone.
Chapter sixty-two.
On the incision for the watery swelling.
The watery swelling is an accumulation of moisture in the white peritoneum which is under the skin of the scrotum surrounding the testicle, and it may be in a special membrane prepared for it by nature in some part of the testicle.
So that one thinks it is another testicle. It is between the skin of the scrotum and the white peritoneum which we have mentioned, but this is rare. This swelling develops from weakness affecting the testicles, so that this matter settles there.  It may also result from a blow to the testicles.
This moisture is of many colours; it may be yellowish, or bloody red, or black and dirty, or white and watery, which is most common. The signs by which it is recognized are:
Where the water collects, if it is in the white peritoneum which we have mentioned, the swelling is roundish and slightly elongated, like the shape of an egg, and the testicle does not appear because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
If the moisture is in a special membrane, then the swelling is round on one side of the testicle.
Therefore, one imagines that it is another testicle. If the moisture is between the skin of the scrotum and the white peritoneum, then it is palpable. But if you wish to know the colour of the moisture, probe the swelling with the square probe, the image of which has been given previously;  then judge what is inside by what comes out with the probe.
If we proceed to treatment with cautery, you should order the patient to be bled if he is able and you see that his body is full. Then he should lie on his back on something slightly raised, and you should place many cloths beneath him, then you should sit on his left.
And you should order a servant to sit on his right, extending his penis to one side of the scrotum and towards the groin. Then you take a wide scalpel and incise the skin of the scrotum lengthwise from the middle to near the pubis.
Make the incision straight, parallel to the line that divides the scrotum in half, until you reach the white peritoneum containing [the testicle]; then strip it back, being careful not to tear it. Your stripping should be more on the side adhering to the testicle, and strip back as far as you can.
Then widely incise the peritoneum filled with water and remove all the water. Then separate the edges of the incision with hooks and draw the peritoneum upwards, but do not touch the containing scrotum skin. Cut the peritoneum however you can, either completely or piecemeal.
Especially its thin side, for if you do not completely cut it, you cannot be sure that the water will not return. If the testicle emerges from its covering during your work, when you have finished cutting the peritoneum, replace it then close the edges of the scrotum skin with sutures.
Then treat it like other wounds until it heals. If the testicle is found to be corrupted by another disease, then it is necessary to ligate the vessels in the spermatic cord to prevent bleeding, then cut the testicle from the spermatic cord and remove the testicle; then treat it as mentioned.
If the fluid is collected on both sides, know that they are two hydroceles. Incise the other side as you did the first, similarly. If it is convenient for you to perform both operations at once, do so, then wipe away the blood.
Insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil or rose ointment, and apply externally more wool soaked in a potion and oil, spreading it over the testicles and the flanks of the abdomen, then place folded cloths on top; these are the compresses. And tie it from above with a six-tailed bandage in this manner:
Then two cloths are taken, sewn together, and stuffed with fluffed wool in this manner.  Make the straps with which it is fastened from cotton or wet wool, and gather them tightly in the waistband of the trousers; one strap is taken across the buttocks to the back.
The second is taken towards the pubis, and the two straps are placed on the thighs below, and the remaining two straps on the groins; all the tightening is gathered in the waistband of the trousers.  This hydrocele can also be treated by cauterization instead of incision with an iron.
This is done by taking a small, sharp cautery iron and incising the skin of the scrotum with it while hot, as we described, until the white peritoneum containing the water is exposed; then take another cautery iron of this shape:
It resembles the Greek eye; then sear that peritoneum with it while hot until all the moisture comes out; then stretch the peritoneum with hooks and scrape off the containing layer with the cautery and cut it as much as you can until you excise it all, and be careful not to touch the testicle with the fire.
Know that this procedure is safer and prevents blood loss more effectively than incision.  If during the incision or cauterization you encounter a hot swelling, bleeding, or something else, you should treat whatever occurs according to the appropriate treatment previously mentioned.
If the patient is cowardly and cannot endure this procedure because of its unpleasantness, then use cauterization as I describe, if it is confirmed by the evidence we have presented.
If the hydrocele is watery, then the patient should sit on a high chair, and then sear the lower part of the swelling with a wide scalpel. Make the cauterization along the length of the body and make the opening wide so that all the water flows out. Then apply cotton to the area and bandage it.
Leave it to drain the remaining water, then treat it until the wound heals. If the water does not come out when you cauterize it, and this is because the white membrane obstructs the opening of the wound and prevents the water from coming out,
then you should insert a goose feather or the instrument used to extract water from hydroceles, the image of which has been shown previously, or enlarge the opening of the wound slightly.
Know that the water may return and collect after six months or so. If it collects, cauterize it in the same way, and the patient should protect himself during those days.
Chapter sixty-three.
On the incision on the fleshy membrane and its treatment.
Know that an incision on this membrane is one of the dangerous things leading to death in most cases; therefore, we see that it should be left alone and kept safe.  I will now mention working on it and the types of swelling.
I say that many swellings may occur in the bodies which are composed of the two testicles, and this is due to many causes; either from a sharp fluid flowing to the testicles, or from a blow.  The color of the swelling is the color of the body, and it does not contain water.
And the swelling is firm, and perhaps the swelling is hardened, its color is dark, and it is insensitive. It may be from the twisting and swelling of the arteries, as has been previously mentioned, or from the swelling of the veins. That which is from the swelling of the arteries is known by the dispersion of the swelling.
If you press it with your fingers, it does not disperse. And it should not be incised at all. As for that which is from the swelling of the veins, nothing of it disperses when pressed with the fingers. And this type may be incised, as well as other flesh.
And that is to incise the skin of the testicle, then to extend the testicle upwards and bring it out of the white peritoneum, and to free the spermatic cord from the vessels, and to ligate the vessels, and to cut the spermatic cord after freeing it from every side of the testicle, if the testicle has adhered to that growing flesh.
Then it is necessary to bring out the testicle and cut it. And if the adhesion is between some of the peritoneums or between the vessels, then it is necessary to free all that adhesion and cut it with a circular incision.
If the growth of flesh is in the place of adhesion which is from behind, then it is necessary to cut all of it and bring out the testicle as we said. When your work is completed, dress the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and syrup, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
Chapter sixty-four.
In the treatment of hydrocele with a hernia.
A hernia is a twisted tumor, somewhat twisted, similar to a bunch of grapes, with relaxation of the intestines and difficulty for the patient in movement, exercise, and walking.  The treatment of this condition is similar to what has been mentioned before, and is quite perilous.
However, it is necessary to mention the procedure as the ancients did. I say that it is necessary to seat the patient on a high chair, then push the intestinal contents downwards, then grasp the skin of the testicle with your fingers along with the vessels that are close to the penis.
And let a servant hold it for you, stretching it tightly. Then, incise with a wide, sharp scalpel, making an oblique incision alongside the vessels until the vessels are exposed. Then, dissect from each side as I described to you in the dissection of the arteries in the temples. Then, insert a needle with a doubled thread.
And tie it at the first place where the hernia appeared and tie it also at its end, then incise it in the middle with a vertical incision along its length, and remove what has collected inside of it from the foul, putrid fluids.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds, so that it will heal and the vessels that nourish one of the intestines will fall away; this is not a problem. But if the hernia affects all the vessels, then it is necessary to remove one of the intestines with the vessels, lest the testicle lack nourishment. before cutting the vessels, so that they wither and are of no use.
Chapter Sixty-five.
In the treatment of intestinal hernia.
This hernia occurs from a fissure that appears in the peritoneum extending over the abdomen near the groins from the abdominal cavity, so the intestine descends from that rupture into one of the scrotums.
And this rupture is either from a fissure in the peritoneum or from its extension.  These two types occur from many causes, either a blow, a jump, a shout, lifting something heavy, and the like.  Its sign, if it is from the extension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs little by little over a long time.
It does not occur suddenly, and the swelling is even towards the depth because the peritoneum constricts the intestine. Its sign, if it is from a fissure in the peritoneum, is that it initially causes great pain suddenly, and the swelling appears differently under the skin nearby.
That is by the exit of the intestine and its passage outside of the peritoneum.  The omentum may exit with the intestine, and this hernia is called intestinal and omental. It may be with gas, and the stool may flow into the intestine and be retained there, resulting in the death of the patient.
Because it causes severe pain and gurgling, especially if constricted. Treatment of these types of illness with cautery is dangerous, so one should be cautious about resorting to it. The method of treatment is to instruct the patient to return the intestine into his abdomen by hand if it is possible to return it.
Then he lies on his back in your hands and raises his legs. Then you extend the skin next to the groin upwards and incise the entire skin of the scrotum lengthwise. Then you insert hooks into the edges of the incision to the extent needed to open the incision with them.
The incision should be large enough to allow the testicle to emerge. Then you dissect the membranes under the skin of the scrotum until the white, hard peritoneum is exposed on all sides. Then you insert your index finger into the area next to the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum under the skin of the testicle and the second peritoneum, and you loosen the adhesions behind the testicle. Then with your right hand you pull inwards on the skin of the scrotum, and with your left hand you extend the white peritoneum upwards, raising the testicle with the peritoneum toward the incision.
You instruct the assistant to pull the testicle upwards, and you completely loosen the adhesions from behind.  You explore with your finger lest there be any twisted intestine in the hard white peritoneum; if you find any, push it down into the abdomen.
Then you take a needle with thick thread, twisted from ten threads, and insert it at the end of the peritoneum under the scrotum skin next to the incision. Then you cut the ends of the twisted thread so that there are four threads. Then you overlap them firmly and tie them tightly to the peritoneum which, as we said, is under the scrotum skin, on two sides. Then you also twist the ends of the threads and tie them tightly so that nothing from the vessels that nourish them can reach them. Lest a hot swelling result from that, and also make a second ligature outside the first ligature, distant from it less than two fingers. After these two ligatures, leave of the peritoneum under the scrotum skin the amount of a finger bone.
Lest a hot swelling result from that, and also make a second ligature outside the first ligature, distant from it less than two fingers. After these two ligatures, leave of the peritoneum under the scrotum skin the amount of a finger bone. And you cut all the rest in a circle and remove the testicle with it. Then you make an incision at the bottom of the scrotum skin from which blood and pus flow, as we described before. Then use wool soaked in oil, and place it in the wound and use the ligature which we described.
And the white peritoneum, which we mentioned after its cutting, may be cauterized cautiously against bleeding, for that often happens.  And you leave the ligature until it falls off by itself. If it is slow to fall off, then loosen it with hot water and apply something that will make it sweat until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the rest of the treatment for wounds until it heals.
Chapter sixty-six.
Concerning the windy hernia.  I have not seen anyone dare to treat this windy hernia with iron. The ancients mentioned that it should be done as we described in the hernia with a sac, that is, that the vessels should be gently ligated below after incision.
Then you incise in the middle and treat the tumor with what opens it until the vessels fall off, and treat the wound as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter sixty-seven.
Concerning the hernia which is in the groin.
A hernia may appear in the groin, as we said, so the place swells but nothing of the intestine descends into the scrotum; and if it descends, it is slight and returns at all times.
But if time passes, the fissure in the peritoneum increases until the intestine and epiploon descend into the scrotum, and this happens from the extension of the peritoneum which is in the groin, as we said. That is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes and the place swells and protrudes, and its treatment is by cauterization, as I have previously described.
And it may be treated with an iron instrument in this manner: the patient lies on his back between your hands, then you incise the swollen, protruding hernia transversely to the extent of three fingers, then you loosen the peritoneum which is under the skin.
Until, when the white peritoneum which follows is uncovered, you take a probe and place it on the protruding part of the peritoneum and press it into the depth of the abdomen, then you sew the two protruding parts of the peritoneum to the end of the probe and attach one to the other with the suture.
Then you withdraw the probe, and do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle nor anything else, as I have taught you in the treatment of intestinal hernia, then you treat it with the treatment of wounds.
Then if the threads are cut, I clean them and dress the wound until it heals, for the peritoneum contracts and the protrusion of the hernia does not increase.
Cauterization is better in this situation because it is closer to safety.
Chapter sixty-eight.
On the relaxation of the skin of the scrotum.
The skin of the scrotum often relaxes in some people without the flesh inside relaxing, and its appearance is unsightly.  Whoever wishes to treat it should put the patient to sleep on his back.
And he cuts all the relaxed skin over the thick skin, then brings the two lips together; and if you wish, first sew the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches, and make sure of the stitching, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it with the treatment of other wounds until it heals and the stitches fall out.
Chapter sixty-nine.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
Therefore, I should not have mentioned it in this book, but I mentioned it for two reasons: one, so that it may be in the physician's knowledge if he is asked about it, and he may know the treatment of someone who suffers from it; and the other, that we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
Such as lambs, goats, cats, and the like of animals.  So I say that castration is of two types: either by crushing or by incision and cutting.  That which is done by crushing, its method is that we seat the animal in hot water until its testicles relax, soften, and hang down.
Then crush them with your hand until they dissolve and are not discernible to the touch. As for castration by incision and cutting, it is necessary that you hold the animal and squeeze the skin of its testicles with your left hand, then tie the cords and make one incision on each testicle.
Until the testicles emerge, then cut them after you have skinned them and do not leave anything of the membranes on them except the thin membrane that is on the vessels.  This type of castration is better than that which is done by crushing.
Because crushing may leave something of the testicles, so the animal desires mating. Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
In the treatment of the hermaphrodite.
The hermaphrodite in men is of two types: one is that which appears in what is behind the scrotum or in the skin of the testicle between the two testicles, a shape like a woman's vulva with hair, and urine may flow from that which is in the skin of the testicle.
As for women, there is one type, and it is above the vulva on the pubis like small male genitals, protruding outwards, one of them like a man's penis and the two like testicles. And the treatment of the three types.
The two types from men and the one type from women, it is necessary to cut away that excess flesh until its trace is removed, then treat it with the treatment of other wounds until it heals. As for the second type from men, from which urine comes forth which is in the skin of the testicle, there is no action in it nor is there any benefit from removing it at all.
Chapter seventy-one.
On the excision of the clitoris and the protruding flesh from the vulvas of women.
The clitoris may increase in size beyond the natural state, until it becomes large and unsightly, and it may grow large in some women until it is spread like that of men and leads to intercourse.
It is necessary to grasp the excess of the clitoris with your hand or with a hook and cut it, and do not go to excess in cutting, especially in the depth of the root, lest bleeding occur.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds until it heals. As for the growing flesh, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the womb until it fills it.
And sometimes it protrudes outwards like a tail. Therefore, some of the ancients call it the tail-like disease.  It should be cut as the clitoris is cut, and treated until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
In the treatment of prolapse.
Prolapse is when a woman's vulva is not perforated, or the perforation is small. This may be natural, from birth, or accidental.  The accidental kind results from a pre-existing condition, such as an excessive growth of flesh.
or a thin or thick membrane.  This may be located in the depth of the uterus, or on its sides, or above or below it. It prevents intercourse, conception, and childbirth, and may also prevent menstruation.  Its identification is done by the midwife's examination.
If the obstruction is apparent and near, but if it is not apparent, then she should examine it with her fingers or a probe. If the obstruction is a thin membrane and is close to the labia, then immediately pierce it. This is done by placing something like pledgets on the labia, then placing both thumbs of both hands.
The woman lies on her back with her legs spread. Then, she forcefully stretches the labia until she pierces the thin membrane and the obstruction opens. Then she takes wool, soaks it in oil, and places it on the area. The woman has intercourse every day, so that the area does not close again.
If the membrane is thick and dense, then it should be incised with a wide scalpel resembling an aspen leaf. If the obstruction is from an excessive growth of flesh, then hook it with hooks and cut it.  Have with you styptics that do not sting, such as acacia, shiyan, and frankincense, mixed with egg white.
Then use a wide lead tube, so that the wound does not close quickly. Keep it in place for several days and use a wick of dry linen. Then treat it with the usual treatment for healing until it is healed.  Another growth of flesh may appear in the uterus; it should be cut in the same manner.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor.  A cancerous tumor that is in the uterus should never be treated with iron.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red pustules that appear in the vulva of women.
As for hemorrhoids, it is a swelling of the mouths of the veins, so that much blood constantly flows from them. If hemorrhoids persist, they become warts.  I have already mentioned their types and signs in the classification; we will mention them here.
What accepts treatment and what does not accept treatment. I say that if hemorrhoids and warts are deep in the uterus and do not appear to the touch, then there is no treatment for them with iron.  Those that are at the mouth of the uterus and are palpable are treatable.
It is necessary that the woman enter a cool house, then stretch the warts with a scraper or a rough cloth and cut them from their roots. Then apply to them, if blood flows, one of the styptics that stop blood without causing pain.
Such as acacia,  cistus, and frankincense, and similar things. Then she should raise her legs to the wall for an hour, then lower them into cold water. If the bleeding persists, then seat her in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate peels, gallnuts, and similar things. Then take barley flour, knead it with honey and vinegar.
And apply it as a compress to her back. If the bleeding stops, otherwise, apply cupping to the loins and breasts without restriction. Then take wool, dip it in the juice of spurge, or the juice of verbascum, or the juice of the branches of  the  ivy with a gallnut solution and apply it to the place.
If the swelling subsides, then treat the area with ointment until it heals. As for the red pimple, it resembles the heads of millet, rough in appearance. It is necessary to cut what appears of it, according to what I mentioned concerning warts, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
Concerning the suppuration which occurs in the uterus.
Many types of tumors may occur in the uterus, such as cancer, the hardened tumor, ulcers, fistulas, the phagedenic ulcer,  fistulas, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and the hot tumor. I have mentioned all these diseases, their types, and their signs in the classification.
It is necessary that we mention in this chapter the hot tumor which occurs in the uterus, if it is among the tumors which collect for a time, how its suppuration is done with iron.  It is necessary to observe; if the pain of the tumor is severe in its beginning, with throbbing, inflammation and fever,
and you see it red when you touch it, do not hasten its suppuration, and treat it with what aids in ripening, until when these symptoms subside; then it is necessary that the woman sit on a chair with two seats and lean on its back.
and she gathers her legs towards the lower part of her abdomen, and her thighs are spread apart, and her arms are placed under her knees, and they are bound with a suitable bandage.
Then the midwife sits on the right side and uses the instrument with which the mouth of the uterus is opened, the image of which comes later. And it is necessary, if you wish to use this instrument, that you estimate with a probe the depth of the woman's uterus, lest what enters from the instrument be more than the depth of the uterus.
This harms the patient. If the instrument is longer than the depth, then it is necessary that pledgets be placed on the lip of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the depth of the uterus, and it is necessary that the spiral which runs in the instrument be on the upper side.
And you hold the instrument and the assistant turns the spiral until the cervix is opened.  Then, if the abscess appears and is touched by the hand and is soft and thin, then it is necessary to incise it at its head with a wide scalpel. Then, when all the pus is evacuated,
it is necessary that a soft pledget soaked in rose oil or green oil containing some astringent be placed in the wound, and the pledget should be outside the incision in the cervix; and clean wool soaked in water in which alum has been boiled should be placed externally on the uterus and on the pubis.
Then, after two days, treat with the ointment which is suitable for this until it heals.
And the uterus and the wound may be washed by injecting with water and honey, then with water in which licorice root or long rhubarb has been boiled, then return to the ointment; if the abscess is hidden inside the uterus.
Then it is necessary to refrain from treating it with iron, but it should be treated with what we mentioned in the section.
Chapter Seventy-Five.
On instructing midwives how to treat living fetuses if they emerge in an unnatural manner.
The midwife should first know the form of natural childbirth.  Among its signs, if you see the woman straining downwards and yearning to breathe air, and what she is suffering from childbirth becomes easier for her.
And the emergence of the fetus is hastened. Know that this birth is in the natural manner, and its emergence is head-first with the placenta either with it or hanging from its navel. If you see these signs, then it is necessary to press her abdomen so that the fetus emerges quickly.
For if it descends head-first, the placenta descends with it and cleanses itself well from those impurities.  Anything other than this birth is unnatural and reprehensible, for the fetus may emerge feet-first, or its hands and feet may emerge before its head.
Or one hand or one foot, or its head and hand may emerge, or it may emerge folded, and perhaps it turns over onto its back, and similar reprehensible forms.  Therefore, the midwife should be skillful and gentle with all these things and beware of mistakes and errors.
And I am explaining each form and how to deal with it so that you may recognize and understand it. If the fetus emerges head-first, the natural emergence, and this is difficult for the woman and the labor pains are severe for her, and you see that her strength has weakened, then seat her on a chair and instruct women to support her.
And apply a poultice of cooked fenugreek and moist ointments to her womb. Then the midwife inserts a small scalpel between her fingers and incises the placenta, or incises it with her fingernail, until the moisture within flows out, and she presses the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then she should inject the woman with fenugreek saliva mixed with shirāj ointment. Then, after the injection, she should instruct her to strain and make her sneeze with a sternutatory and hold her mouth and nose, for the fetus will emerge immediately. If the hands of the fetus emerge first, then they should be gently returned little by little.
If it is not possible to insert [the hand], then place the woman on a stool and raise her legs upwards, then shake the stool on the ground while the woman holds on so that she does not fall when it is shaken. If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, then cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them moderately, for then it will come out.  The emergence of the fetus with its feet: if the fetus emerges with its feet, then they should be pushed upwards, and then the fetus should be turned little by little until it is straightened, then take one of its legs and extend it little by little.
When they emerge to the thighs, instruct her to strain and make her sneeze with a sternutatory, for then it will come out. If it does not come out as we have described, otherwise, return the fetus little by little until it is in its natural position, for then it will emerge smoothly. If it is prevented by all that we have described.
Take some henbane saliva, fenugreek saliva, shirāj ointment, and dissolved gum arabic, and pound everything well in a mortar. Then apply it to the vulva of the woman and the lower part of her abdomen, then seat her in lukewarm water until it reaches the sharasif.
If you see that her lower parts have softened, make a pessary for her from bitter aloes and let her retain it.
