When the pessary has been retained for an hour, seat her on a chair, then make her sneeze, closing her mouth and nose, and gently squeeze her lower abdomen; then the fetus will come out immediately. The emergence of the fetus on its knees and hands.
If it emerges in this manner, carefully insert your hands little by little; then the woman should lie on her back, hanging her loins downwards while lying on a bed, and she should clasp her hands; then gently press above her abdomen. Then remove the cushions from under her legs so that her lower parts are elevated. If the fetus does not emerge, take both her legs and move them vigorously, then gently press above the perineum until the fetus ascends upwards. Then the midwife inserts her hand and gradually adjusts the fetus.
And instruct the woman to strain until the fetus emerges.  The emergence of the fetus crosswise, hanging by one of its hands. Carefully try to return its hand; if you cannot return it, make the woman stand and walk. If she cannot walk, let her lie down.
on her bed and shake her legs vigorously. Then use the pessary that I described from the ingredients. If labor pains seize her, seat her on a chair, then treat the returning of the hand and adjusting the fetus into the natural position, and make the woman sneeze and instruct her to strain until it emerges.
The emergence of the fetus on its back, extending its hands, and its face towards its mother's back. The midwife should grasp the hand of the fetus, then turn it little by little and gently adjust it. Once it is straight, apply what we described of the ointment and pessaries to the vulva.
Then tell her to move slightly. If she does this, seat her on the chair and tell her to lean to her right side. Then tell her to strain and make her sneeze with a sternutatory; then it will emerge. The emergence of the fetus upright on its side.
If it descends to the mouth of the womb in this manner with the placenta, tear the placenta with your fingernail until it is free of moisture. If it adheres to the face and neck of the fetus, cut it from the umbilical cord lest it suffocate and die, then push the fetus inwards until it is as it should be.
And instruct the woman to strain repeatedly; then it will emerge easily. The emergence of twins or many fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and three or four may be born and live, although this is rare. As for five, it is something outside of nature; they will not live at all.
And they are surrounded, even if numerous, by one placenta, and they are separated by a membrane barrier between them, connected to the umbilical cord of each one. They may emerge like a single fetus, naturally and easily, or they may emerge in a difficult manner as we described.
If some of them are difficult at birth and become as we have described in unnatural forms, treat them as we have described. Let the midwife be gentle and do what she does with gentleness and patience, and let her devise a solution for every situation with whatever means she can find that leads to safety.
Mention of what is conceived in the womb of fetuses that are aborted. One, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten may be conceived in the womb. It has been confirmed to me that a woman aborted seven, and another fifteen, all conceived.
By the will of the Almighty and Knowing. All of this should be in the knowledge of the midwife, for perhaps such a thing may occur to her one day, so that she may meet it as is appropriate.
Chapter seventy-six.
In the extraction of the dead fetus.
If you treat the dead fetus with what I mentioned in the section on treatment, and it does not come out, and you are compelled to use your hand, then you should look: if the woman is healthy and strong, and has none of the symptoms that one fears might cause her death, then the woman should lie on her back on a bed.
And her head should be tilted downwards and her legs raised, and a servant should hold her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed, lest her body be pulled when the fetus is drawn out.
Then her vulva should be moistened with moist ointments, along with the saliva of the dog and fenugreek and flaxseed. Then the midwife should anoint her hand with these ointments and saliva, and then gently insert it into the womb; then she should seek with it a place to insert the hooks into the fetus, and the suitable locations for that.
Look: if the fetus is positioned to descend headfirst, then insert the hooks into its eyes, or into the nape of its neck, or into its mouth, or into its palate, or under its chin or clavicle, or into the areas near the ribs, or under the scapulae.
But if its descent is feet first, then [insert the hooks] into the groin and the middle ribs.  It is necessary to hold the hook with the right hand, and to place its bend between the fingers of the left hand, and to insert the hand gently and insert a hook into some of those locations that we have mentioned.
Until you reach something empty, then insert another hook opposite it, or a third if needed, so that the traction is even and does not lean to one side. Then pull with a steady pull; and the pull should not be straight only, but should be moved in every direction.
So that its exit is facilitated, as is done with a molar when it is extracted.  It is necessary, in the meantime, to relax the pull; if it is obstructed from one side, then the midwife should anoint some of her fingers with ointment and insert them from that side to rotate what is obstructed. If part of the fetus emerges, then move the hooks to other locations.
She should do this until the entire fetus emerges. If a hand emerges before anything else and cannot be returned because of its compression, then it is necessary to wrap a cloth around it and then pull it until, when it is entirely emerged, it is cut.
Either at the joint of the wrist or at the elbow.  And thus she should do with the other hand and with the feet. If the head of the fetus is large and experiences difficulty in its exit, or if there is collected water in its head, then it is necessary to insert a hooked scalpel between the fingers.
And to incise the head with it so that the water emerges, or to extract it with the instrument called the *mishdakh*, the image of which comes with the images of the other instruments in the chapter which follows this.
She should do likewise if the fetus is naturally large-headed. Then it is necessary to incise the skull or crush it with the *mishdakh*, as we have said, then to extract the bones with forceps. If the head emerges and is compressed at the clavicle, then let it be incised until the moisture in the chest flows out.
For the chest then constricts. If the chest does not constrict and its emergence is not achieved, then cut as much as you can. If the lower abdomen is swollen or if it has a blister, then it is necessary to puncture it so that the moisture flows from it.
If the fetus is on its feet, then pulling it is easy and straightening it to the mouth of the womb is simple. If it is compressed at the abdomen or chest, then it is necessary to pull it with a cloth that you have wrapped around your hand and incise the abdomen or chest until what is in them flows out.
If all the limbs are extracted and the head is retracted and retained, let the midwife insert her left hand. If the mouth of the womb is open, then she inserts her hand into the neck of the womb and seeks the head with it and pulls it with her fingers to the mouth of the womb, then inserts one or two hooks into it and pulls it with them.
If the mouth of the womb has constricted due to a hot swelling affecting it, then it is not necessary to be forceful with it, but it is necessary then to use the pouring of oily, moist things and to use compresses and to sit in waters that relax and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, if it is possible to straighten it, then let what we mentioned for the living fetus be used. If that is not possible, then let it be cut and removed. It is necessary not to leave anything of the afterbirth inside at all. When you have finished your treatment, use what we mentioned of the treatment for the hot swellings that appear in the wombs.
If bleeding occurs, then let her descend into astringent waters and use all other treatments.  And I have witnessed a woman who had conceived, and the fetus died in her womb, then she conceived again upon it.
Then the other fetus also died.  After a long time, a swelling appeared in her uterus and it swelled until it opened and began to discharge pus.  I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, but the wound did not close, so I placed on it some strong, drawing ointments.
Then a bone came out of the place. Then days passed, and another bone came out. I was amazed by this, since the abdomen is a place where there is no bone. I estimated that it was from the bones of the dead fetus, so I examined the wound and took out many bones from the bones of the fetus's head.
I continued doing this until I removed many bones from her, and the woman was in the best of health.  She lived for a time afterward, with a little pus issuing from the place. And I have only mentioned this rarity here.
Because in it there is knowledge and help for what the skillful physician attempts with his hand in treatment.
Chapter seventy-seven.
In it are the images of the instruments which are needed in removing the fetus.
Image of a screw to open the mouth of the womb.
This is the binder with which books are bound; it has two screws at the ends of the two pieces of wood, except that this screw should be finer than the binder, and should be either of ebony or of boxwood, and the width of each piece of wood should be the thickness of two fingers.
And their width is about a finger, and their length is a span and a half, and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two guides of the same material as the wood itself, which are fastened in them; their length is half a span or a little more, and their width is about two fingers or a little more, and these two guides…
are those which enter the mouth of the womb to open it by turning the screws.  Image of another instrument for the same purpose, finer and lighter.
It is made of ebony or boxwood in the shape of hooks, except that it has two guides at its end, as you see; the length of each guide is about a span, and their width is two fingers.  If you wish to open the womb with it,
then seat the woman on a bed with her legs spread apart between her thighs, then insert these two guides, close together, into the mouth of the womb, while you hold the end of the instrument below between her thighs, then open your hand with the instrument as you do with the hooks, to whatever extent you want to open the womb. until the midwife does what she wants. Image of another screw mentioned by the ancients.
Image of the instrument which pushes the fetus.
Image of the forceps with which the head of the fetus is grasped.
It resembles scissors, it has teeth at the end as you see, and it may be made rectangular like hooks, in this image as you see, it has teeth like the teeth of a saw, it cuts with them and tears.
Image of a pusher also.
Image of a hook.
This hook should be somewhat thick so that it does not break when the fetus is pulled with it. Image of a hook with two barbs.
Image of two wide scalpels for cutting the fetus.
All these instruments, the more numerous their types and the better prepared they are by the craftsman, the quicker his work and the higher his reputation among people. Therefore, do not despise any instrument that you have prepared, for there will inevitably be a need for it.
Chapter seventy-eight.
On the removal of the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, you should instruct the patient to hold herself, then cause her to sneeze with a [nasal] catheter, and press your hand on her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out in this way, well and good; otherwise, take measures.
And pierce a hole in the lid and place in it herbs that open the mouth of the womb, such as rue, henbane, dill, chamomile, wormwood,  fumitory, and centaury—all of these medicaments or some of them—and immerse them in water, and place them on the fire. Then place a silver tube on the hole in the lid of the pot, and the other end in the mouth of the womb.
And hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the womb, then cause her to sneeze as we said. Then the placenta will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to dip her left hand in  oil of ceruse or mallow juice.
Then she inserts it into the vagina and searches for the placenta. If she finds it, she grasps it and pulls it out little by little. If it is attached deep in the womb, insert the hand as we described, until, when you find the placenta, you pull it out little by little, carefully.
Lest the womb fall out with strong pulling, but it should be moved gently to the sides, right and left, then the amount of pulling is increased. Then it will yield and become free from adhesion. If the mouth of the womb is constricted, we have described treatment with sneezing and treatment with the pot and herbs.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, avoid violence upon it in pulling again, but it is necessary to tie what has come out of it to the mouth of the womb to the thigh of the woman, then inject her with the tetrapharmacon ointment, for it will cause it to rot after a few days.
And it will dissolve and come out, except that if it becomes putrid, a bad smell will rise from it to the stomach and head, and that will harm the patient.  Therefore, suitable fumigation should be used.
Therefore, some of the ancients have tried the fumigation of rue and dried figs.  The image of the instrument with which the woman is fumigated when menstruation and the placenta are retained, and so on:
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or it is made of copper. The thin end is placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire, and the incense is placed on the embers. She holds it until that incense is gone and replaces it with more.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
In the treatment of the non-perforated fistula.
Many boys are born with their rectums imperforate; a thin membrane has closed it.  The midwife should perforate this membrane with her finger; otherwise, let her incise it with a sharp scalpel, being careful not to touch the muscle.
Then she places wool soaked in syrup and oil upon it, then treats it with ointments until it heals. If she fears it will close up, she places a lead tube in the opening for many days, removing it whenever the child wants to defecate.
The rectum may also become obstructed by the healing of a wound or a tumor.  This adhesion should be incised, then treated as described above, with medication and the lead tube.
Chapter Eighty.
On the treatment of fistulas which occur in the lower area.
Fistulas which occur in the lower area are a complication and thickening which occurs near the anus externally or internally, from one side or another. The fistula may be single or multiple.
If this complication becomes chronic, it opens and a watery white fluid or thin pus drains from it. Some of these fistulas may penetrate to the bladder and the intestine, while others do not.  Penetrating fistulas can be identified by the passage of feces and flatus from them when the patient defecates.
Worms may also emerge from them.  There may be fistulas, if they are internal, penetrating to the bladder and the urethra; and there may be some penetrating to the hip joint and the sacrum.  A fistula penetrating to the rectum is also distinguishable from one that does not.
Insert your index finger into the rectum and introduce a thin probe into the fistula, made of copper or iron, if the fistula is not tortuous. If it is tortuous, introduce a thin lead probe or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or hair with your finger. If you feel nothing at all, and nothing like feces, flatus, or worms emerges from the opening as we have said, then know that it is not penetrating, and proceed to treatment.
If the fistula penetrates to the bladder or urethra, the indication is the passage of urine from it and the failure of the site to heal with medication.  If it penetrates to the hip joint or sacrum, the sign is the probe reaching that location.
If there is no tortuosity to the bone, and pain is present in that region, and pus constantly emerges from it, and treatment is ineffective and it does not heal with ointment—all these penetrating fistulas—there is no cure for them at all.
And their treatment is a futile effort for those ignorant physicians who attempt them. As for those that are not penetrating and are not chronic, recovery is hoped for with cautery, as I describe and have tested.  The patient should lie on their back between your hands.
Raise his legs and tilt his thighs towards his abdomen. Then insert the copper or lead probe, if the fistula is tortuous, until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it towards the rectum, you should insert your index finger into the rectum.
If you feel the probe with your finger, clearly exposed, without feeling any membrane or flesh between your finger and it, then know for certain that it is penetrating, so do not tire yourself with it, for there is no cure for it, as we said.
It has been said that it heals in some people rarely. Of the treatments which hope for benefit is to heat a thin cautery iron according to the size of the fistula, as previously explained, and insert it, hot, into the fistula until it reaches towards the rectum, then repeat this two or three times.
Until you know that all that excess, matted flesh resembling a bird's quill has been burned. Then treat it with a pledget soaked in butter, until the burned flesh is removed, then treat it with healing ointments; if it heals.
Otherwise, it will never heal with any other treatment. But if you insert the probe and it does not reach your finger which is in the rectum, and there was a thick partition of flesh or membrane between it and the probe, and you see the fistula near the surface of the skin, then incise the skin at the beginning of the fistula. And as you pass with the incision along with the probe, which is in the fistula, until you reach with the incision where the end of the probe has reached, and the probe is released and falls out. Then cleanse that matted flesh resembling a bird's quill, so that nothing remains of it.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If blood overwhelms you and prevents you from cutting that flesh, then the best and quickest remedy is cauterization with fire or a strong medicine, because cauterization with fire combines two good things: burning that excess flesh.
And cutting the blood and drying the moisture. Then treat it with pledgets soaked in butter or sulfur powdered with oil, until the place suppurates and that burned flesh comes out in the pus. Then treat the wound with healing ointments that grow firm flesh.
These are medicines which constrict and dry, for when it is closed, it is healed and there is no fear of recurrence.  If the fistula has reached the depth of the rectum and is far from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the rectum and explore with it.
If you feel the probe and between you and it a membrane of peritoneum or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then the action in it is only by way of hope, and that is to use one of three methods: either to cauterize it as we said.
Or to incise it until you reach its bottom so that you can easily insert pledgets and treat from near, and do not extend the incision lest you cut the muscle surrounding the rectum, so that involuntary bowel movements occur in the patient. Then treat it as we mentioned; perhaps it will heal as we said.
Or to penetrate that membrane and pierce it after incising it near the rectum, as we said, either with a probe or with another sharp-pointed instrument. Then clean out all the hardened flesh in the incision as far as you can, thoroughly.
Then suture the whole wound, including the incision and the upper opening, until it closes, and the fistula remains open inside the rectum, which is easier for the patient. And the fistula may be perforated in this manner.
And if you insert the probe into the fistula and it is on the side of the rectum toward the surface of the body with the skin and the circumference of the rectum, then take a probe with a perforated tip like a shoemaker's needle, in this manner:
And insert a twisted thread of five or so threads into it, then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its bottom. If it is penetrating into the margin of the rectum from inside, nearby.
Then bring the thread out through that hole by inserting your finger into the rectum and bringing out the end of the thread, and bring both ends together and tighten them, and leave it for a day or two.  Whenever the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh between the ends of the threads is severed and falls away.  Then treat the wound until it heals and recovers.
If the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, penetrate it however you can, except that it should not be too deep, for you should not do that because of the muscle, lest you cut it. Then proceed as we have mentioned until it heals.
The image of the hooked scalpel with which fistulas are incised: the hook is very sharp, and the other side is not sharp, lest you cut what is not needed, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-One.
Concerning the opening of hemorrhoids from which blood flows, their excision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: either they are inside the rectum, resembling red bladders or like grapes, and there are small and large ones, and blood constantly flows from them.
And they are single or numerous, and are outside the rectum and at its edges, except that those which are outside the rectum are mostly less moist, with yellow water or a little blood constantly flowing from them, and they are the color of the flesh.
The treatment of those inside the rectum is that you order the patient to strain and bear down until the rectum protrudes and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you quickly grasp them with hooks or seize them with your fingernail and cut them at their bases; if the hooks do not hold because of their moisture and laxity,
then roughen them with a coarse cloth or a piece of rough sponge, or pull them with your fingers, then cut them. Then, after cutting, sprinkle some sharp powders on them to act in place of cautery, or cauterize them as described in the chapter on cautery.
Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals. If the rectum does not come out, inject the patient with an enema containing a little irritant to wash what is in the rectum and cause it to come out quickly when the patient strains. As for the hemorrhoids outside the rectum, their treatment is easy and simple.
That is, you grasp them with your fingernail or hook them with a hook and cut them, then treat them as we have mentioned until they heal. And whoever dislikes cutting with iron, it is necessary that he use ligature in this way: take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the hemorrhoid upwards and pass the needle through its base from the other side, and wrap the ends of the thread below the needle while it is crosswise, and pull the hemorrhoid tightly, then tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this with all the hemorrhoids, and leave one without piercing it.
so that the excess blood will flow from them. Then place a cloth soaked in rose oil or kyrioti oil on the rectum and tell the patient to be still, then leave him until they fall off. When the hemorrhoids fall off, treat them with ointments and the rest of the treatment until they heal.
As for the treatment of fissures, they often occur from the dryness of the stool and the retention of the temperament. If they become chronic and medicine does not work, then it is necessary to scrape it with the blade of a scalpel or with your fingernail until it becomes moist and the upper layer, which prevents its healing, is removed.
Then treat it until it heals as it should. If it does not heal, repeat the scraping more severely than the first time until it bleeds well and is skinned, then treat it, for it will heal.
Chapter eighty-two.
On the treatment of ingrown and non-ingrown nails, dry warts, and the ant.
A nail is a round nodule, the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail. It occurs on the whole body, especially on the soles of the feet and the toes, and causes pain when walking.
It is necessary to incise around the nail, grasp it with a hook or forceps, and pull it out from its root, then treat it. If you wish, cauterize it as previously described in its chapter. As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, matted, round protrusion on the surface of the body.
They frequently appear on the hands of children.  Its treatment is to stretch the wart and cut it, or pierce it with a silk thread or a hair until it falls off.  If you wish, cauterize it as previously described with fire or a caustic medicine.
Ingrown and non-ingrown warts may be removed with the instrument I have described in the treatment of the ant. As for the ant [wart], it is also a small, matted, thick protrusion on the surface of the body, extending very deep; and if it is exposed to cold, one feels a sensation similar to the stinging of ants.
It occurs throughout the body, but most often on the hands.  Its treatment is to take a tube made of a strong goose feather or eagle feather, and place this tube over the ant [wart] so that the circle of the tube surrounds it on all sides; then rotate your hand with the tube until you incise around the ant [wart].
Then push it into the depth of the flesh, and the ant [wart] will be removed from its root. If you wish, do this with a copper or iron tube in this manner:
The upper part of the tube should be solid and twisted to make it easier for the fingers to grasp and twist it. If you wish, cauterize the ant [wart] as previously described in the cauterization of warts in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
On the shapes of instruments used for injections in rectal ailments, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe may be made of silver, china, or cast or beaten copper.  These instruments may be made small or large, according to the users. Those used to treat young children should be small, and those whose rectums are narrow or painful should have very gentle syringes.  Image of a large syringe:
Its upper funnel, to which the bladder is attached, is wide in this manner; it has a stopper where the bladder is tied, as you see.  Its lower end, which is inserted into the rectum, is solid, very smooth, and slender; on one side are two holes, and on the other, one hole.
The size of the hole should be the thickness of an awl, or slightly larger. The bladder, in which the medicine is placed, should be made from an animal bladder or sheepskin, fashioned like a small bag, its diameter being one cubit.
It is pierced with many holes in a circular pattern, the distance between each hole being the thickness of a finger. Then, a strong thread, made of twisted yarn of ten or so strands, is inserted into these holes, and the bladder is gathered together like a bag. Then, when the medicine is placed inside, it is tightly tied at the head of the syringe between two stones, and then it is injected.  This is an image of the circle of the bladder and the hole:
The method of injection is to fill the skin or bladder with warmed medicine, to a degree the patient can tolerate. Be careful that it is not excessively hot or cold. Then the patient lies on his back and raises his legs.
A mat or blanket is placed under him. Then the tip of the syringe is lubricated with oil, egg white, fenugreek saliva, or something similar. Then it is gently inserted into the rectum. Then an assistant takes the bag in both hands and squeezes it strongly until everything in the syringe is emptied into the intestines.
Then the syringe is removed, and the patient raises his legs to the wall. If the medicine stimulates evacuation, let him restrain himself as much as he can. If he sleeps all night with it, it is more effective. If it is injected for diarrhea and evacuation is hastened, repeat the injection until he recovers.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the rectum, the syringe should be very gentle and smooth, smoother than those used to inject children. This is an image of a gentle syringe with its skin stretched over it:
Chapter Eighty-Four.
In the treatment of wounds.
I mentioned in the classification the treatment of things similar to cauterizations, and I will mention here their treatment by hand and by medication completely. I say that wounds differ according to the thing which causes the wound and according to the location where the wound occurs.
The things which cause wounds are many, like the blow of a stone, or a cut from a sword or knife, or a stab from a lance or stick or arrow, and so on from many things.
As for wounds according to locations on the body, like a wound which occurs on the head, or neck, or chest, or abdomen, or liver, and similar organs.
And I am describing the treatment of some wounds to make them a measure and rule for all wounds, and I begin with simple head wounds especially, because the complex ones will be mentioned in the beginning of the third chapter of this book.
So I say that when a simple wound occurs in the head and there is no fracture of the bone, I look; and if it is from a blow of a stone or the like, and only the skin has been split, and the wound is large and I fear for the patient the occurrence of hot inflammation, then I hasten and incise the cephalic vein appropriately. And do not delay that, and let blood be drawn from him according to his strength, if nothing prevents bloodletting, especially if the blood which comes out of the wound is little; and let the bloodletting be from the opposite side to the injured part.
And warn the patient against fullness, and thick drinks and foods; and if something prevents his bloodletting, let him reduce food and not approach meat or drinks.  And apply to the wound, if hot inflammation occurs in it, cotton soaked in rose oil alone or with a styptic drink.
And if you are safe from hot inflammation, apply to the wound, if it is fresh and the air has not changed it, the vulnerary which I describe to you shortly; but if the air has changed it, apply to it some ointments.
Until pus is formed, then treat it until it heals. And if the wound is large, and is from a cut of a sword or the like, and its edges do not come together by themselves, then join it by suturing, as I describe in the suturing of abdominal wounds.
If the skin exposes the bone and hangs down, and its adhesion is only in a small part, cut it off, then treat the wound with medicines which produce firm flesh instead of the skin, and order the patient to nourish himself with foods containing strength, such as heads and brains and the like.
If gangrene occurs in the skin and does not adhere to the bone, cut away all that is gangrenous and throw it away, then treat it.  If in these wounds there is an artery or vein bleeding blood, and the bleeding does not stop with medicines, search for the artery.
If you find it and it is not severed, sever it with the scalpel and tie it. And if necessity demands, if what we have mentioned is not effective, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and superficial, their treatment is easy and simple; it suffices to treat them by sprinkling on them this vulnerary while they are still bleeding.
Before the air changes them. The preparation of the vulnerary is: take one part frankincense, two parts of acacia, and three parts of slaked or unslaked lime; crush all together, sift it, and fill the wound with it, and bind it tightly so that it adheres well.
It will coagulate with the blood; then bandage it from above with bandages and leave it; do not unbind it as long as it does not swell, for the medicine will not come off until it heals.  Lime alone may do this if you do not have frankincense and acacia.
This action may also be done with alum in many large wounds if its application and binding are properly done, and the wounds are fresh and bleeding, provided the air has not changed them; but if the air has changed them somewhat, especially in summer,
then there is no point in applying this vulnerary; treat it as mentioned, by applying some ripening ointments, or apply a poultice of barley flour made with water and honey until the pus is drawn out, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
But if a fracture of the bone occurs with the wound, and it is slight, pull it with a splint.  I have described the treatment of fractures of the head in what follows, in the surgery of the neck; if the wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between it and a wound of the head if it is simple.
But if a nerve or artery of the neck has been severed, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treating it with things that contract strongly, such as verdigris and vitriol and the like, for they harm the nerve and cause it to spasm.
And nothing cold at all should be placed on the wound because the essence of the nerve is cold and its connection is to the brain, which is the noblest of the organs; and what you treat it with should be soft medicines, such as natron washed with fresh water several times, moistened with oil or rose oil and tutty.
If it is also washed with fresh water and other mineral stones in this manner and moist ointments. But if the wound is large, use sutures or approximate its lips with pledgets, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has a depth and a cavity forms in its bottom where pus collects, lance it in the lowest place in it. If an artery has been severed in the wound and you observe its blood flowing, ligate it or cauterize it or tie it off if necessity demands it.
If the wound has severed some or all of the cartilages of the larynx and the windpipe remains intact, approximate the edges of the skin by sutures over the windpipe, but do not touch the windpipe itself; rather, smooth it and restore it to its natural shape, then tighten it firmly and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with ointments which have astringent and drying properties until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, examine it; if there are bone splinters in it, extract them all, or as many as you can; and what you cannot remove, leave until the wound becomes foul and heals, and their removal becomes easy.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle upon it the powders we have described. If a bone becomes foul in the wound and suppurates, treat it with what will be mentioned in its chapter, in the surgery of the chest and between the shoulders.
If it is a wound from a spear or knife and you see it has depth, look; if air escapes from it when the patient breathes, know that it is a mortal wound. If it does not have depth and is fresh with its blood, do not at first apply powders to it, nor bind it tightly, lest the blood be retained in its depth.
Then it will return to the heart and kill the patient. But place on it an attracting ointment and dress it twice a day. If you do not have an ointment, place in the mouth of the wound old lint to absorb the moisture which comes from it, and place the patient to sleep on the wound.
so that what collects in it may flow out. And if three days or more have passed since the infliction of the wound, and the patient has not experienced convulsions, or bad palpitations, or shortness of breath, and you see all his conditions are good, know that the wound is healthy, so treat it with cautery and other treatment until it heals.
If it is difficult to see it and the discharge is always continuous, know that it has become suppurating, so treat it in its chapter; the patient on the wound, so that what collects in it may flow out, and if the wound is in the chest or back, treat it as previously described with stitching.
If it is large, with powders; if it is small, if it has affected the bone and broken off pieces from it, explore the wound and proceed to remove those pieces if they are clean; if they are not clean, leave them until...
the wound becomes putrid, for then it is easy to remove them. As for the other wounds occurring in the other members, their treatment is governed by what we have mentioned.
Chapter eighty-five.
On wounds of the abdomen, the protrusion of the intestines, and their suturing.
The wound which occurs in the abdomen may be large, or it may be small or medium.  The large one may have one or several intestines protruding from it, and their replacement and suturing will be more difficult and arduous.
A small wound may also be difficult in another way, and its replacement may be more difficult. If the intestine is not immediately replaced in its place, it swells and thickens, making its replacement more difficult. For this reason, the medium-sized wound is preferable, because the replacement of the intestine is not as difficult with it as it is in these two types.
Know that the suturing of the abdomen is of four types: two are general, suitable for suturing the abdomen and for suturing other wounds of the body; and the other two are specific, applicable to other wounds, but they are most especially for the suturing of the abdomen.
And I will describe them to you one by one with explanation and clarification. I say that if the wound is small and a part of the intestine protrudes from it and its replacement is difficult, this is for one of two reasons: either because of the smallness of the wound, as we said, or because the intestine has been swollen by the cold air.
If this is the case, then it should be warmed by dipping a sponge or a damp cloth in lukewarm water alone, or in water in which anise, rue, and spikenard have been cooked, and wiping the intestine with it until the swelling resolves.
A medicament with astringent properties may also do this, and it is better than water alone in resolving the swelling.  When you see the swelling has resolved, moisten the intestine with water in which alkanet or madder has been cooked, for it makes its replacement easier.
If its return is still difficult after this treatment, then enlarge the wound slightly with the instrument used to open fistulas.  The first treatment, if successful, is better than incision. Incision is only resorted to after necessity and the failure of other methods. This is the image of the instrument:
One side of it is curved and limited, the other side is unlimited. The thin end is not as sharp as a lancet, but somewhat blunter. It is an instrument resembling a small forceps, as you see. When the wound has been widened and the intestine returned, it should be replaced in its natural form.
And its proper place, if you can manage it, is best. But if the wound is wide and located in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on his back with his legs raised higher than his head; if it is in the upper abdomen, his head and chest should be raised higher than his lower body.
Similarly, if the wound is on one side of the abdomen, always keep the side where the surgery is performed higher than the other side.  This should be done in major and moderate surgeries.
As for minor surgeries, position the patient as you are able. Then have a skilled assistant at hand, holding the entire wound with both hands, bringing its edges together, and then revealing it to the suture operator bit by bit.
This is the description of a single general suture: take one needle, or several needles, according to the size of the wound. Then leave a space at the end of the wound about the thickness of the little finger and insert a single needle without thread into the edges of the skin. with the edges of the peritoneum under the skin from the inside, until you pass it through from that side.  Having brought together the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, forming four layers, then tie a doubled thread around the needle several times on both sides until the edges of the wound are firmly joined.
Then leave a space the thickness of a finger also, and insert another needle, then fasten it with thread as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this with as many needles as needed until you have completed the entire circumference of the wound. Let the needles be of moderate thickness.
For very thin needles quickly cut the flesh, and thick ones are difficult to insert into the skin. Therefore, they should be of moderate thickness, and the needle should not be inserted into the edge of the skin too deeply.
lest the flesh be quickly severed and the wound open before it heals; nor should the stitches be too far apart, lest the wound fail to unite. Then cut off the ends of the needles lest they injure the patient when he sleeps, and make for him pads of linen cloth on each side to hold the ends of the needles.
And leave them until you know that the wound has healed. This type of stitching with needles is best suited for small wounds, because one or two needles, or so, may suffice for suturing them.
As for the method of suturing the second type, that is, the common method, it is to bring together the four edges, namely the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, at once with a needle threaded with a twisted thread of moderate thickness.  Then, when you have passed the needle through these four edges, return the needle from the side where you began.
So that the thread lies interwoven from the top of the wound, so that the stitching is like the stitching of a purse with which goods are tied.  And make the space between each stitch the width of the little finger. This stitching is known to all people.
With this stitching, a wound was sutured which appeared on the abdomen of a man who had been wounded by a knife. The wound was more than a span in length, and about two spans of the small intestine had protruded. The wound was in the middle of the abdomen.
I replaced it after its intestine had remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours. The wound healed in about fifteen days, and I treated him until he recovered. He then lived for many years, managing all his affairs, although the physicians had judged that he would never recover.
And it is remarkable that I did not treat him with ointment, because I was in a place where there were no medicines. I used to apply old cotton wool to the wound twice a day, and I would wash it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the two types of special stitching, I mention it according to Galen's words: you begin the stitching from the skin, and insert the needle from the outside to the inside.  When the needle has passed through the skin and the muscle, proceeding straight along the whole length of the abdomen…
…you leave the edge of the peritoneum on the side where you inserted the needle, and you pass the needle through its other edge from the inside to the outside, into the other edge of the peritoneum. When you have passed it through, pass it again through this same edge of the peritoneum from the outside to the inside.
And leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side, and pass the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, and as you pass it through, pass it into the edge of the peritoneum on its side, until you have passed it through completely.
Then begin again from this same side, and sew it together with the edge of the peritoneum on the other side, and bring the needle out of the skin near it. Then return it into that skin and sew the edge of the peritoneum on the other side together with this edge of the peritoneum.
Bring it out from the skin on its side, and do that again, and do it repeatedly until you have sewn up the whole wound in that way. The general description of this stitch is that it is like sewing furs together.
That you sew the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin, and leave it once from the other side until you finish. As for the second type of special stitch which Galen also mentioned, this is his exact statement:
That you sew it in the way that some physicians sew it, by bringing each part to its natural counterpart, and joining the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge, and the edge of the muscle to its other edge; and that is as I describe to you.
You should insert the needle into the edge of the muscle nearest to you from the outside, and pass it to the inside in it alone, and leave the edges of the peritoneum. Then return the needle and pass it from outside to inside in both edges of the peritoneum, then return it again.
And pass it from inside to outside in the edge of the other muscle which is on the opposite side. This type of stitch is better than the easy common stitch, which is the stitch mentioned previously. This is Galen's exact words. Some have said that the suturing of the abdomen may be done with two needles. That is, you insert one thread into them both, and begin by inserting the needle from your side and passing it to the other side, and insert the other needle from the opposite side, and pass it from your side, according to the seamstresses' stitching exactly.
Know that if the wound is in the middle of the abdomen, then its suturing is more difficult than in other parts of the abdomen. As for the treatment of the wound, its treatment is the same as for other wounds. That is, if you find the wound fresh with its blood before the air changes it, and you return the intestines and sew it and secure it, then apply the medicated lint to it.
If the wound has been altered by the air, then apply some of the ointments that you prepare until it suppurates and the threads fall out and the peritoneum and muscle unite, then treat it as you treat other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have medicines, apply to it from the time it begins to suppurate, used cotton, and change it twice a day as I have taught you, until it heals. For you do not need other treatment in most cases if the wounds are simple.  If you fear that the wound will affect the principal organs with pain,
Then it is necessary to dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and armpit. If he feels pain or putrefaction in his intestines – and this often occurs – then inject him with a lukewarm astringent black drink.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and become a penetrating wound into his body. And know that what is thick from the intestines is easier to heal, and what is thin from them is more difficult to heal.
And as for the intestine known as the jejunum, it does not accept healing from a wound that occurs to it at all, because of the abundance of its veins and their size, and the thinness of its substance and its proximity to the nature of the nerve. And as for if what emerges from the wound is omentum and you find it soft, then replace it according to your replacement of the intestine, equally.
If a period of time passes and it has turned green or black, then it is necessary to tie it with a thread above the place which has blackened, lest bleeding occur, for in the omentum are veins and arteries, then cut below that ligature.
And make the ends of the thread hanging from below the wound, outside of it, so that it is easy for you to pull it and remove it when the omentum falls off and the wound suppurates.  Mention of the wound which occurs in the intestine: And as for if a rupture occurs in the intestine and it is small…
Then it is possible that it will heal in some people, because I saw a man who had been wounded in his abdomen by a spear thrust, and the wound was to the right of the stomach, and the wound became chronic and became a fistula from which feces and wind emerged. So I treated him although I did not hope for his healing. So I continued to treat him gently until he healed and the place joined.  When I saw that the place had joined, I feared for the patient that a bad incident might occur to him in his abdomen, but no bad incident occurred to him at all and he remained in the best of his condition. Correctly he eats and drinks and copulates and enters the bathhouse and exerts himself in his service. And some experienced people have mentioned that whenever a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be sutured in this manner.
And it is that large-headed ants are taken, then the edges of the wound are brought together, then one of them is placed, its mouth open, on the edges of the wound. When it has seized and tightened its mouth, its head is cut off, for it will adhere and not become undone, then another ant is placed near the first.
And you continue to do that with several ants according to the size of the wound, then you return it and suture the wound, for those heads remain adhering in the intestine until the intestine heals and the patient is not harmed at all.  And it is possible that the intestine may also be sutured with fine thread.
That which is drawn from the intestine of the animal adhering to it after it has been entered into a needle is that the end of this thread from the intestine is taken, then it is smoothed, then a fine twisted linen thread is tied to its end, then that thread is entered into the needle. And in it is the intestine thread, and with it the intestine is sutured, then it is returned to the abdomen. And this type of suturing with ants and intestine is only by way of hope and expectation.
But if the perforation is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, then there is no remedy and no healing at all.
Chapter Eighty-Six.
In the treatment of the cold and fistula.
Know that every wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic and longstanding and becomes an ulcer and does not heal, and constantly discharges pus that does not stop, is generally called, in whatever organ it may be, a fistula; and we call it a cold, and a fistula.
It is in reality a complex, adherent, hard, white mass with no pain, having a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some have called it a feather; and it is sometimes moist, constantly discharging pus, and sometimes the moisture is interrupted; and this moisture may be much or little, thick or thin.
And I have already mentioned in the classification, when discussing wounds, that every ulcer that does not heal and in which flesh does not grow, is due to one of nine reasons: one of them is either due to a deficiency of blood in the body, or to its poor quality, or because inside it and on its edges there is hard flesh that prevents the growth of good flesh. Or because it is very dirty and foul; or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the material it discharges is of poor quality; or because the medication is unsuitable for its treatment; or because of a general epidemic that has occurred in the region; or because of a specific characteristic of the region.
Or because it is very dirty and foul; or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the material it discharges is of poor quality; or because the medication is unsuitable for its treatment; or because of a general epidemic that has occurred in the region; or because of a specific characteristic of the region. As happened in the city of Zaragoza, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is naturally slow; or because there is one or several bones in it. I have explained all these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medication in the classification.
It is appropriate here that I inform you of the treatment of fistulas and abscesses which is done by hand. Know that fistulas may occur in all parts of the body; some of them end in large veins and arteries or in a nerve.
Or to the peritoneum, or to the intestines, or to the bladder, or to a rib, or to a vertebra of the spine, or to a joint of the compound joints, such as the hand and foot; and some lead to the vicinity of a principal organ, and so on.
Those fistulas of this kind are among the difficult-to-cure diseases, whose treatment is laborious and requires knowledge. If, however, the fistula has not reached the locations I have mentioned, then there is hope and expectation for its treatment.
And if you wish to know this, take a probe of copper or iron. If the fistula is straight, examine it with this. If the fistula is tortuous, examine it with a probe of thin lead, for lead is soft.
It will thus pass easily when entering and bend towards the tortuous part. If the fistula has many openings which you cannot discover with the probe, inject one of its openings; then the moisture which you inject will pass towards the other openings and flow out.
Then thoroughly investigate in any way possible to ascertain whether there is bone or nerve, whether the bottom of the fistula is near or far, or whether it is a single fistula with many openings.  Ascertain all this to the best of your ability by questioning the patient and observing the pain when your hand probes the area.
And similar signs and causes of the tumor.  When you have ascertained all this with certainty, then proceed to treatment with confidence.  This is to observe whether...
the fistula is superficially near or in a safe place far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or one of the places I mentioned to you, then incise the fistula as previously described and remove any adhesion, diseased flesh, and excess flesh.
And whatever grows in its opening, such as flesh or a wart, and treat it until it heals. If the fistula is deep-seated and straight, you should incise it as deeply as possible, then cleanse it of all diseased flesh.
Then use a pledget soaked in strong medicine and insert it to the bottom of the fistula which you can reach with an instrument; do this repeatedly until the strong medicine eats away all the remaining corruption in the bottom of the fistula.
Then dress it with ointments that promote healthy flesh growth until it heals. If it does not heal in this way, cauterize it as previously described. If the cause of the fistula is bone, and you are certain of this, then probe and incise it as I have told you, unless something prevents you from…
a nerve, or a major organ as we said. If the bone is exposed and there is some corruption and blackness in it, scrape it until the corruption is gone, then treat it with something that will heal it until it heals.  If it does not heal and pus continues to form as before…
know that you have not completely removed all the corruption.  Expose everything again and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it to the best of your ability, then dress it. If it heals, otherwise know that the corruption is in a deep recess. Expose everything again.
and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it to the best of your ability. If the diseased bone is small and you can remove it, then remove it with delicate forceps suitable for this.
If there are many bones, thoroughly remove them all and do not leave any behind to the best of your ability. If something like ligaments obstructs you, and there is no danger in cutting them, then cut those ligaments and the flesh, if it is there, and remove the bones.
If you are unable to remove the bones at that time, apply something to the wound that will cause it to decay and leave it for several days until the flesh and ligaments around the bones decay. Keep the wound from closing, the incision you made during your work.
Rather, if you fear that, apply cotton soaked in powdered sulfur with oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, or one of the green ointments. For when those ligaments decay and the bones become loose, it will be easy to remove and extract them.
If it is a single large bone, such as a bone of the leg or thigh, and so on, and only its surface is diseased, then scrape it thoroughly until that blackness and decay disappears, then dress the wound.  If a large part of it is diseased and the decay has reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to remove it.
And cut it all away to where the decay ends; then treat it until it heals. And I will tell you about a case of gangrene that occurred in a man's leg, to serve as an example and aid in your treatment. This man was young.
About thirty years old, he experienced pain in his leg due to a cause that moved within his body until the matter reached the leg and swelled into a large tumor, and there was no external cause. Then time passed with the mistakes of the doctors until the tumor opened.
And a great deal of matter flowed from it, and he was mistreated until the leg became gangrenous and many openings appeared in it, all discharging pus and bodily fluids. A group of physicians treated him for about two years, but none of them were skilled in the craft of surgery.
Until they came to me. I saw his leg and the matter flowing from those openings in a great stream, and the man's body was emaciated and his color yellow. So I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
And I found that some led to others from all sides of the leg.  So I began by incising one of those openings until I uncovered some of the bone. And I found it decayed, eroded, blackened, putrefied, and perforated until the perforation reached the marrow. Then I excised what was revealed to me.
And I removed the decayed bone, and I thought that there was no decay in the bone other than what I had cut and excised, and that I had eradicated it. Then I set about closing the wound with cauterizing medications for a longer period, but it did not heal. Then I returned and uncovered the bone again beyond the first uncovering.
And I found the decay connected to the bone. So I excised what was revealed to me of that decay also. Then I attempted to close it, but it did not close nor heal. Then I uncovered it again, and I continued cutting the bone piece by piece and attempting to close it, but it did not close, until I had cut away about a span of bone.
And I removed it with its marrow. Then I closed it with medications, and it healed quickly and recovered.  This repetition in its operation and incision was necessary because of the patient's weakness and his low tolerance, and my fear for his death, for he was subject at all times to severe fainting from excessive vomiting.  Then he recovered completely.
And in the place of the bone, firm flesh grew, and his condition in his body improved, and his strength returned, and he managed his affairs, and in walking he suffered no harm whatsoever. If a bone in a place of the body is broken,
then it is necessary to pull it apart in this manner: you take a ligature and tie it to the end of the protruding bone, and you instruct someone to pull it upwards, and you make another ligature of thicker wool than the first ligature.
Then you tie it to the flesh under the bone and pull its end so that the flesh is drawn downwards, and you expose the flesh from the place which you want to saw so that the saw does not injure the flesh, and you place a firm board or plank under the bone from below.
For if you do that, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the decayed bone.  And it is necessary that the sawing be slightly above the place of decay, lest there be decay in the cavity of the bone which does not appear on its surface to the touch, so that you are compelled to saw it a second time.
If the bone is decayed and is not protruding but its parts are connected to one another, and the decay is in its middle or in some part of it, then expose the flesh from all its sides, then place the board underneath, then saw it from the first side where the decay is.
When you have finished sawing it, saw it from the other side, and let the sawing be a little distance from the decay, as we said. If the decay is in a joint, cut the decayed joint itself and saw the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the decay is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy but excision. If the decay is in the metacarpus of the hand or foot, the matter of its treatment is very difficult, but you should peel away the decay however it appears to you, and excise it and cleanse it in any way you can.
And by any means which straightens itself for you, provided that a vein or nerve does not impede you. And know that the instruments and saws for cutting these bones are numerous, according to the position and setting of the bones, their thickness and thinness, their size and smallness, and their hardness and porosity. Therefore, for each type of work, one should prepare an instrument suitable for that work.
Know that the operations themselves may indicate to you the type of instrument you need, if you have long experience and knowledge of the arts of this craft, because it is the skill of the craft and the witness to types of diseases. He may devise for himself instruments similar to those for each disease, and I am depicting for you at the end of this chapter several instruments.
to make them examples to be imitated and a measure by which you measure others, God willing. Image of a saw:

Chapter eighty-seven.
In the amputation of limbs and the sawing of bones.
Limbs may become putrid, either from an external cause or from an internal cause. If you treat that corruption with medicinal treatment and the treatment is not effective, and you see the corruption spreading in the limb, nothing hindering it,
it is necessary to amputate that member to where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may be saved thereby from death or from a great affliction which is greater than the loss of the member. And a sign of this happening to someone is that the member becomes black, until you think that fire has burned it.
Or it may become putrid after the blackness, until that putridity spreads to what is next to that member and attacks the whole body.  Therefore, hasten to cut it off.  Likewise, if the cause of the corruption is the sting of some venomous creature, like a sea scorpion, or a snake, or a tarantula, and the like.
If the corruption or the sting is in the tip of a finger, cut off the finger and do not allow the corruption to spread to the hand.  Likewise, if it has taken hold in the hand, cut it off from the origin of the forearm, and do not allow the corruption to spread until it attacks the forearms.
Likewise, if it has taken hold in the arm, cut off the arm at the elbow in the joint itself. If the corruption progresses and you see it has reached towards the shoulder, do not cut off the shoulder at all, for in that is the death of the patient, and use other treatments as much as possible.
And similarly you do with the leg: if the corruption takes hold in the toe, cut off the toe at one of the joints; and if it takes hold in the instep, cut off the whole foot; and if it ascends to the knee, cut off the leg at the knee joint.
And if the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no recourse but to leave it and surrender the patient to death. The method of cutting off or excising a member is to tie a ligature below the place you wish to cut and tie another ligature above the place.
And one assistant pulls the lower ligature downwards, and another assistant pulls the upper ligature upwards, and you yourself expose the flesh between the ligatures with a broad scalpel until all the flesh is exposed, then it is cut or excised; and it is necessary that you place linen cloths on all sides.
Lest the saw touch the healthy area, which would cause the patient excessive pain and hot swelling. If bleeding occurs during your work, quickly cauterize the area or apply some styptic powder, then return to your treatment until you finish.
Then bind the injured member with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals.  I will tell you about an example of this same affliction which appeared in a man’s leg:  that is to say, there appeared in his leg blackness with burning resembling fire.
And that corruption first appeared in his toe, until it took the whole leg.  So the man, of his own accord, when he saw the corruption spreading in the member, with the severity of the pain and burning, cut it off at the joint, and healed.
Then, after a long time, that same corruption appeared in the index finger of his hand.  He came to me, and I tried to repel that corruption with the medicines I applied to the hand after cleansing his body. But the corruption was not repelled, and it continued to spread into the other finger.
Until the corruption seized the hand. He urged me to amputate his hand, but I refused, hoping to repel the corruption, and also fearing for his life if I amputated his hand, because the man's strength was failing.  When he despaired of me, he went back to his town.
Then I heard that he himself had cut off his entire hand and healed.  I have recounted this story to be of help in cases of this kind of disease, and to be an example to be followed and acted upon.
Chapter eighty-eight.
On the treatment of fistulas and the method of injecting them with medicines.
If a swelling occurs in one of the fleshy members, and the duration of the swelling is prolonged until it gathers matter, then bursts or is lanced, and all the matter that was in it comes out, and the place remains empty like a vessel.
And the skin over it is like a rag, thinned, and its corruption has not reached the point of affecting bone, nerve, or ligament.  From this it deserves to be called a fistula, and it is not called a sinus unless its duration is prolonged until the corruption affects something of these members.
Then it is called a sinus or a fistula, and its treatment is to cut all that skin as it goes around, especially if it has thinned and become like a rag, and you are certain that it will not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere, and its corruption has not reached that point, and there is thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with injection. This is that you look at the fistula; if it is large and the pus that flows from it is foul-smelling, then inject it with the Egyptian medicine.
Which is vinegar, oil, honey, and verdigris, equal parts, gathered in a vessel and cooked over the fire until the medicine thickens and becomes the consistency of honey. Then take what you need from it and thin it with water and honey, and inject the fistula with it, and bind its mouth, and leave the medicine in it for about two hours.
Then you remove it by pressing; you do this for several days until the fistula is cleansed and the foul smell is gone.  It may also be injected with ash water if this medicine is not available to you. This is that you take the ash of grapevine wood or the ash of oak wood and pour water on it.
Then strain it and inject it until you are certain that the fistula has been washed. If the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then inject it with water and honey in which some powdered verdigris has been mixed.
Or inject it with honey and a syrup mixed together, for honey cleanses and washes, and the syrup adheres to the fistula, especially if the syrup has a binding and drying quality. When you have done this several times and are certain that the corruption of the fistula has gone, then inject it with something that will grow flesh in it.
Such as taking some palm ointment and dissolving it in rose oil and an astringent syrup, or inject it with some other medicines and ointments whose properties we have established in the chapter on ointments. If the mouth of the fistula is narrow and does not admit the injection tube,
widen it slightly with an iron instrument, or place in it a tent soaked in the Egyptian ointment or the theriac ointment until it widens.  Similarly, if the mouth is too wide, bring its edges together with sutures and leave only enough space to admit the injection tube, no more.
Similarly, if the opening from which the pus flows is high up, incise it in the lowest part so that the pus will flow downwards, for if the pus is retained in the depth of the fistula it prevents the growth of flesh there.
If you cannot incise the fistula downwards as you wish, then raise the limb so that the pus flows easily, in whatever way is convenient, so that no pus at all is retained.
As for the cauterizing medicines which are applied externally to the fistula, and the method of applying them: take one of the cauterizing ointments similar to what you want, and spread it on a linen cloth; let the cloth be large enough to cover the whole fistula. Then cut with scissors opposite the mouth of the fistula a hole slightly larger than the mouth of the fistula; then spread some soft ointment on another cloth, the size of the hole, and place it on, keeping it away from the mouth of the fistula so that the pus may easily escape; do not remove the large cloth until afterwards.
several days; as for that on the mouth of the fistula, remove it at all times so that whatever pus has collected there may escape, and to see what kind of pus escapes from it, whether it is much or little, good or bad.
And also examine the fistula itself: does the patient feel pain in it or not, and is there swelling or not? If your treatment continues thus and you see that the matter which escapes is slight at the mouth of the fistula,
know that the fistula has adhered or is near to adhering; then dissolve it in a day or on the third day; and when you have dissolved the wound, change the cloths and renew the ointment; if after a long time thin pus escapes from the fistula, do not despair of its closing.
Its healing is near, for some people are slow in the growth of flesh in their wounds, and others the opposite; if after many days, non-laudable pus escapes from the fistula, know that the fistula has not closed; if the fistula is slow to close,
increase the drying medicines, and let the medicines be moist in consistency and dry in power, like palm ointment if excess of litharge has been added to it; and among the most effective things with which to treat it is to take palm ointment,
and dissolve it with rose oil and sprinkle on it old wine, moderate in consistency, then knead it with it and use it; and he may do this if palm ointment is not available to him: honey, if you cook it until it thickens and use it, or take myrrh, aloes and costus, and grind them all together and sprinkle them on the honey while it is on the fire. Then spread it on a cloth and bind it on the fistula, or apply honey which you have cooked until it thickens, and sprinkle on it the drugs, and place on it the cloth and bind it.
Long zedoary, the root of the asphodel,  and powdered cyclamen and centaury may be used in such a case, singly or mixed; grind them, sieve them and sprinkle them on the honey which I have described, and use it; if the fistula persists and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption in its depth.
And if it has affected the bones or the other hard nerves or ligaments, treat it as a fistula, as previously described.
Chapter eighty-eight.
On the treatment of the felon, the injured nail, the amputation of the superfluous finger, and the separation of adherent fingers.
A felon is an excessive growth of flesh under the nail of the thumb of the hand or foot, and it may grow in other fingers. If its duration is prolonged and its treatment neglected, it swells into a hot tumor.
Or it becomes putrid and foul until it eats away the root of the nail, and perhaps the whole of it, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone until it has a foul smell, and the tip of the finger becomes broad, and its color becomes livid.  If you treat it with what we mentioned in the classification and your treatment is not effective,
then it is necessary to cut off with an iron instrument all the excess growth that grows from the nail, and then cauterize the wound afterwards, for cauterization is very beneficial in this. But if the bone is sound and the nail is also sound,
and the angle containing the nail has pressed the flesh inwards and is pinching and injuring it, then it is necessary to place a thin pledget under the angle of the nail which pinches the flesh and raise it upwards and cut that flesh gently.
And you should apply to what remains of the flesh of the cauterizing corrosive drugs until it all disappears, then treat it with ointments until it heals. But if the corruption has affected the bone, then it is necessary to cut off that bone and remove it, for it will not heal at all as long as there is a corrupt bone in it.
If you see the corrosion and corruption spreading in the finger, cut it off at one of the joints as previously mentioned, then treat it until it heals. If the nail receives a blow or injury and severe pain occurs in it, then it is necessary to bleed the patient first.
Then you should incise the nail with a sharp scalpel, an oblique incision from above downwards, and be careful not to reach with the incision to the flesh under the nail, for by that you would cause the patient severe pain, and it would be a cause for the growth of excess flesh in the place. Then treat the area with what relieves pain.
As for the extra finger which grows in some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, or it may have bones in it, and it may have a nail, and the growth of some of them is at the root of the joint of some fingers.
Or its growth may be in some of the finger joints. Those that grow in some of the finger joints do not move, and those that grow at the joint of the finger may move. As for those that are fleshy only, it is easy to cut them off, and that is to cut them off at their base with a broad scalpel.
As for those whose growth is at the root of the joint, their treatment is difficult, so avoid cutting them. As for those that grow in the finger at one of the joints, it is necessary first to cut their flesh in a circular cut to the bone, then to saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for that.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the adhesion which occurs to the fingers, some to others, this often occurs, and this is from what a person is born with, and it is at the healing of a wound or a burn by fire and the like.  Then it is necessary to incise that adhesion.
Until the fingers return to their natural form. Then place between them a pledget or a piece of lint soaked in rose oil, lest it quickly adhere, and separate them, or place between them a thin lead plate until the place heals as it should.
And likewise, if adhesion occurs to some of the fingers with the palm, incise that adhesion according to what is suitable and corrects the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On cutting hemorrhoids.
Hemorrhoids are twisted, thick veins, filled with black, superfluous blood.  They occur in most parts of the body, most frequently in the legs, especially in the legs of blacksmiths, bakers and porters. First, one should use strong rubbing of the body with black bile.
Then bleed the patient in the basilic vein. As for treating them with iron, there are two ways: one is to incise and remove the black blood; the other is to cauterize the vein and remove it entirely. As for incision, it is in this manner:  first bathe the leg with warm water well.
until the thick, turbid blood dissolves. Then bind the patient's leg from above his thigh to below his knee with a bandage. Then incise the vein in one, two or three places with a wide incision.
Then squeeze out the black blood with your hand from the lower leg upwards and from the upper leg downwards, until you remove the amount of blood you consider sufficient or what the patient's strength can bear. Then bind it and instruct him to avoid foods that generate black bile.  Repeat the purging and bloodletting.
When the veins are full and this harms the patient.  As for cauterizing it, it is done in this manner: shave the patient's leg if it has much hair. Then take him to the bathhouse and bathe his leg in hot water until it reddens and sweats, or let him do vigorous exercise.
If a bathhouse is not available, heat the limb. Then incise the skin opposite the vein lengthwise, either at its end near the knee or at its lower part near the heel. Then open the skin with hooks and dissect the vein from all sides until it is palpable.
When it appears, you will see it is dark red. When it is freed from the skin, it looks like a tendon. Then insert a probe beneath it until it rises and emerges from the skin; suspend it with a smooth, blunt hook. Then make another incision near that incision, about three fingers' breadth away.
Then dissect the skin from over the vein until it appears. Then lift it with the probe as you did before and suspend it with another hook as you did initially. Then make another incision, or many incisions if necessary.
Then dissect it and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel. Then pull and dissect it until it emerges from the second incision. Then pull it to the incision above it and do this until you pull it from the third incision, above all the incisions, until when all of it has emerged, cut it.
If it does not yield to traction and dissection, insert a needle with strong doubled thread, tie it, and pull. Insert the probe underneath and use it to guide your hand to each side until it emerges. Be careful not to break it, for if it breaks, it will be very difficult for you to dissect it.
And harm will befall the patient from it.  When you have dissected it all, apply wool soaked in a potion and rose oil or olive oil to the site of the incisions, and treat it until it heals. If the varix is tortuous, with twists and turns in various directions,
and is not straight as we said, then it is necessary that you make an incision on it at each part of its twists and turns, then suspend it with hooks until you dissect it entirely.  Be careful when incising it not to cut or injure the vein, for it will be difficult for you to dissect it, so exert yourself carefully.
Image of the incision used to incise the varix:
Image of the blind hook:
It should not be hooked like other hooks, nor should it have a sharp point lest it injure the vein. It should be thick, smooth, and curved, for if it is thin it will cut the vein with its thinness. Rather, it should have a good thickness, as we have said.
Chapter ninety-one.
On the removal of the varicose vein.
This vein develops in the legs in hot countries like the Hijaz and the Arab lands, and in hot, arid lands with little fertility.  It may also develop in other parts of the body besides the legs, and its development is due to an infection that occurs under the skin.
Just as living creatures and worms occur inside bodies, and the gourd worm and the worm that develops between the skin and flesh, the sign of the beginning of this vein is that a severe inflammation occurs in the leg, then the area swells, and then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that swelling.
As if it were the root of a plant or an animal, so when its tip appears, it should be wrapped with a small piece of lead weighing from one dirham to two dirhams, and tied, leaving the lead hanging from the leg.
So, whenever something emerges from it to the outside, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it becomes very long, cut some of it and wrap the remainder; do not cut it from its origin before it has all emerged, for if you cut it, it will retract and enter the flesh, causing swelling, infection in the area, and a bad ulcer.
