Therefore, it should be carefully and gradually pulled until it is completely removed and nothing of it remains in the body. From this vein, some people may have something that is five or ten spans long.
It has been reported to me that it came out of a man twenty cubits long. If it breaks while you are treating it, insert a probe into its hole and probe it deeply along the body until all its material is emptied. Then, try to cleanse the area with medicines for a few days.
Then treat it with the treatment for tumors. This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint of the leg or in the leg itself, resulting in many openings.  From each opening, a branch emerges; treat it as we mentioned in the classification and as previously described.
Chapter Ninety-Two.
Concerning the incision for the worm that develops under the armpit, and is called the cow's disease.
This disease is called in some of our countries "the cow's disease," because it often affects cows. It is a small single worm that develops between the skin and the flesh, and moves throughout the body, ascending and descending.
It is perceptible to the touch as it moves from one member to another, until it breaks through the skin at some point and emerges.  Its formation is from the corruption of some humors, as happens with worms, snakes, and pumpkin seeds in the abdomen.
The danger from its harm is that if it moves in the body and ascends to the head and reaches the eye, it may break through it and emerge, destroying the eye. This occurs frequently. If you wish to treat it and remove it, this should be done when it is moving and is perceptible to the touch.
It is necessary to tightly bind above and below it with a strong bandage, then incise over it and remove it. If it has sunk into the flesh and you cannot find it, apply cautery with fire to the area until you burn it. The greatest danger, as we said, is its potential to damage the eye.
If you see that it has reached the head near the eye, bind tightly below it on the forehead, then incise over it and remove it.  The patient should be treated by purifying the body with purgative medicines for bad, corrupt humors, and should abstain from foods that produce putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-Three
Concerning the incision for the disease known as the gnawing ulcer.
This disease, which in our country is called *nafir*, is a pain that appears in some organs and then spreads from one organ to another. I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was called to a sick woman in the countryside. She uncovered her arm and showed me a slight swelling in a vein of the forearm. After a short time, I saw that swelling moving along the ulna like a worm, ascending to her shoulder as quickly as possible, like mercury.
When it moved from one place to another, the pain disappeared from that place and settled in the shoulder. Then, after a while, it spread throughout the body until it reached the other arm. Then she told me that it circled her whole body, as I witnessed.
I was amazed at the speed of its movement from one organ to another. I had never before seen this disease with my own eyes in this way, except that I had seen people whose pain moved from one organ to another, but I had not seen it with my own eyes as I saw it in this woman.
I could only explain it by the fact that the woman was from the countryside, with a dry body and visible veins; therefore, that moving wind was perceptible.  It would not appear in this way in people of comfort, with moist bodies and hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it, and the patient feels pain, and it appears to you visibly as we have mentioned, then bind above and below it with a bandage and incise it until that congested wind is expelled, and cauterize the place.
If you do not see it with your eyes, then treat it by means of things that dispel and disperse winds, such as the seeds of the  *mentan* and *sakbij* and similar medicines.
Chapter ninety-four.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and according to the places in the body where they lodge. As for their differences according to their types, there are large and small ones; some are hollow glass, others solid glass; some have three points, others four.
Some have barbs, some have splinters. As for those according to the members in which they lodge, they are of two kinds: either the members are principal hollow organs, such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. If an arrow lodges in one of these organs, and the signs of death which I shall describe to you appear, then you should avoid extracting that arrow from it, for death will overtake its owner in most instances. But if these bad signs do not appear, and the arrow is not hidden deep within the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  Among the signs of a wound to the brain: if an arrow pierces the bone and wounds the membrane covering the brain, there will result from it a severe headache, stupor, and dizziness.
But if these bad signs do not appear, and the arrow is not hidden deep within the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  Among the signs of a wound to the brain: if an arrow pierces the bone and wounds the membrane covering the brain, there will result from it a severe headache, stupor, and dizziness. Redness in the eyes and redness of the tongue, convulsions, mental confusion, vomiting, and sometimes blood comes from the nostrils or ears; sometimes speech is lost and the voice is gone, and a white moisture resembling gruel flows from the wound, along with a watery substance like serum.
If these signs appear, refrain from treating the patient and from extracting the arrow, if it has not been extracted.  As for the signs of an arrow which has struck the heart: if it is near the left breast, it will be felt as if it has been driven into something solid, not into something empty.
And sometimes the arrow will have a movement resembling the pulse, and black blood will flow from the wound, followed by coldness of the extremities, cold sweat, and fainting. Know then that death is inevitable. A sign that the arrow has wounded the lung is the outflow of frothy blood from the wound.
The vessels near the neck swell, the patient's color changes, he breathes heavily, and he seeks to inhale cold air. If the arrow strikes the diaphragm in the chest, it will be near the lower ribs, and there will be great difficulty in breathing along with severe pain.
He will sigh and all the muscles of the shoulders will move. If the arrow strikes the liver, severe pain will follow, and blood resembling liver in redness will flow from the wound. If the arrow strikes the stomach, something undigested from the food may come out of the wound.
And its effect is obvious. If the arrow strikes the abdomen and becomes embedded there, and something like excrement, fat, or intestine, which has been perforated, comes out of the wound, then there is no hope of treatment or of extracting the arrow. If the arrow strikes the bladder, urine will come out.
And if something protrudes from it and the pain intensifies for the patient, know that he is doomed. As for the other organs, such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, vertebrae of the back, collarbone, thigh, leg and similar organs, they are mostly unharmed.
When the arrow does not encounter an artery or nerve, and the arrow is not poisoned.  And I tell you some of what I have witnessed regarding these arrows so that you may thereby be guided in your treatment. That is, an arrow once struck a man in the inner canthus of his eye at the root of the nose.
I removed it for him from the other side, under the lobe of the ear, and he recovered, and no harm befell his eye. I removed another arrow for a Jew, which had struck him in the lobe of his eye beneath the lower eyelid; the arrow had become hidden, and I only grasped its small tip which is attached to the shaft.
It was a large arrow from a composite bow, with a smooth, square iron head, lacking barbs. The Jew recovered, and no harm befell his eye.  I removed another arrow from the throat of a Christian; the arrow was an Arabian arrow.
It had barbs, so I made an incision between the jugular veins; it had sunk into his throat. I carefully extracted it, and the Christian recovered. I removed another arrow for a man who had been struck in the abdomen; we judged that he would die from it.
But when thirty days or so had passed, and his condition had not changed, I made an incision over the arrow and skillfully extracted it; he recovered and suffered no harm. I saw a man struck by an arrow in his back, and the wound had closed over it.
Then, after seven years, the arrow emerged at the root of his thigh. I saw a woman who had been struck in her abdomen by an arrow; the wound had closed, and the arrow remained.  Her condition did not change, nor did she experience any harm in any of her natural functions.
I saw another man struck by an arrow in the face; the wound closed, and he felt little pain.  Many such cases occurred. I removed an arrow for a man from the Sultan's guard; it had struck him in the middle of his nose, inclining slightly to the right.
The entire arrow was embedded. I was called to treat him three days after the arrow struck. I found the arrow wound very narrow. I explored it with a thin probe, but I did not feel it. He felt a stinging pain under his ear on the right side of the incision.
I hoped that this stinging pain was from the arrowhead. I applied a dressing with strong drawing and ripening properties, hoping that the area would swell and reveal the arrow's location so that I could incise it. But nothing occurred to indicate that the arrow had reached that location.
I continued the dressing for many days, but nothing happened.  In the meantime, the wound closed, and the patient despaired of removing the arrow for a time, until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose. He informed me, and I applied a strong, corrosive medication to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it. I felt the thin end of the arrow that attaches to the shaft. Then I further opened the wound with that strong medication until the arrowhead became visible.  This took about four months.
Then, when the wound had widened and I could insert forceps, I pulled and moved it, but it would not come out. I continued to manipulate it and devise methods using various instruments until one day I grasped it firmly with forceps, as shown in the last chapter.
The physicians judged that the cartilage of his nose would not heal, but I healed it, and the wound closed, and the patient recovered completely, without any further harm. I tell you how to extract some arrows so that it may serve as a measure and guide for what I have not mentioned.
Because the parts and details of this craft cannot be grasped by description, nor contained in a book. Rather, the skillful craftsman infers from the little to the much, and from what is present to what is absent, and devises a new operation and a new instrument in strange emergencies.
If we turn to this craft, I say that arrows are extracted from the members in which they are embedded in two ways: either by pulling from the point of entry, or from the opposite side, and those which are extracted from where they entered.
Either the arrow is protruding in a fleshy place, in which case it is pulled and removed.  If it does not yield to removal from the place where it entered, you should leave it for days until the surrounding flesh rots, thus making it easier to pull and remove.  The same applies if it is embedded in bone.
If it does not yield to removal, leave it also for days and try pulling and moving it every day; then it will come out. If it does not yield to removal after days, you should pierce around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a fine drill, until you widen the space for the arrow, then pull and remove it.
If the arrow is embedded in the skull bone, and has penetrated deeply into one of the ventricles of the brain, and some of those symptoms I mentioned appear in the patient, then refrain from pulling the arrow and leave it until its condition improves after days.
then attempt to pull out the arrow. If it is deeply embedded and does not yield to pulling, use the drills around the arrow as I described to you, then treat the wound until it heals. And if the arrow has disappeared into a place in the body, and is hidden from sensation, probe it with a probe. If you feel it, pull it with some instruments suitable for pulling it. If you cannot reach it because of the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow, and there is neither bone, nor nerve, nor vein,
then incise it until the wound is widened and you can reach the arrow to remove it. If it has barbs, grasp them and free the flesh entangled in them from every side by any means you can.  And attempt, if you cannot free the flesh, to break the barbs and twist them until you free them.
And when you attempt to remove the arrow from any place, use the twisting of your hand with forceps in all directions until you free it.  And be extremely gentle lest the arrow break and it becomes difficult for you to pull it out. If you cannot manage it at that time,
leave it for days until the flesh around it putrefies, then return to it; it will then be easier. If bleeding hinders you, use what we mentioned in its chapter.  And take care to avoid cutting a vein, nerve, or tendon and use skill.
By any means you can free the arrow, and let this be done gently and calmly and steadily as I described to you. And it is necessary for you, when you pull the arrow, to position the patient in the same position as he was when the arrow struck; this is more appropriate.
If you cannot do that, use whatever positions you can. As for the arrow that emerges from the opposite side, either something of it will have emerged outside, or you will find the tip of the arrow by touch just under the skin, and see that it is protruding.
Then make an incision upon it, and let the incision be only as large as is needed for the forceps.  Then pull it; it will be easy to remove. If it is stuck in a bone, twist your hand in a circle until the arrow affects the bone and widens a space for itself, then pull it.
Otherwise, leave it for some days, then return to it until it comes out. If the arrow shaft is still in place, push it with it. If the shaft has fallen off and you want to use pushing, then insert the hollow instrument so that its cavity enters the tail of the arrow, then push it with it.
If the arrow is hollow, push it with an instrument that enters that cavity; the arrow will then easily come out. If the arrow is poisoned, it is necessary that you scrape away all the flesh in which the poison is, if you can, then treat it with what is suitable for that.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, or in the abdomen, or in the bladder, or in the flank, and it is near enough to be felt with a probe, and you can make an incision upon it, then make an incision, being careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then stitch the wound if it needs stitching, then treat it until it heals.  Image of the forceps with which arrows are extracted:
Their ends are like the beak of a bird; they are made like a file, so that when they grasp the arrow or anything, they do not let go.  Large, small, and medium-sized ones can be made, all according to the size of the arrow, and the width and narrowness of the wound.
Image of the hollow pusher:
Image of the solid pusher:
This has a solid end like an awl to facilitate its entry into the hollow arrow and to push it.
Chapter ninety-five.
On the incision of veins.
The veins which it is customary to incise in the body are thirty veins, of which sixteen are in the head: the two pulsating veins which are behind the ears, known as the "al-ḥasīsayn", and the two arteries which are in the temples, which are apparent, and the two veins which are at the corners of the eyes, known as the "al-nāẓirīn".
And the vein which is erect in the middle of the forehead, and the vein which is at the tip of the nose, and the two jugular veins which are in the neck, and the two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins which are in the lower lip—these veins are known as al- jahārik—and the two veins which are under the tongue.
And as for the veins which are incised in the arm and hand, they are five veins: one of them is the cephalic, which is from the lateral side, and the common people call it the vein of the head; and the median cubital, which is the middle vein, and it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, and the common people call it the vein of the body.
And the basilic, which is situated on the medial side, and it is also called the axillary, and the common people call it the vein of the abdomen; and the vein of the forearm, which is situated on the ulna, and it is that to which it adheres, and it is that which appears above the thumb clearly; and the "asīlim".
And it is the vein which is between the little finger and the ring finger; it has two branches. And in the leg and foot there are three veins: one of them is that which is under the hollow of the knee from the lateral side; and the second is the saphenous, and its place is at the heel from the medial side; and the vein of the sciatic nerve, and its place is at the heel from the lateral side.
And in the other leg there are three veins like them. As for the two veins which are behind the ears, the benefit of incising them is for chronic colds, migraine, and favus, and chronic bad sores of the head, and the manner of incising them is as I describe.
And that is to shave the head of the patient and to rub his head well in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth, then the patient's neck is constricted with a turban until the two veins appear and their place behind the ears in the two low places of the head, then you feel them with your finger, and wherever you feel their pulse under your finger, there you mark with ink.
Then you take a lancet-like scalpel, which is known as the *nashshāl*, then you insert it under the vein in the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone, then you raise your hand with the vein and skin upwards and you cut the vein with the skin a cut that is severed, and the length of the cut is the amount of two joined fingers.
Or something similar, and you let out the amount of blood that you want, then you bind them with bandages and leave them until they heal. And it was mentioned earlier in the beginning of the book their cutting and cauterization. As for the two arteries which are in the temples, the benefit of incising them is for chronic migraine, severe headache, persistent ophthalmia, and the flow of sharp humors directed towards the eyes.
And the manner of incising them is as I describe to you: the patient constricts his neck with a turban until the veins appear clearly to the touch and their pulse is apparent under your finger, then at that time you mark with ink, then you lift the skin from above the vein upwards with your index finger.
And you insert the *nashshāl* scalpel from below and raise the vein upwards and sever it as you did with the other two veins, and you let out blood according to your need, then you loosen the constriction of the patient's neck and place your finger on the vein for an hour.
Then you place on it cotton and a bandage and bind it tightly from above and leave it until it heals.  And their mention, cutting, and cauterization were mentioned earlier in the book. As for incising the vein of the forehead, its benefit is after incising the cephalic vein for chronic facial ailments such as ringworm, ulcers, and erysipelas.
And the manner of incising it, as I describe to you: the patient constricts his neck with a turban until the vein appears, then you take the instrument called the hatchet, and this is its image:
You place the projecting spike which is in the head of the hatchet on the vein itself and strike from above with a comb or something similar, and you leave the blood to flow to the amount you desire, then you loosen the constriction of the patient's neck and bandage it until it heals.
And it may be opened with a wide scalpel, except that it should not be sharp-pointed like other scalpels, but rather slightly wide-ended, and you incise it obliquely, because the bone is near, so the scalpel may break if it is thin.
As for the two veins which are at the temples of the eyes, their benefit is for eye ailments such as scabies, erysipelas, and blepharitis, and facial diseases. As for the manner of incising them, it is that the patient constricts his neck with a turban, then you incise them while you are standing over his head, and let the incision be slightly obliquely lengthwise with a small, slightly wide scalpel.
For the place has no flesh in it; if the scalpel is thin-pointed, it may break. Then you let out the amount of blood you need and place cotton on them and bandage them for one night, then you unbind them. As for the incision of the vein of the nose, it is beneficial for acute fevers.
And severe headache and facial diseases such as the red erysipelas which appears in the nose, especially if it is chronic.  The manner of incising it is that the patient constricts his neck, then you hold his nose with your left hand and take a thin, long scalpel.
And you insert it into the middle of the nasal septum itself, between the nasal partition, straight, because the vein does not appear to the touch there. Then the blood will emerge immediately.  You should move your hand with the scalpel a little and let out the amount of blood you need, then bind it for a night, for it heals quickly.
As for the jugular veins, the benefit of incising them is for shortness of breath, the beginning of leprosy, and melancholic diseases which appear on the surface of the skin, such as black vitiligo, scabies, and bad ulcers and cankers.  The manner of incising them is that the patient constricts beneath them in his neck with a ligature and the surgeon stands at the head of the patient.
And the patient sits on a chair, then you incise the vein lengthwise, a somewhat wide incision, then you let out a moderate amount of blood, or according to what you see as necessary. Then you do likewise with the other vein, then you untie the ligature.
And you tie the veins moderately so that the patient does not suffocate, and you leave him until the next day, for the wound will heal. As for the veins of the temples, the benefit of incising them, after incising the cephalic veins, is that it benefits from canker sores in the mouth, corruption of the gums, bad ulcers, cracking of the lips, and bad ulcers.
which are in and around the nose. The manner of incising them is that you seat the patient before you and tie his neck with a turban, then you turn back his lips and look at the two veins, one of which you see to the right of the lip and the other to the left of it, and you distinguish them by their blackness.
And that is also because around them are small, black veins; so cut them with a cutting incision. If it is difficult for you and you do not know what they are, then aim to cut the larger and more obvious ones. And likewise, do in the two veins which are in the lower lip.
As for the two veins which are under the tongue, the benefit of incising them after incising the cephalic veins is for the inflammations which occur in the throat from disease of the uvula and diseases of the mouth.  The manner of incising them is that you seat the patient before you facing the sun, and raise his tongue, and look under the tongue, on one side a vein and on the other side a vein, and their color is toward blackness.
Then you incise them, and be careful not to go too deep in cutting them, for beneath them are arteries, and perhaps bleeding from those arteries will occur. As for the three veins which are incised in the elbow, they are those which custom has established for incising in all people, and their incision is in two ways.
Either by piercing with a wide, basil-shaped or olive-shaped lancet to the depth, or by cutting with a knife-shaped lancet, which is the *nishal* [and this is its picture], a picture of the wide basil-shaped lancet:
It is wide as you see, so that it is suitable for opening hollow, full, prominent, apparent, thick veins, and those which contain thick, turbid blood. And this is a picture of the olive-shaped lancet:
And this lancet is less wide and thinner at the point, suitable for incising thin veins which contain thin, yellowish blood. And this is a picture of the *nishal* lancet:
This is the *nishal*, which is suitable for cutting, and there are types of it, wide and thin, according to the width and narrowness of the veins also. And one may infer from this (description) others (types of lancets), and it is well-known among the artisans.
As for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of its incision is that it draws blood from the illnesses which are under the throat and neck, from what is next to the chest and abdomen.  It is necessary for the phlebotomist, when incising it, to be cautious and to be on the neck side of it, for beneath it is an artery; if he errs and goes too deep with the lancet, he will cut that artery.
Then bleeding will occur; therefore, it is necessary that its incision not be done with a lancet for puncturing, but its incision should be a cut with the *nishal*.  If the basilic vein does not appear clearly, then it is necessary to avoid it and turn to another (vein) or seek one of its branches.
Or you may incise in its place the vein of the arm, for it is clear, and incise it with the *nishal* lancet as we said. If you wish to incise it itself, then before tightening the arm, it is necessary to feel the place until you identify the position of the pulse, then mark it with ink.
Then you bind the arm and incise the vein with a sharp incision with the *nishal* lancet, as we said, and take care that the incision falls away from the position of the artery. And when you see, upon tightening the ligature, a swelling in the place which you marked with ink, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see that the blood, upon incision, spurts like the urine of a boy, and the blood is thin and red, then know that it is from the artery. Then immediately place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger. If the bleeding stops—and it often stops—then tighten the arm.
And leave it, and warn the patient against neglecting it, and let him be on his back and not move for days until it heals. If the blood does not stop and overcomes you and you do not have medicine at hand, then amputate the artery if it appears to you, for its ends will contract and the blood will stop.
Or take a pistachio shell and split it, and take one half and bind it tightly on the vein with a ligature and pledgets until the next day. If the blood stops, well and good; otherwise, treat it with what was previously mentioned, by placing styptics, and stopping its blood is not difficult in most cases.
Because of the smallness of the wound and the tightness of the ligature in the arm, inform him of that. As for the *akhal* vein, the benefit of its incision is that it draws blood from the upper head and the lower body, because it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, as we said.
It is necessary for the one who bleeds him to be on his back when he bleeds him, for there is a nerve underneath. If he exceeds in the insertion of the lancet and injures the nerve, numbness occurs in it which is difficult to cure, and perhaps it will not heal at all. And this nerve is often perceptible to the touch.
If it is hidden in some people and is thin and not discernible, then you should make your incision of it with a lancet, and avoid the nerve as much as you can. If the vein is between two nerves, then split the vein lengthwise. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of its incision is that it draws blood from the head.
It benefits from diseases of the eyes, and it is necessary in this vein especially, if you wish to bleed it, to make the incision with an olive-shaped lancet or with a wide, basil-shaped lancet, because it is the safest of all veins, because there is no artery nor nerve beneath it.
Except that it is necessary for you, when bleeding, to avoid with the lancet only the head of the muscle and seek the soft place.  It does not harm him if he is not hit with the first blow to be bled again many times, except that perhaps it swells in some people if it is not bled in the first blow.
But that swelling does not harm him at all. As for the method of bleeding, its complications, and what should precede in its treatment, the first of that is that you should know that bleeding is either used only in preserving health.
And it is either used in illnesses. That which is used in preserving health and maintaining it, and in guarding against the occurrence of illnesses, is that the bleeding be in one of the three veins in the elbow, I mean the cephalic, the median, and the basilic, and that the bleeding be at the beginning of spring if the signs of fullness appear.
And the bleeding should be on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours of the day have passed.  As for the bleeding which is used in illnesses, it does not have a limited time, but whenever necessity and need require it, whether by night or by day.
And at every hour and at every time.  And children should not be bled until fourteen years have passed for them, nor should old men who have passed sixty years be bled. If someone intends to bleed for any reason whatsoever,
he should cleanse his bowels before bleeding with a gentle enema if there is much retained excrement in them, lest there be drawn into the veins, at the time of bleeding, foul matter from the intestines which harms the principal organs. And he should not bleed the feverish, the drunkard, or the intoxicated until that passes from them.
And let him also beware of bleeding after dysentery, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive coitus, exertion, exercise, wakefulness, fasting, and everything that weakens the strength, whether physical or mental. Then consider the thinning of the humors beforehand if the blood is thick.
by means of foods, drinks, and medicines, if he is able to do so. Then let him enter the bath if nothing prevents him, or let him practice some exercise so that the blood is thinned, and let him make his bleeding in the middle of the day as we have said, and let him intend to free his chest that day from all bad psychological effects.
such as worries, anger, and fear, and from all physical effects like excessive exertion and labor, coitus, and the like. And let things be prepared for his session which people customarily use, from kinds of perfumes and aromatics and entertainments and the like, each person according to what he is able.
Then the phlebotomist sits on a cushion which is higher than the cushion on which the patient sits, then he extends his arm and the phlebotomist rubs it with his hand two or three times, then he tightens the ligature with the forceps and twists it two or three times, and let the tightening be moderate.
For if the tightening is not moderate, either by excess in tightening, it prevents the flow of blood, or if it is towards relaxation, it also prevents the flow of blood. Then after the tightening, it is necessary that the patient rub both his hands together.
until the veins swell and become apparent to the touch. Then the phlebotomist wipes the lancet with a little old oil, especially, then he places the index finger of his left hand on the same vein a little below the place which he wants to bleed, lest the vein slips away and avoids the incision. Because some veins you find are like a tendon, they slip away during phlebotomy, and some are full of wind, so when the lancet is placed on them, they sink below the lancet and deceive the phlebotomist, and the lancet does not open the vein, and if it opens it, then its opening is narrow.
Therefore, the phlebotomist should be careful and patient in all these matters, then he lowers the lancet; if the vein opens from that attempt, otherwise he repeats it another time a little below or above that place quickly if the place has not swollen.
If the patient swells or is frightened, leave him for a day or two and do not tighten the bandage, for it may bring a hot swelling, and do not enter the bath, then he may repeat the phlebotomy if he wishes. If the lancet is inserted and the opening is small and the flow of blood is thin,
and you fear that the desired amount of blood will not come out, then return the lancet to the same hole gently and straight, and increase the opening slightly, and do that quickly before the place swells, for in many people the place may swell with a small opening.
If you see that it has swollen, do not repeat it at all, for it will not help you; place something of oil dregs on it, for it facilitates the flow of blood and it is better in this place than the oil itself and other ointments as well.
So use oil dregs in all your phlebotomies of the veins when the flow of blood is difficult.  The theriac and the shakhzanya may also do this, if one of them is placed on the place, for the blood thins and dissolves if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of the phlebotomy—and this often happens, especially to one who has been phlebotomized only that once or whose vein opening was small—then act quickly and place on the swelling a sponge soaked in slightly warmed salt water and bind it for an hour, for it will resolve.
And it is necessary to do that after the complete exit of blood from the vein itself or from another vein. If something of blackness or greenness remains in the place after days, it does not harm; if you wish, then apply something of aloes and myrrh that have been dissolved.
Or something of the juice of the *fudanj* and the like. Often a swelling and a pustule occur when phlebotomizing the basilica vein; place your hand on it, and if you find that it softens when you probe it, then that is a bad pustule, so beware of putting anything on it that we have mentioned.
For it may bleed arterial blood; but bandage it with something constricting to solidify the place, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.  It is necessary to remove from each person blood in proportion to his strength and what appears of the color predominating in the blood.
For if the blood is black, let it come out until it becomes red; and likewise if you see it thick, let it flow until it thins; and likewise if it is sharp, until its sharpness goes away. It is necessary for someone who is full-bodied and strong and needs to have blood removed all at once…
…to widen the phlebotomy and the lancet to be wide. And for someone who is weak, the opposite. And it is necessary to remove it in stages and the hole to be narrow. And the best thing to use in phlebotomy is that which is sharp, honed, and slit, not punctured.
And this type of phlebotomy is safe from bleeding and from cutting a nerve; and it is better and safer than phlebotomy across or lengthwise. And whoever is accustomed to fainting at phlebotomy, it is necessary to give him before the phlebotomy something of bread soaked in pomegranate juice or oxymel.
If he is feverish and blood is removed from him in three or four times, then if he is cold-tempered, let him take before phlebotomy bread soaked in a drink of myrobalan or in a drink of honey sweetened with “afāwiyah” or in a fragrant basil drink.
If fainting occurs during phlebotomy and its cause is the removal of a great deal of blood, it is necessary to give him meat broth and a light basil drink; and he should use perfuming with galbanum, and rub his chest with it; and use all that we mentioned in the section on fainting.
which is from vomiting. And as for whoever wants to relax his arm and let his blood flow again, it is necessary for whoever had his phlebotomy for copious evacuation and whose strength is weak, to let the blood flow little by little according to his strength on consecutive days.
And as for whoever wants to relax his arm and let his blood flow again and his body is strong, let him do that after seven or nine hours from his first phlebotomy. And as for whoever wants to draw the blood from his body to the opposite direction to which it inclined, it is necessary to relax him on the second or third day.
And as for someone whose body contains much blood, which has become hot and sharp and caused fever, it is necessary to remove blood from him in one go, and to remove a large amount, and to widen the opening until fainting occurs, after having observed all the conditions of phlebotomy. And that you place your hand on his pulse during the flow of blood, lest death occur instead of fainting; for this often happens if the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligence occurs.  And it is not necessary, if you want to loosen the arm and let the blood flow again.
And if the mouth of the vein is closed and the exit of blood is difficult, do not prick it forcefully or twist it strongly, for that is very bad; rather, either leave it until you phlebotomize it again, or remove with the blade of the lancet what has solidified of the blood in the mouth of the vein.
Or apply something of salt dissolved in water, or apply something of theriac or  shakhzanaya, and prick it gently until the blood comes out; and if the vein is swollen, leave it.
And do not touch it until the swelling subsides; and if necessity demands letting the blood flow again, then either phlebotomize above that place, or phlebotomize in the other arm or in another vein.
As for phlebotomy of the median cephalic vein, it is phlebotomized instead of the basilic and cephalic veins if they are not found or are hidden, because it is composed of them.  And its method of phlebotomy is that the patient inserts his hand into hot water until the forearm reddens and the vein appears clearly, then it is tied above it with a ligature, a little moderately tight.
Then phlebotomize the vein slightly obliquely, neither across nor lengthwise, and let the opening be wide, and let your phlebotomy be a little above the wrist joint; and if the exit of blood is difficult, return the hand to the vessel of hot water and let the blood flow into the water.
Until it reaches your need. If you are in the summer days, you may dispense with returning the hand to hot water, and the use of hot water is most often for making the blood flow in winter.  And phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all veins. Because there is no major vein or nerve beneath it. As for phlebotomy of the median vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for ailments of the liver. And its method of phlebotomy is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand after inserting it into hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is no major vein or nerve beneath it. As for phlebotomy of the median vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for ailments of the liver. And its method of phlebotomy is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand after inserting it into hot water until the vein swells. And it becomes very perceptible to the touch. Then phlebotomize it slightly obliquely; and even if you cut it completely, it will not harm him at all. Be cautious, do not thrust your hand deeply with the lancet, for beneath it are the nerves of the fingers, and the location is devoid of flesh. Then return the hand to the hot water.
And leave it to flow. For if you do not return it to the hot water, the blood will congeal in the mouth of the vein and cease to flow.  And when you have removed the amount of blood needed, place fat and salt on the vein lest it close quickly.
And similarly you should act with every narrow branch.  As for the benefit of phlebotomy from the left hand, it is beneficial for ailments of the spleen; and you act in its phlebotomy as you did in the second, the same. As for phlebotomy of the saphenous vein, its benefit is for the diseases that are in the lower part of the body, such as ailments of the uterus.
And retention of menses, and diseases of the kidneys, and chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar diseases.  And its method of phlebotomy is that he inserts his leg into hot water and one rubs it until the veins swell, then you tie above the joint of the leg with a ligature, and the vein is located at the heel, on the surface, towards the great toe.
And many branches ramify from it on the surface of the foot. Therefore phlebotomize in its widest branch, or at the heel, where it joins; this is better and safer. For if you phlebotomize it on the surface of the foot, be cautious of the nerves beneath it on the surface of the foot.
And make your incision as if you intend to sever it, and let the lancet be sharp. If the exit of blood is difficult, let him return his leg to the hot water, and leave the blood to flow until it is emptied. If the phlebotomist misses the vein on the first attempt,
let him return to phlebotomize a little higher, for the place is safe, and no harm is feared from it if you are cautious of the nerve, as we said. And similarly you act with the saphenous vein of the other leg, the same.  As for the sciatic vein, its location is, as we said, at the heel, from the outer side.
And the benefit of its phlebotomy is for pain in the hip, if that is from excessive hot blood. And its method of phlebotomy is that you cause the patient to enter the bath, and hasten, and bind his leg from the hip to above the heel by four fingers with a thin, long bandage; for it does not appear except in this way.
Then, if it appears, phlebotomize it in any way you wish: either by incision, which is best, or you may sever it completely, or incise it; for its location is safe, and it is very hidden in most people. If you do not find it,
and it does not appear at all to the touch, then phlebotomize some of its branches, which are those that appear on the dorsum of the foot towards the little finger and ring finger. And be cautious of the nerves, and let out the amount of blood that you desire.
Then untie the bandage, and place cotton on the site of phlebotomy, and bind the place; for it quickly heals.
Chapter ninety-six.
Concerning cupping and its method of use.
Cupping vessels may be made of horn, wood, brass, or glass; and cupping is of two kinds: one is cupping with incision and evacuation of blood, and the other is cupping without incision.
And this cupping without incision is of two kinds.
And the cupping vessels of the nape of the neck, which are the two sides of the neck on both sides; and the cupping vessels of the chin, which is under the lower jaw; and the cupping vessels of the shoulders; and the cupping vessels of the coccyx, upon the rump; and the cupping vessels of the forearms, which are the middles of the arms; and the cupping vessels of the legs; and the cupping vessels of the heels.
Cupping draws blood from the small veins scattered in the flesh; and for this reason, it does not weaken the body as bloodletting does.  Nor should cupping, whether with fire or without fire, be used in any illness arising from plethora, until the whole body is evacuated.
If cupping is needed because of illness or custom, we use it at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, in the middle, or at any time.
That is because some people, when blood increases in them to the point that it needs to be removed by cupping, feel heaviness and headache; and some feel fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck; and some feel itching in their face and sides, darkness and burning in their eyes; and some itch at the site of their cupping.
And some laugh excessively; and some find the taste of blood in their mouths, their gums swell, and they spit blood; and some sleep excessively; and some see blood, redness, slaughter, and wounds in their sleep, and things like that.  When we see any of that,
especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we then order cupping after two or three hours of the day have passed. As for the benefit of cupping the temples, it benefits from heaviness in the head and water flowing into the eyes.
But it should be after emptying the whole body. This cupping may be a substitute for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.  It is cautioned against using it for those who are cold-headed or have a cold, for it greatly harms them.
Therefore, the elderly and those with cold illnesses in their heads should not use it, and those who habitually use it will develop forgetfulness.
Therefore, you should instruct the barber-surgeon to lower his hand with the cupping glass a little downwards, for fear of forgetfulness being generated.  As for cupping the nape of the neck, it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilic and cephalic veins.
Therefore, it benefits from asthma, shortness of breath, rupture of the respiratory apparatus, cough, and fullness. The cupping of the nape of the neck should be raised a little, for if it is lowered, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the temples, it benefits from pains occurring in the head, ophthalmia, migraine, angina, and toothache. It is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilic vein. You should instruct the barber-surgeon not to deepen his hand with the lancet, lest he cut a vein and cause bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it benefits from canker sores in the mouth, corruption of the gums, and similar diseases in the mouth.  It may take the place of phlebotomy of the veins in the lips. As for cupping the shoulders, it benefits from palpitations which result from fullness and heat.
As for cupping the inner aspects of the forearms, it benefits from what benefits phlebotomy of the three veins—the basilic, cephalic, and median—because it draws blood from all those small veins in the flesh, and it draws those small veins from other thicker veins until the drawing reaches the three thick veins.
You should instruct the barber-surgeon not to go too deep with the lancet, because the place is devoid of flesh and underneath are nerves and arteries. As for the single cupping performed on the coccyx, it benefits from hemorrhoids and lower ulcers.
You should instruct the barber-surgeon that the cupping glass should be large and made of copper, because the place needs strong suction and a glass cupping glass might break and require many incisions. As for cupping the legs, it visibly reduces fullness.
Because it draws blood from the whole body, it benefits from chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder; it promotes menstruation; it benefits from boils and abscesses; and it takes the place of phlebotomy of the saphenous veins and the veins of the ankles, except that it greatly weakens the body and causes fainting in most people.  The benefit of cupping the ankles is similar to the benefit of cupping the legs.
The method of applying cupping glasses is that the cupping glass is first placed empty and moderate suction is applied.  Do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but rather place them quickly and remove them quickly so that the humors may flow to the place evenly. Continue to repeat this until you see the area has reddened and opened.
When the redness of the blood appears, then you make an incision and gently resume suction. Then look at the condition of the bodies; if someone has loose flesh and porous skin, you should make only one incision, lest the place ulcerate.
Instruct the barber-surgeon to widen the incision and deepen it slightly, and to adjust the suction gently and with a light movement. If the blood is thick, then he should make two incisions; the first to open a path for the thin blood and its watery part, and the second to completely extract the thick blood.
If the blood is very turbid, repeat the incision a third time to achieve the goal. In short, if we want to remove a small amount of blood, one incision is sufficient; if we want to remove a large amount of blood, we make more incisions, especially if we estimate that the blood is thick.
It is necessary to make a deep incision, and the moderate limit of the incision is only the depth of the skin. What ointments and waters should be used when applying cupping glasses, and what the cupped person should avoid. As for someone whose skin is thick, hard, dry, and whose pores are tight,
Then you should anoint the places for the cupping glasses with opening, softening, and resolving ointments. If it is in the summer, then for example, with rose ointment, or violet ointment, or sweet almond oil, or pumpkin seed oil.
And if it is in the winter, then like daffodil ointment, or iris ointment, or chamomile ointment, or lily and the like. If the waste is thick and cold, then let the ointment be marjoram ointment, or balm ointment, or benzoin ointment, or rue ointment, and the like.
If the cupped person has wide pores and soft flesh, then the ointment should be avoided. These people should wash their cupping sites after cupping with rose water, or cold water, or gooseberry water, or pumpkin water, or chickweed water, and the like. As for someone whose blood is very moist,
he should wash his cupping sites with vinegar, or with water of myrtle and sumac, and the like. As for someone whose waste products are thick, he should wash his cupping sites with old wine, or with marjoram water, or a decoction of rue, or chamomile, and the like. Cupping should be avoided in the bathhouse and immediately after the bathhouse.
Rather, it should be done an hour or two after leaving the bathhouse. No one should sleep after cupping. What the cupped and bled person should take care of before and after cupping.
First, it must be observed whether the cupped or bled person is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation predominate in his blood. Then he should take cooling things like pomegranate and chicory with vinegar, lettuce, oxymel, and sherbet, and the like. And he should make his foods of small birds, lamb meats, stews and sour things, and the like.
And if his temperament is cold, then he should be given honeyed drinks and quince drinks, or seed oxymel.  And he should consume moderately aromatic wine, which is between old and new. And he should be instructed to eat little, and his food should consist of small birds, partridges, sparrows, and squabs, stewed. And the drink on the day of cupping and bleeding should be more than the food.
And it may be necessary to give some people at certain times some of the Faruq antidote, or musk medicine, or  shiltha before cupping and before or after bleeding, to strengthen the principal organs and to thin the blood. But it should not be given to those who are feverish.
As for cuppings which are without a condition, they are those which are placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, the region of the kidneys, and the hip joint; for these organs do not tolerate the condition being placed on them.  Rather, what is intended by them is either the drawing of blood from one organ to another.
Such as placing the cupping glass on the breasts in a case of epistaxis, or we use it to remove a cold humor which has settled in an organ, as when we place the cupping glass on the abdomen and navel; for it loosens the organ, warms it, and removes pain by resolving that humor.
And it may be placed on the kidneys if there is an obstruction or stone in them, for by the force of its drawing it may perhaps open the obstruction or remove the stone from its place.  And likewise it acts if it is placed on the liver and spleen in the case of a humor which disturbs them. And these cuppings may be used empty, by suction only.
And it may be used with fire, and it may be used filled with lukewarm water in cases of inflammation; and this is done by filling the cupping glass, and let it be large, with hot water alone, or with water in which some herbs suitable for that purpose have been boiled.
Then it is placed full on the affected area, held, and removed, and repeated several times according to need. And this is the picture of the cupping glass which is used with fire:
Its mouth should be the width of two open fingers, as we have depicted, and its depth half a span; and in its side, about the middle of it, there should be a small hole about the size of a needle's opening. It is made of Chinese copper or yellow copper, with a thick edge.
Smooth, even, and polished, lest it injure the member when placed upon it; and in its middle there should be a transverse groove of copper or iron, where the lighted candle is placed. And this cupping glass may be made larger than we have described, or smaller, according to the diseases.
And the use of it is as follows: for the cupping glasses of children and thin people are not the same as those of men and stout-bodied people. As for the method of applying this cupping glass with fire to the member, it is that you prepare a burning wick of tightly-twisted linen or a small candle of pitch and place it on the middle of the solid rod which is in the middle of the cupping glass.
So that the fire may ascend upwards towards the bottom of the cupping glass, lest it burn the patient's body. Then it is placed on the member, and the finger on the hole which we have mentioned; so that when we have held the cupping glass as long as we need, we remove the finger.
And the vapor will come out through that hole, and the cupping glass will adhere to the place. Then you prepare the wick in the same way and replace it if you need to. As for the cupping glass which is used in the disease of the shosha with water, it has neither a solid rod nor a hole.
But it is used by being filled with water and placed on the member only. And this is its form:
And this cupping glass, the larger it is to hold more water, the better it is.
Chapter ninety-seven.
On suspending the leech.
Leeches are used mostly in cases where cupping glasses cannot be applied, either because of the small size of the member, such as the lip, gum, and the like, or because the member is devoid of flesh, such as the finger, nose, and the like.
And the method of using them is that you should choose leeches which are in fresh, clean water, free from impurities, then leave them for a day and a night in fresh water until they are hungry and there is nothing left in their bellies. Then the body is first purged by bloodletting or cupping, then the affected member is wiped.
Until it reddens, then it is placed upon it; and when it is full, it falls off. And if it is possible to suck the place with a cupping glass, it is more effective; otherwise, wash the place with vinegar, then with plenty of water, and massage and squeeze it. If the bleeding continues after the leech falls off, and this is a trickle, then let a linen cloth be soaked in cold water.
And place it on top until the trickle stops. If the bleeding is profuse, sprinkle upon it powdered vitriol or alum or the like of astringents until the bleeding stops, or place on the affected area halves of peeled lupins.
And leave it until the lupin sticks to the place, for then the bleeding will stop. And it is necessary, if it is needed to reapply leeches, not to apply those leeches if others are available.  If the leeches refuse to attach, then let the place be wiped with fresh blood.
Or insert a needle into the place until some blood comes out, then apply it; for when it senses some blood, it will stick in place. If you wish to make it fall off, sprinkle upon it something of aloes or salt or ashes; then it will fall off in place.
Chapter Three.
On setting (bones).
This chapter also includes what is needed in the art of medicine, which is the setting of fractures and dislocations occurring in bones.
Know, O my sons, that this chapter is claimed by the ignorant among physicians and the common people, and by those who have never examined the books of the ancients in it, nor read a letter from them.
For this reason, this art of knowledge has become extinct in our country, and I have never encountered anyone skilled in it at all. And I have only acquired what I have acquired from my long reading of the books of the ancients and my eagerness to understand them, until I extracted the knowledge of that from them, then I adhered to experience and practice throughout my life.
And I have drawn for you from that in this chapter all that my knowledge encompasses. And my experience has followed upon it, after I have brought it close to you and freed it from the complexities of prolixity, and shortened it to the utmost, and clarified it to the utmost, and depicted for you in it many pictures of the instruments which are used in it.
For it is from the increase of explanation, as I did in the preceding two chapters, and there is no strength except in God, the Most High, the Almighty.
Chapter One.
In it are summaries and generalities concerning the matter of fractured bones, and it is necessary to present them before we begin to mention the fractured and dislocated members one by one.  Therefore it is necessary that we mention at the beginning of this chapter a summary of speech and sections. You will first be compelled to understand them and stand upon their truth, you and whoever else is eager to learn this noble craft.
So I say that when a fracture, dislocation, contusion, or fall occurs to someone, it is necessary to hasten first to bleed him or purge him, or both. Unless something prevents it, such as weakness of strength, or if the one to whom this has happened is a child or an old, feeble man, or if the time is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then restrict his diet to cold vegetables and the meat of birds and gazelles; and forbid drinks, coarse meats, and overfilling with food, and every food that fills the veins with blood, until the swelling is safe and the pouring of matter to the place is not expected.
Then let the patient return to his first regimen, which was his custom.  When the broken bone begins to knit, then the patient should nourish himself with foods that provide much thick, strong nourishment, containing viscosity, such as pottage, rice, heads, necks and cow tripe. And eggs, soft fish, and thick drinks, and the like; for by this regimen the consolidation of the fracture will be quicker and better. And know that broken bones, if they are in strong men and old men, cannot ever unite and knit to their first nature.
Because of the dryness of their bones and their hardness. And it may unite and knit, what was of bones in the extreme of softness, in the manner of the bones of small children. But nature grows on the broken bone from all its sides something resembling glue, in it is thickness that adheres to it and strengthens it until it binds one part to another.
And binds one part to another until it comes to the utmost strength and firmness as it was at first, until nothing hinders him from his actions.
For this reason it is necessary to give the patient foods which contain firmness, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said. And know that fractures differ in their kinds according to the difference of the members, for a fracture of the bone of the leg differs from a fracture of the bone of the head, and a fracture of the bone of the chest differs from a fracture of the bone of the back.
And so are all the other members, each differing from another. And we shall come to the mention of each kind of fracture, explained in its chapter, detailed from the others. And the kind of fracture of the bone may also differ in itself.
For it may be its fracture a splintering without fragments being produced in it, and it may be its fracture along the length of the bone and its fracture may have fragments and superfluous parts separated and unseparated. And the fracture may be with a wound and perforation in the skin, and the fracture may be a slight crack.
And for each kind there is a special method in its setting, as will be mentioned in detail in its place. And of what makes known the fracture of a bone is its crookedness, and its swelling, and its appearance to the sense, and its roughness when you touch it with your hand.
Then when there is not in the place an apparent crookedness, nor roughness, nor do you feel upon touching the bone a disturbance, nor does the patient feel much pain, then there is not a fracture, but it may be a sprain, or a slight fracture, or a slight crack.  Therefore, it is not proper to move it at all by pulling or probing.
But apply to it of the medicines, the mention of which will come after a while, what suits the place, then bind it gently. And know that if the bone is broken and cracked into two without fragments being produced in it except that each part has moved from its companion.
Then it is necessary for you to hasten at once to setting it and aligning it before a hot swelling occurs to it.  If a hot swelling occurs to it, then leave it for days until the hot swelling subsides, then set it in whatever way you are able with gentleness and skill.
And know that its setting and alignment is easier than a bone in which fragments have occurred. Then it is bound as will be mentioned.  If there are fragments in the bone, then it is necessary to pull the broken member from both sides, whether hand or foot.
Either with your hands if the member is small, or with two ropes, or with a rope and hand. And let your placing of the member be on a level surface in its natural shape, until when the broken part of the bone is extended, then return those fragments to their places in whatever way you are able with skill and gentleness.
And strive with all your effort that you do not cause the patient pain or suffering by your action, and strive with all your effort to join one of the bones to its companion in the best manner. And it is necessary at that time that you touch and feel them with your hand; if you see there something contrary, correct it and align it to the extent of your ability.
And beware of strong pulling and forceful probing as many ignorant people do, and they often cause by their actions a hot swelling or inflammation in the member, as I have witnessed from their actions repeatedly.
Then after the setting, perfection, and binding, keep that member still and quiet, and warn the patient not to move it while awake or asleep, and when he turns and is restless, and when he defecates, and in all his movements to the best of his ability, and let him seek that the position of the member be a position in which he is safe from pain.
And that is because whenever he feels pain or suffering in the position of the member, he should move it to another position in which he does not feel pain, and he should strive that this position be level and straight.
Lest crookedness occur in the member if it is set, and as for the manner of binding the broken member, it is as I describe to you. Know that broken members differ in their smallness and largeness and their shapes; those which are small, such as the arm, finger, forearm and the like.
Then it is necessary that the bandages of the rags be soft and gentle, and those which are thick, like the thigh, back, and chest, then it is necessary that the bandages be wide and firm, for the wide bandage is necessary for the large member and tightens it from every side with equal tightness so that no defect intervenes.
Then, after you have finished the setting, you begin to apply the appropriate plaster. With a soft, pliable bandage on the place of the fracture, then you begin to wrap the bandage on the place of the fracture itself three or four times, according to what the member requires, and you tighten your hand slightly with the bandage, then you take it to the upper side of the place of the fracture.
With less tightening than your tightening of the broken place, then you spread the wrapping a little from the place of the fracture and loosen the tightening little by little until you take from the correct place something suitable, then you take another bandage and wrap it also on the broken place.
Wrappings, then you take the wrapping to the lower side of the fracture, and let your action in tightening and loosening the wrapping be as we mentioned in the first upper wrapping, then you place between the wrappings of the soft bandage or rags what evens out the crookedness of the fracture.
If there is crookedness, otherwise do not put anything in it, then you wrap another bandage around it, then you set on these wrappings the firm splints at once if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, then apply what soothes that inflammation and removes the swelling, and leave it for days, then tighten the splints on it then; and let the splints be from broad, carved, skillfully prepared halves of reeds, or let the splints be from sieve wood.
which are made from pine or palm stalks or willow or boxwood and similar things of what is available from that, and let the width of each splint be in this manner and this shape exactly.
Except that the splint which is placed on the fracture itself should be slightly thicker and wider than the other splints, and as for the length of the splint, it is made according to what suits the broken limb in its size and smallness.  Image of the splint:
Then you tighten the splints with another bandage, according to your first tightening exactly, then you bind from above with firm threads according to what we mentioned of the tightening, which is that your tightening should be more on the place of the fracture. And the farther it is from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And the threads should be moderate in thickness and thinness, and let them be of wet linen especially, because if the threads are thick, as I have seen done by the ignorant who make their threads from twisted linen strips, this is a great mistake.
Because the tightening with them becomes excessive, and thin threads are also unsuitable because you do not achieve with them the tightening you want, and there should not be less than a finger between the splint and the splint.
If the patient is harmed by the edges of the splints after tightening in the healthy areas, then place under them soft tow or fluffy wool so that nothing harms him from that. As for if the fracture is with a wound and a rupture in the skin occurs, we will mention that separately.
And know that not every broken limb should be tightened with splints from the first day, and that if the limb is large, then the splints should not be placed on it except after five or seven days or more, depending on your assurance that hot swelling will not occur.
And this is the description of the bandages which the ancients used to bind with, which are placed on fractures, dislocations, and sprains; a description of a general, brief bandage which sets fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women, because it is something which is not overcome by heat or cold.
And it is that you take from the dust of the mill, which is the essence of the flour which adheres to the walls of the mill during the movement of the millstone, then knead it as it is without sifting it, with egg white, and make its kneading neither thick nor thin, then apply it.
The description of another bandage which sets fractures, dislocations, and sprains:  Take of mastic, leadwort, acacia, orris root, gum tragacanth, and sugar, ten drachms of each; and of myrrh and aloes, five drachms of each; and of the lote tree, twenty drachms; and of Armenian or Roman clay, twenty drachms. Pound everything and mix it with the water of the lote tree or with egg white if the temperament of the patient is hot; then use this bandage, for it is good in composition, sets broken bones quickly, and is suitable for most people because of its moderation.
The description of another bandage which is also useful for fractures and sprains: Take gum tragacanth, mastic, and white lead, ten drachms of each; myrrh and aloes, five drachms of each; acacia, six drachms; Armenian clay, twenty drachms. Pound everything finely, sift it, and knead it with water or egg white, and use it.
The description of a bandage for joints and bones which have shifted from their places, and which alleviates the pain affecting them, and for bone fissures and fractures: Take carded wool and dip it in vinegar and cooked oil, and apply it to the affected area. This bandage does not have the power to set (bones).
But it is excellent in alleviating hot swelling and relieving pain in particular. The description of another bandage which sets broken bones: Take the leaves of the black fig and the leaves of wild poppy, and pound them together and apply them moist. The description of another brief bandage used when a bone fracture is healing.
And if you want to resolve the rest of the swelling, take of the root of white lead, chamomile, violet flowers, and crushed acorns, one part of each; pound everything together and knead it with white of egg if the member is not hot; but if it is hot, then knead it with coriander water or with water and use it.
The description of another bandage stronger in resolving than the first, used when a hard swelling occurs during the healing of a bone: Take of the root of white lead, flaxseed, fenugreek, rosemary, germander, violet flowers, and chamomile, one part of each.
Pound everything together and knead it with vinegar or with fresh water or with white of egg, all according to the heat of the member and the stillness of its fever. As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that it should be placed on the broken member when setting it.
Except for the cerate made from wax and oil only, and he described it as being moderate between thickness and thinness. As for Galen, he saw that things which contain dryness with some heat, such as myrrh, aloe, frankincense, and the like that we have mentioned, should be placed on the broken member when setting it.
The description of a bandage which is useful for weakness and pain: Take of myrrh, alum, human hair that has been cut or bird feathers, white lead, and salt, equal parts; pound, sift, and knead, and bandage with it. As for the amount that the bandage should remain...
Then it is loosened; that is to say, you should look: if there is no pain or itching in the afflicted part, and the broken bone has not moved from its place, do not loosen it for many days. But if there is severe itching or distressing pain or swelling in the place.
Then hasten to loosen it at once and do not delay that at all, and remove the bandage from it. Then take a soft cloth or a wet sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the place with it until the itching and pain subside, then let the member rest for an hour.
Then apply to it carded wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose oil, and bind it on for a night, until you are safe from hot swelling, and the swelling of the member subsides, and its pain goes away. Then return it to gentle tightening and a light bandage, and do not tighten it as you did at first.
And treat it gently until it heals.  If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and inflammation, and all the symptoms have gone completely, and you need a bandage and tightening, then renew it as you did at first, the same. If nothing of what we have mentioned occurs in the member.
Then do not loosen it except after three, four, five, or seven days; and it may be left for twenty days; all this according to what appears to you of the condition of the member, as we said, until the fracture is set and the flesh begins to knit around it.
Then also increase the tightening more than your first tightening, and also increase the nourishment of the patient more than what was mentioned before. If you see that the place of the fracture has dried out and become thinner than it should be, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it.
Then bathe it with lukewarm water each time you loosen it, and let this be every three days, and lessen the tightening a little. For by this action, nourishment will flow to the member, and it will heal quickly. As for what the ignorant among bone-setters do, of breaking the member again,
if it is not set properly at first, and is set crookedly, that is a mistake in their action and a great deception. And if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and acted upon it, and I have seen no trace of it at all from any of them. And the correct thing is not to do it.
Chapter Two.
Concerning accidental fractures in the head.
The types of accidental fractures in the head are numerous, their forms varied, and their causes diverse.  Some fractures are caused by a sword blow, and this may either sever the bone entirely until it reaches the membrane beneath the bone. As a wedge does in wood.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called a wedge fracture.  Alternatively, the sword may cut some of the bone and sever only its surface, without the cut penetrating to its end; this type of fracture is called an absolute extraction.  The wound in these two fractures may be either large or small.
Some fractures are comminuted or contused, caused by a blow with a stone, or a fall onto a stone or something similar. This fracture may be penetrating, nearly reaching the membrane under the bone, or it may be on the surface of the bone.
The wound in these two fractures is also either large or small.  Some fractures are hidden in the bone, fine as a hair, being a slight crack.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called hairline.  Another fracture is caused by a fall or a blow from a stone or the like, which drives a section of the bone inwards, creating a depression in the area, similar to what happens to a copper pot when struck, causing a portion of it to cave inward.
This occurs most often in the soft bones of the head, such as the heads of children. All these types of fractures may have loose or non-loose fragments. We will discuss the treatment of all of these in their proper place, and we will identify all these types of fractures by examining them.
And examining them with probes and removing the corrupted flesh from them. As for the hairline type, it is identified by examining the bone, wiping it, and applying ink; the fracture will appear black. As for the treatment of the fracture, first look at the patient's symptoms.
If you see symptoms that clearly indicate danger, such as vomiting bile, convulsions, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, sunken eyes and redness, and similar symptoms, do not approach the patient or treat him, for death is inevitable with these symptoms in most cases.
If you see symptoms that do not alarm you and you hope for his recovery, then proceed with his treatment.  If the injured person comes to you immediately after the injury, and this is during the winter…
you should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case.  If it is in the summer, you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane under the bone is corrupted and those symptoms we described appear.
If the fracture of the bone has reached the membrane covering the brain, and there is crushing and bruising, you should cut away the crushed and bruised part as I will describe to you.  This is to shave the head of the injured patient.
And uncover the bone in whatever way is possible for you, and according to the shape of the wound and what is easiest for the patient. If, when you uncover the bone, you find bleeding or hot swelling, treat that as necessary.
This is to pack the area with cloths soaked in a potion and rose oil, and cauterize the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding stops. Then you begin to rasp away and remove the bone, which can be done in one of two ways.
The first method is to cut the bone with a fine saw with a narrow blade.  This is its image:
Then you use another saw, slightly wider than the first. This is its image:
Then you also use another saw, wider still than the second. You should have several saws of different sizes, some wider and some shorter than others, and their edges should be extremely sharp.  Let them be made of Indian steel or good quality steel.
Use gentle pressure on the saw so as not to shake the head and injure it. If the bone is strong and solid, it is necessary to drill holes around it before using the saws, with the drills called non-penetrating drills.  They are called non-penetrating drills.
Because they do not go beyond the skull bone to what is behind it, since the drill has a round edge below its sharp head, similar to a ring or small circle, preventing it from penetrating and going beyond the thickness of the bone.
You should have many of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of the bone, so that for every skull you have a drill whose sharp point is appropriate in length and shortness to the thickness of that skull. This is an image of three types. Of the drills: large, medium, and small.
As for how to drill around the fractured bone, you place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then you move the drill to another place, making the distance between each hole about the thickness of an awl or similar.
Then cut between each two holes with the cutters, doing so with the utmost gentleness as we have said, until you remove the bone either with your hand or with another instrument prepared for that purpose.
Such as rasps and delicate forceps. You should be very careful that the drill or cutter does not touch anything of the peritoneum.  If the bone is scraped clean and freed from the peritoneum, if it was attached to it, and you have removed it, then you should cleanse it.
And smooth the roughness remaining on the bone with another instrument similar to the cutter, except that it should be finer and more delicate than the other cutters. If anything remains of small bones and splinters, take them gently with the instruments you have.
Then treat the wound afterwards with lint and the ointments which will be mentioned. As for the other method of treatment, it is a very easy method, far from danger; Galen mentioned it and praised it greatly. This is what he says: First, you should begin by uncovering the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and obvious, until, when you have uncovered that part, you place under it the tip of this lenticular cutter, and this is its image:
Its lenticular part should be smooth so that it does not cut anything, and its sharp part should be on its sides extending lengthwise, as you see, so that the lenticular part rests against the peritoneum and the sharp edge of the cutter is against the bone; then strike the cutter from one side.
With a small mallet, until you cut all the bone gently as it rotates, and you are safe from the membrane, with nothing frightening happening to it at all, even if the surgeon were the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he were sleepy.
If anything of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some parts of the bone, scrape it off with the tip of the lenticular cutter itself and carefully remove it, for it will be removed without harm or fear.
But if the bone fracture has not penetrated to the membrane, and what is broken of the bone is its surface, and roughness and small fragments remain in it, then you should remove that roughness and extract those fragments with fine forceps, several of which have been made of different sizes, so that you can use the most appropriate and suitable ones for each place, according to what the work requires.
