And the same broken bone and its shape.  You should first use the widest of those forceps to clean the bone, then use a finer one, and continue to do so successively until you come to use the finest and thinnest of them all.
As for the other small, hair-like cracks and slight fractures, you should use for each one a treatment according to what leads to its healing; this is something that is not hidden from anyone who has the least skill in this art and has understood what we have written and explained about larger fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you remove the bone, you should take a linen cloth the size of the wound and dip it in rose oil and place it on the mouth of the wound, then take another folded or triangular cloth. And dip it in syrup and rose oil and place it on the first cloth, and do this as gently as you can, so as not to weigh down the peritoneum, then use a wide bandage on top and do not tighten it except to the extent that it only holds the wound.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are sure the hot swelling has subsided, then unwrap it and afterwards use medicines which have a drying effect, such as iris roots, crushed costus, powdered spikenard, zedoary, and the like.  You should make a powder from these medicines.
And sprinkle it dry on the wound. In short, use a medicine that cleanses without stinging, and make every effort during your treatment of the wound to keep it clean, free from grease and dirt, and do not let pus collect in it at all.
Because if pus collects on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts and putrefies it, causing great harm to the patient. This may occur in the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if treatment is neglected.
If blackness appears on its surface, and the patient shows the symptoms we have mentioned, know that he will inevitably die. However, if the blackness is caused by a medicine applied to it, and it was in the nature of that medicine to cause that blackness,
then you should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, mix them well, and smear a cloth with them, then apply it to the peritoneum and treat it with the appropriate types of treatment until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the reduction of a fractured nose.
Know that only the upper two parts of the nose, or one of them, can be fractured, because they are bone; the lower part is cartilaginous and does not fracture, but rather is subject to bruising, bending and flattening.
If one of its parts is fractured, you should insert your little finger into the nostril and set the fracture from the inside with your index finger and thumb from the outside, until you restore the nose to its natural shape.  Let this be done gently, taking care that your actions do not cause the patient pain.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and the finger cannot reach it, it should be set with the end of a slightly thick probe. If the fracture is on both sides, do likewise.  And let it be set on the first day of the fracture.
If possible, otherwise after seven or ten days when the acute inflammation has subsided. Then insert a pledget of linen into the nostril if the fracture is on one side, or two pledgets if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the pledgets be thick enough to fill the nostril. Some early bone-setters mentioned that the pledgets should be moistened with fat and changed every day, but I do not think so; rather, the pledgets should be moistened with egg white mixed with ground barley.
Then leave the pledgets until the bone sets and the cartilage hardens.  Goose quills, wrapped in soft linen, may be inserted into the nose at the site of the pledgets; this will provide stronger support for the fractured nose and prevent the patient from being unable to breathe.
This is not necessary; you may do it or you may use pledgets. If acute inflammation develops in the nose during treatment, apply a compress of  kyrios  or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or some diachylon ointment.
If no acute inflammation develops, dress the outside with fine semolina and powdered costus mixed with egg white, and then apply a soft bandage. Do not bind the nose at all if the nasal bones are only slightly fractured.
Or if they are shattered, you should incise, remove the fragments with an appropriate instrument, then suture the incision and treat it with appropriate ointments that promote healing. If a wound occurs inside the nose, treat it with pledgets and use lead tubes. Until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On the setting of lower jaw fractures.
If the lower jaw is fractured, and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, then if the fracture is only external and not broken in two and is not depressed inwards, its identification is easy.
If the fracture is in the right side, then the index finger of the left hand should be inserted into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is in the left side, then the index finger of the right hand should be inserted and gently lift the prominence of the fracture from the inside outwards.
With your other hand outside the bone, control and straighten it. If the fracture of the jaw is broken into two, then traction should be applied from both sides to straighten it until it is set. If a displacement has occurred in the teeth.
Or if they are separated, then tie what you expect to remain with a gold, silver, or silk thread. Then place a pledget on the fractured jaw, then place a folded cloth over the pledget, and then place over the cloth a tight, large splint, or a piece of leather the length of the jaw. Then bind it from above according to what seems suitable to you and agrees with its setting, so that it does not become dislodged, and instruct the patient to remain still and quiet, and give him soft liquids as food. If you think that something has changed in its appearance in any way, then hasten to unbind it on the third day.
Then bind it from above according to what seems suitable to you and agrees with its setting, so that it does not become dislodged, and instruct the patient to remain still and quiet, and give him soft liquids as food. If you think that something has changed in its appearance in any way, then hasten to unbind it on the third day. Then repair the dressing with mill dust mixed with egg white or semolina flour after removing the pledget, and place a soft pad over the dressing. As long as the dressing adheres and the condition of the bone does not change, leave it.
Do not unbind it until it heals and the fracture consolidates; this fracture often consolidates in three weeks. If a hot swelling appears during that time, use what we have repeatedly mentioned to calm it until the swelling subsides.
But if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, look: if a bone splinter or splinters have separated from the bone, gently remove those splinters with whatever instrument suits you for removing them. If the mouth of the wound is narrow, widen it with a scalpel as much as you need.
Then, when you have removed those splinters and none remain, stitch the wound if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for it. And suture the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the treatment of a fractured clavicle.
The clavicle most often fractures anteriorly at the acromial end, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways: either it breaks into two without splintering, which is easiest to set;
or splintering occurs in the fracture, which is more difficult to set; or the fracture may be accompanied by a wound. If the fracture is without a wound, the treatment is to have two assistants, one to hold the arm on the side of the fractured clavicle, the other to extend the neck toward the other side.
Then set the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, and there is no prominence or deformity. If you need more extension, you should place a ball of rags or wool under the patient's armpit.
The size of the ball should suit your needs. Extend and elevate the clavicle and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is set properly. If you cannot pull the end of the clavicle outward because it is deeply impacted, the patient should lie on his back.
A moderately sized pillow is placed under his shoulder, and the assistant presses his shoulder downward until the impacted clavicle rises. Then set and align the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the clavicle is broken and moving, you should incise over it and remove the fragment gently. If the fragment is embedded in the bone, try to cut it with one of the instruments prepared for this purpose. After placing under the clavicle the instrument that protects the pleura, which is an instrument of wood or iron, and this is its image:
It resembles a spoon without a handle.  Its width should be according to the size of the bone, and its length according to what is convenient for the procedure. Let it have two ends, as you see; one end wider and the other narrower.
If the wound made when removing the bone fragment is wide and you are certain there is no risk of inflammation, close the wound edges with sutures. If the wound is small or you fear inflammation, treat the wound with lint and pledgets according to the size of the incision.
If inflammation develops, wet lint in rose oil and vinegar or syrup and apply it. As for setting a bone without incision or wound, apply a plaster made from mill dust and egg white. Place a soft pad over it, then place a ball under the armpit if necessary. Then take a very long bandage, about a cubit wide, and apply a folded pledget over the pad and plaster.  Then take a splint of thin wood. Let its width be three fingers and its length the same, then wrap it in a cloth. Then insert that cloth with the splint into the part of the bandage that lies over the fracture, then tighten the bandage over the fracture as you would wrap it. And pass it over the neck and under the sound armpit and under the affected armpit, and repeatedly pass it to each side, as you see fit, so that the binding firmly fixes the fracture, which is not hidden from you.
The whole point is that the splint does not move from the broken bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient every day; whenever the bandage loosens and you see that the splint has moved, correct it and tighten the bandage. Then have the patient sleep on his back, and place a small pillow under his armpit at night.
So that his arm is raised from his side, thus raising the clavicle fracture with the raising of the shoulder, or bind his arm to his neck, and do not untie the bandage unless an incident occurs in the area such as itching or swelling, for twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see a reason for it. And leave it bandaged until the clavicle fracture heals and consolidates, and it becomes stronger; this takes about twenty-eight days. And in some people it may be less than that.
Chapter Six.
On setting a fractured humerus.  The humerus rarely fractures in its wide part; rather, it fractures at its extremities.  If a part of it is fractured or it is fractured in its middle, this is known by touch, and the method of setting is according to the nature of the fracture.
Set it and restore it to its natural shape by every means you can, then apply to the area mill dust with egg white and soft tow, and place over it a pad of folded cloth; then apply a broad splint of wood.
Thin enough to encompass the whole shoulder or slightly wider. If, under the splint, there is a prominence in any part of the shoulder, level that prominence with soft tow so that the splint lies evenly, then bind it above with a long bandage.
Bind it firmly and ensure that the splint does not move from its place. Examine the bandage every day; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint if it has moved from its place. Let the patient lie on his sound side. The shoulder heals in twenty [days].
days or twenty-five days.  When this time is completed, unbind the bandage, for you are then safe, for it is among the bones which are not to be feared for shattering or displacement. If a splinter of bone protrudes and is pressing under the skin, incise over it and extract it. And do what was mentioned in the fracture of the clavicle to calm the hot swelling, if anything of that occurs.
Chapter Seven.
On the treatment of a fractured rib.
The rib may be fractured in its middle, though this rarely happens; but its ends are more often fractured and shattered.  Among its symptoms, if its middle is fractured, is that it inclines downwards, and there is intense pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing.
And perhaps blood is expelled, and the depression in the broken bone is apparent and not hidden from the touch. Its treatment is that the patient lies on his back, and a pillow is placed between his shoulders, then his shoulders are pressed down, and the ribs are gathered together by the hands from both sides, and he is gently straightened in every way that is possible.
And continue until the shape of the bone is restored as it should be, then apply a bandage and a splint, and place over it a splint made from a thin, light board of willow or elm, or something similar, after wrapping it in cloth. Then gently bind it to the fractured bone lest it shift, and pass the bandage around to the back several times, and tighten it firmly, then check the bandage at all times, and whenever it loosens, tighten it.
And if necessity dictates its removal because of suppuration that occurs in the area, or pain, or swelling, then proceed immediately to its removal, and take off the bandage and treat what has occurred, according to its treatment, then reapply the bandage if you see fit. And face [the patient] and keep him firmly bound until he heals.
Chapter Eight.
Concerning the setting of broken ribs. Know that fractures of the ribs occur in the thick parts which are next to the spine, and their extremities in front are subject to contusion because they are cartilaginous, and the knowledge of this is not hidden from the sense upon examination with the fingers.
And set it by straightening the fracture with the fingers in the best way possible, until the shape is as it should be, then bandage and bind the broken bone with a splint if it is needed. If the fracture of the ribs is inclined inwards, then intense pain occurs to the patient.
And it is depressed like the depression which occurs to one who has a puncture wound, because the bone depresses the diaphragm, and there also occurs to him shortness of breath, coughing and the ejection of much blood, and this is difficult to treat, and the ancients devised many stratagems for it.
And some of them said that it is necessary to give the patient foods which generate flatulence and wind so that the abdomen swells and expands, and the fracture is driven outwards. And we dislike this lest it be a confirmation of the occurrence of hot inflammation, if it has not occurred; and if it has occurred, then it increases it and confirms it.
And some of them said a cupping glass should be placed on the place, then sucked strongly, and this is more like the reasoning, except that it is feared that the cupping glass draws humours to the place in its weakness. And some of them said that it is necessary to cover the place with wool which has been dipped in hot oil.
And a pad is made between the ribs until it is full, so that the bandage is level when it is wrapped around in a circle. Then the patient is treated with the treatment of a puncture wound, with regard to food and medicine, if a severe matter burdens the patient which he cannot bear. And the bone was depressing the diaphragm with a harmful depression, and we feared for the patient; therefore it is necessary that we incise the place and uncover the broken rib, then we place under it the instrument which protects the peritoneum, which has been previously described, and we cut the bone gently and extract it.
Then we bring together the edges of the wound, if it is large, with stitching, and we treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot inflammation appears in the meantime, then act quickly, and wet pads in rose oil and place them on the place, and treat the patient with what calms the inflammation from the inside as well.
And he lies on the side on which sleep is easier for him until he heals.
Chapter Nine.
In the setting of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if a fracture affects them—and this rarely happens to them, and what most often happens to them is a contusion, and so too the vertebrae of the back—then if this happens to someone and you want to know whether it will heal or not heal, look; and if you see that his hands have become relaxed and numb and dead, and he is unable to move them or extend them or flex them, and if you pinch them or prick them with a needle he does not feel it and finds no pain in them, then know that in most instances he will not heal, and he is doomed.
But if he moves them and feels the pinching and pricking in them, then know that the marrow of the bone has been spared and that the patient will heal with treatment.  If the vertebrae of the back are similarly affected and you want to know whether it will heal or not,
look at his legs; and if you see that they have become relaxed and what happened in the hands has happened in them, then if he lies on his back, wind and feces pass involuntarily, and if he lies on his stomach, urine passes involuntarily, and if he lies on his back and wants to urinate, he is unable to do so, then know that he is doomed, so do not trouble yourself with his treatment.  If nothing of this happens to him, the matter is less serious, and the treatment of what has happened is that you seek to alleviate the hot swelling.
by placing on the injured vertebra rose oil alone, or with roasted egg yolks; apply this three times a day until the hot swelling subsides. Then apply one of the strengthening, drying poultices to the area, bind it tightly, and order him to rest and remain still.
He should not sleep except on the side where he feels no pain, until he is healed. If, at the time of the injury, a splinter or something has broken off from the bone, then it is necessary to incise the skin and remove that piece of bone. Then, if the wound is large, bring the edges together with sutures, and then treat with healing ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx, which is the end of the sacrum, is broken, then you should insert the thumb of the left hand into the rectum.
and set the broken bone with the other hand, as much as you can manage and the setting allows; then apply a poultice and splint if needed, and then bind it.
If you feel a broken splinter in it, then incise it and remove it, and treat the wound as described above until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the setting of a fractured hip.
Hip bones rarely fracture; but if they do fracture, it is in such a way that the ends are shattered and the bone is split lengthwise, leaning inwards.  The patient experiences pain at the site, swelling, and numbness in the leg on the side of the fracture.
Its treatment is that you pass your hand over it until you ascertain the nature of the fracture. If the fracture is only at the ends, then set that fracture according to whatever setting seems suitable to you, until its shape resembles its natural form.
If the fracture is lengthwise, or if it has leaned inwards, lay the patient on his stomach so that you may set the fracture.  Once you have set it, apply a bandage, then place a splint of wood or leather over it and bind it tightly.
Do not fear displacement of the fracture or the splint coming loose. Fill the hollows in the hips with something to make them even so that the binding is straight. Instruct the patient to lie on his back or on his sound side. If he develops a hot inflammation,
desist from setting it until the hot inflammation subsides. Apply something to soothe it as described above, then return to setting and binding it as appropriate. If there are splinters or shattered fragments of bone at the ends, they should not be removed.
They should not be touched, but smoothed from the outside as we have said, and the binding left in place until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On setting a fracture of the humerus.
The humerus is that which is between the elbow and the shoulder. If it is fractured, set it in one of two ways. One is to take a smooth, curved splint of moderate thickness, in this manner:
And you tie two bandages to its ends, then it is suspended from a high place, and the patient sits on a chair, then his broken arm is placed on the splint until his armpit is touching the middle of the curve of the splint, then something heavy is hung from above it.
Or a servant stretches it downwards, then the physician sets the fracture with his two hands until he reduces the fracture as it should be. The other method is that the patient lies on his back and his hand is suspended from his neck with a bandage.
Then you instruct two servants, one to hold above the fracture with his hands and the other to hold below it, each pulling in his direction. And if you wish the traction to be stronger, then tighten a bandage below the fracture and another above it, and each of the servants pulls in his direction.
If the fracture is near the shoulder joint, then the middle of the bandage should be under the armpit and the other end below the fracture towards the elbow.  Similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then the bandage should be in that place and on the elbow itself.
Then you set the fracture gently without violence, until the fracture is set as it should be and unites well. Then you bandage it if there is no hot swelling. If there is hot swelling, then leave bandaging it until the seventh day, and apply moistened wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then bandage it. The method of bandaging is to apply the compress to the fracture, then apply a bandage of new cloth over the compress, then bind the forearm to the arm itself, and place his hand open on his shoulder.
And you apply the cloth and bandage to the arm and forearm so that the forearm acts as splints, if nothing prevents you and nothing changes in the broken bone. If you fear that something will change, then use splints.
And that is to place on the fracture itself a splint that is wider and stronger than the other splints, and let there be the width of a finger between each splint. And let the length of the splints be according to the fracture, with an addition of three fingers on each side.
Then you bandage the splints with the bandage I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And that your bandaging should be tighter at the site of the fracture.
And the farther from the fracture, the less tight the bandage should be. If you see that placing the splints and bandaging as we said is suitable at the time of your setting the limb, then do so.
And if you fear hot inflammation, leave off the bandaging and splints until the seventh day, as we said. Then examine the bandage every three days, lest there be chafing or swelling at the site, or the nourishment be prevented from reaching the limb because of excessive tightness.  Amend all this as we have mentioned.
But if you are confident that none of this will occur, do not unbind the bandage except after many days.  The patient should lie on his back with his hand on his stomach, and a moderately full bolster of wool should be placed under the armpit.
And examine at all times, night and day, lest the shape of the broken limb be disarranged, or the bandage become loose.  Amend all this with your effort. And let the patient's diet be in the order we presented, that the food should be light at first, until the bone begins to knit.
Then it is proper to strengthen his diet. For it is the habit of the arm and leg to knit in forty days. Then it is proper to unbind and use the bath and treat with ointments suitable for this. If the fracture is severe and comminuted,
do not unbind the bandage until fifty days or two months.
Chapter Twelve.
In the setting of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, called the ulna. One is small, which is next to the thumb, and the other is large, which is situated beneath the small one from below. Perhaps the larger ulna is broken alone, or the smaller alone, or perhaps both together.
If the smaller upper ulna is broken, then its setting is easy and its healing is quicker. If the lower ulna is broken, then its fracture is bad and its healing difficult.  The worst is if both bones are broken. If the bone that is broken is the smaller upper ulna,
the physician, when setting it, should make its extension easy and gentle until he straightens it. If the larger ulna is broken, then he should make its extension firmer. And if both ulnas are broken, then he should make the extension much stronger.
And it is necessary that the shape of the hand, when it is set and extended, be placed on a pillow; and the thumb of the hand should be higher than all the fingers, and the little finger lower than the other fingers, and the patient should sit squarely upon himself.
And let the pillow be in front of him at the right height, lest the patient suffer hardship.
Then a servant extends the arm from below, either with his hand or with a bandage, and another servant extends it from above likewise. Then the physician straightens the bone until he restores it to the best shape he can. If there are fragments in the broken bone, then they are removed.
Return each fragment to its place to the best of your ability. If a fragment appears that is detached and is piercing the skin, and you have no hope of setting it, then incise it and extract it in the manner we described before. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, then it has been given a separate chapter.
Then take its treatment from there. If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of your setting, then apply a cloth with qirūṭī mixed with rose oil and white wax, and let it be moderate between thickness and thinness, and bind the cloth on it gently until the swelling subsides.
Then remove the qirūṭī and apply the prepared bandage of mill dust with egg white. Then apply the splints; and let the splint that is placed on the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger. And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most cases.
Whether the fracture was in one forearm or in both forearms together, then make your binding on the place of the fracture stronger and tighter. And whenever you move the binding upwards or downwards, make the binding somewhat looser, as was mentioned before at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, and not very hard. And let the thread with which it is bound be of special linen, moderate between thinness and thickness, as we described. And examine the limb and the binding after a few days.
If something happens that needs to be corrected, such as itching that appears in the limb, then it is necessary to bathe the limb with warm water until that itching subsides. And leave the limb unbound for a night so that it may rest, then resume the binding.
If the binding has become loose and the bone has shifted or something like that, then correct all of that with your effort. And look also, if the nourishment prevents reaching the limb because of excessive binding, then it is necessary to loosen it a little and leave it for a few days until nourishment flows to it, then bind it.
If nothing of what we mentioned happens to the patient, then it should not be undone except after twenty days or so. Then, hang the patient's hand to his neck, and let his arm be straight. And let him carefully avoid agitated movements, and let his sleep be on his back.
And know that this fracture of the arm heals in thirty days, or in thirty-two, and perhaps it heals in twenty-eight days; all that depends on the states of the temperaments and the states of strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the setting of a fracture of the hand and fingers.
Fractures rarely occur in the metacarpus and phalanges of the fingers; rather, contusions frequently occur.  So when a fracture or contusion occurs in the hand…
The patient should sit cross-legged, with a chair directly in front, level with him.
Then he places his hand upon it outstretched, and then the bonesmith straightens the broken bones, and the physician sets them until they are properly set. Then a bandage and splint should be applied, unless a hot swelling occurs. Then a splint is applied over it to fit the area.
And it is inserted into a soft rag. If the fracture is towards the bottom, towards the palm of the hand, then make a kind of ball from a rag and tell the patient to grasp it with his broken hand; then tighten it with a long rag.
And let the splint be of supple leather, so that it conforms to the skin with the bending of the whole hand, and tighten it as necessary. If the fracture is outward, then the splint should be placed above and another splint below in the hand so that the hand remains open and upright.
Then the bandage is applied as the hand rotates, and the fingers are interwoven with the bandage. If the fracture affects one of the finger joints, if it is the thumb, it should be straightened as necessary, then bandaged with the hand; and if you wish, make a small, upright splint to support the fracture.
And it should not move. If the fracture is to the other fingers, such as the middle, index, little, or ring finger, then straighten them and bind them with the next sound finger, or bind them all together, which is better, or place a small, upright splint on them.
As we said for the thumb, and examine during and after your setting for hot swelling; then counter it appropriately whenever anything of that occurs, as repeatedly described.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the setting of a fractured thigh.
The thigh bone frequently breaks and is perceptible to the touch because it moves forward and backward. Its setting is done by tightening a bandage above the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, the patient lying on his face.
Then each assisting bandage is extended to its side evenly. This is if the fracture is in the middle of the bone.  But if it is near the head of the femur, then it is necessary to tighten a soft bandage, either of wool or similar.
At the head of the femur towards the pubis so that the extension is upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.  Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, let the bandage be near the knee so that the extension is downwards. Then the physician straightens the bone with both hands until he restores it to its natural form.
And the bone unites well. Then it is necessary to apply the bandage and tightening, if a hot inflammation does not occur in the limb. But if inflammation occurs, leave it for some days until the hot inflammation subsides, then return to its treatment.
As for its tightening, it is necessary to wrap a firm, wide turban around the fracture two or three times, and what remains is left. Then the leg is twisted until the heel is at the origin of the buttock, and a long thread is passed between the thigh and leg near the knee from below, and the ends of the thread are brought up from both sides.
Then wrap what remains of the turban around the leg and thigh. Then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture itself, and place one splint on the leg bone. Then fill the gap between the thigh and leg with soft rags so that the tightening is even.
Then begin strong tightening from the middle at the site of the fracture, three or four turns. And the further the bandage is from the site of the fracture, let your tightening be less, gentler, and looser.
Then take the ends of the thread that you had passed between the thigh and leg, and tie with it the splints above. Then pass the ends of the thread downwards until you reach the heel of the foot, and with them also tighten the ends of the splints from the other side.
Lest the bandage shift from its place. Then leave the tightening on it as long as no inflammation, swelling, or puffiness, and the like, occurs in the limb. If any of that occurs, quickly loosen it and correct whatever occurred, as we have repeatedly mentioned.
If there is a protruding splinter in the bone, it should be set if possible. Otherwise, incise over it and extract it, and treat the wound as previously described until it heals.  This fracture of the thigh may be tightened without the leg being added to it with splints.
As we mentioned in the case of the humerus and forearm, except that its setting in this way does not result in lameness for the patient. But if it is set alone without the leg being added, the patient will inevitably be lame.
Know that the thigh heals in fifty days, or a little more or less, all according to the differences in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the fracture of the kneecap.
Fracture of the kneecap rarely occurs, but contusion occurs frequently. If a fracture does occur, it is either a fissure or a shattering into fragments, and this occurs with or without a wound.
You ascertain all this by touch, and you treat it by aligning the separated fragments with your fingers until they are joined and united, as far as the alignment, gentleness, and skill allow; then you apply a bandage and a circular splint if needed.
And you tighten the appropriate bandage above, then you treat all the conditions which we described in other fractures, such as hot inflammation and the like, by countering each symptom with what is suitable for it until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the treatment of fractures of the leg.
The leg has two bones, one thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the fibula; and they are subject to the same types of fracture as the bones of the arm. Therefore, its treatment is the same as the treatment of the arm, and the procedure is the same. If both bones are fractured, the leg is displaced in all directions; if the thinner bone is fractured, the leg is displaced forward; and if the thicker bone is fractured, and this happens in the lower part, then this is something that is not hidden from you.
So use traction and alignment, and apply splints accordingly, except that if the fracture of the leg is a severe one with many fragments, the traction should be less and gentler, and you should take great care in its treatment.
And in the treatment of the leg there is something in addition to that of the arm: when you have aligned the splints and finished all your work, take two splints of pine wood, the kind used for smoothing rooms, which are placed between the cracks of the boards, or they may be made from palm leaves or something similar.
Choose those that are of moderate thickness, not thin, and let their length be the length of the leg from the knee downwards. Then wrap each one with two layers of cloth along its length, and place one on one side of the leg and the other on the other side.
Let them extend from the knee to the bottom of the foot, then bind the splints in three places: at both ends and in the middle. By this means, the leg is prevented from tilting to the right or left, and it is well supported. A wooden gutter along the length of the leg may also be used.
It is placed in it to keep it from moving, and it is most necessary to do this in fractures when there is a wound, especially. Then examine the leg every day and treat it with great care regarding swelling, inflammation, or anything else. When anything of that sort occurs, counteract it appropriately until it heals.  The bone of the leg knits together in about thirty days, or thereabouts.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the fracture of the bones of the leg and toes.
As for the heel, fracture never occurs there at all. As for the bones of the leg, fracture may occur to them.  The toes also rarely fracture; rather, contusion occurs to them in most instances.
If a fracture occurs to the bones of the leg and you see that some of those bones have overlapped, then let the patient place his foot flat on the ground upright as when walking. Then you should place your foot on what has risen of those bones, then press down on it and align it.
Until they return to their places, then apply a bandage and splint from above, and place a small board with two flat ends under the sole of the foot.  Then bind the foot firmly after wrapping it with cloths and whatever else it needs.
Then if three or four days have passed, untie the bandage. You will find the bones straight, whether they were fractured or dislocated. The procedure, as you see, is as follows. As for if one of the toes is fractured, set it and align it according to what I described to you in the setting of the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the fractured toe along the length of the toe, and let it be slightly wider than it. Then place under the foot the board that I described to you and bind it firmly. If what was fractured of the bones was two or three or more,
then make for each toe a splint of its size, wrapped in soft cloth, and bind the board to the bottom of the foot.  Let each end of the board rest outside the sole of the foot to secure it well.
And you should not forget to attend to all that we mentioned to you in other fractures, from the symptoms that we mentioned, and treat each symptom as it should be.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the fracture of the woman's pubis and the man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, seat her cross-legged, then gently incline her backwards. Let her be held from behind, then let the midwife pack her vulva with cotton until it is full and becomes like a ball within her vulva.
Then the woman is shaken and her loins are raised little by little.  That cotton will come out until it is at the entrance of the vulva like a ball, and the fracture of the bone will return [to its place]. Then a pad is placed on her back. When she wants to urinate, the cotton is gently removed so that she may urinate.
And it is returned in the manner in which it was first inserted, then she returns to her first position.  She does this for seven days or so, then it will knit together. And if you wish, you may take a sheep's bladder, tie a tube to its mouth, and insert the whole bladder into her vulva.
Then blow strongly into the tube until the bladder inflates inside the vulva; then the fracture will return [to its place]. Then pack it with cotton and remain as we have described until it heals. As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, this is used in its setting.
And its setting is as we described for the hip bone.  The correct procedure for these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, especially for one who has some experience and understands this book well, will not be hidden from you, for the fracture itself will indicate to you, in most cases.
Understand the correct way to set and bind it. As for the penis, if it is fractured, take a goose's gullet, insert the penis into it, then place a bandage of cloth over it, bind it, and leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the setting of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
Whoever suffers a fracture with a wound, especially if the bone is large, like the thigh bone or the humerus or similar, should immediately be bled at the appropriate time if the conditions for bloodletting, as we have previously mentioned, allow it.
If the wound is bleeding, you should immediately stop it by applying powdered vitriol if nothing else is available. Then set the fracture that very day and do not delay it.
If no hot swelling occurs, then if a hot swelling does occur, delay setting it until the ninth day, until the hot swelling subsides, and do not touch it at all on the third and fourth days, for it will lead to bad consequences.
If the broken bone is protruding through the skin and exposed, you should try to gently reduce and align it with your hands with a slight pull. If you are not able to reduce and align it with your hands, reduce it with this instrument, which is made of iron and is seven or eight finger-widths long. And its width is according to the wound.
Therefore, the physician should have three or four of them, according to what he needs for treatment in each type of fracture.  Let them be rounded and slightly thick, so that they do not bend when pushed during use.
And let the tip be sharp, with a curve at its end, and let the upper part be thicker, and from its middle to the lower part much thinner.  This is its image:
And it is called in Greek *peron*, meaning a small lever.  Its sharp, curved end should be placed on the end of the protruding bone, and it should be pushed with it until the bone returns and is somewhat aligned, then align the ends of the fracture with each other.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grasp it well, then the end of that bone should be cut until the instrument can grasp it. If you are not able to reset the bone in the way we described at all, then cut it with a similar cut to those we mentioned.
Or saw it with one of the saws, however you can. Then remove any roughness and thin scales remaining on the bone. If, after resetting it, the patient experiences severe, persistent pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you are able to return it to its natural position, then do so, for you will greatly benefit the patient. When you have completed the setting of the bone, dip a cloth in a black astringent drink, especially if it is summer, and do not apply any ointment or anything containing fat to the wound.
Lest putrefaction and corruption occur in it. Then apply the splints when you have finished setting the bone, and leave the wound uncovered by cutting a hole in the bandages with scissors, the size of the wound.
And beware, with all caution, of tightening the wound with the fracture, for many ignorant physicians have done this, causing their patients either death, or gangrene, or tetanus. Let your tightening be gentle, loose, and contrary to the tightening of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or is a large wound, and you fear some of the bad symptoms we have described, and he feels disturbing pain in the place, then you should not apply splints, but make bandages for him from strong cloths in the place of the splints, and bind it with them.
If after a day or two you see that pus has begun to form in the wound, remove the cloth that you placed on it with the drink, then use lint and ointments with which we are accustomed to treat wounds, such as the four-ingredient ointment and the like. You should loosen the bandage.
And examine the wound every day, evening and morning, until it heals and is cured.  It is necessary to position the limb so that the pus flows downwards easily. If many days pass and the wound does not heal and the pus does not stop, then know that there are small fragments of bone.
Then you should probe the wound with a probe; whatever of those fragments is loose, remove and extract it; and whatever of them is not loose, and is causing pain and injuring the limb, remove and extract it by any means you can.
If the wound shows signs of gangrene, or ulceration, or another type of corruption and putrefaction, then you should counter each symptom with a treatment similar to that described in its chapter.
And what you should note and keep in mind is that if a large bone is broken and is sticking out of a limb, such as the femur, humerus, and similar large limbs, do not attempt to pull it or remove it. For death often results from such an action; rather, leave it until it putrefies, and perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see there is a place for treatment; otherwise, leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
In the treatment of complications which arise after some fractures.
This complication often arises after the healing of a fracture, especially one near the joints, and disfigures the limb and may prevent the limb from its natural function.
Observe; if the complication is fresh, use astringent medications such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, gum tragacanth, acacia, and the like. Take some or all of these and mix them with an astringent syrup, or with egg white, or with vinegar.
Apply it to the complication in a bandage and bind it tightly. Leave the bandage undisturbed for many days, then unbind it and reapply another until, God willing, the complication disappears. Or bind a tight lead plate to it.
For lead has the property of removing all that is superfluous in the limbs. If the complication has become hardened and severe, and necessity compels its removal, incise it from above and cut away the superfluous growth, or scrape it off with some scraper until it is gone, and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On the treatment of a fracture if it is set but the limb afterwards remains thin, contrary to its original nature.
If a bone fracture is set, but the limb remains thin and weak afterwards, this is due to many causes. One [cause] is either from excessive loosening and tightening of bandages, or from excessive tightening of the bandages such that nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, or from excessive and repeated rubbing, or from excessive movement at an inopportune time, or from a deficiency of blood in the patient’s body and weakness.
The treatment of this is to nourish the patient and enrich his body until his blood increases, and to use the bath and to make him happy and joyful and so on; then apply pitch to the limb, for the pitch draws much nourishment to it, or continue rubbing it with lukewarm water. until nourishment flows to it and it returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In the treatment of fractured bones which have been set crookedly and prevented from functioning properly.
If, after a limb has healed, a crookedness or a projection of the fractured bone, or a complication appears, and the appearance of the limb is thereby disfigured, unless the limb is prevented from its natural function; then it is not proper to accept the opinion of those who claim that the limb should be broken from the head, as many ignorant physicians and bone-setters used to do in our country.
And this action is greatly blameworthy, leading to great danger, the least of which is injury; but if the crookedness and complication are recent, then it should be softened with water in which emollient herbs have been cooked, such as mallow leaves and root, and rosemary, and the like.
And it should be bandaged with emollient bandages, like well-made diachylon, or the sap of mallow root should be taken and beaten with chicken fat and lard, and bandaged with it; or ripe figs should be taken and pounded with pigeon dung and the like of medicines which are called resolvents.
And sometimes the complication is resolved by constant gentle rubbing with the hands, and the movement of the limb in every direction at all times is employed; but if the crookedness is old and severe and hardened, and the necessity for its treatment with iron arises,
then it should be incised above and the connection of the bone loosened, and what remains of the complication or bone should be cut with delicate cuts, and gentleness should be employed therein with effort and care; then the wound should be treated as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
A dislocation is the displacement of a joint from its place, which hinders movement, disfigures the limb, and causes the patient severe pain and suffering.  If a dislocation occurs, one should immediately attempt to reduce it.
One should not delay at all, for if delayed, the area will swell and become inflamed, making reduction of the dislocation more difficult.  Therefore, one should not delay, nor move or stretch it while it is swollen, because this often causes the patient spasms and harmful pains.
But if this occurs, one should immediately bleed the patient, then wait until the swelling subsides somewhat, then bathe the limb in warm water and oil, then gently reduce it, and treat each limb as described in its appropriate section.
The chapters on dislocations are also arranged, as with fractures, from the upper body to the lower.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
It is rare for both jaws to be dislocated. Their dislocation occurs in one of two ways: either they are slightly displaced and relax a little, or they are completely dislocated and relax completely. toward the chest, so that the saliva of the patient flows and he cannot restrain it, nor can he close his jaw, and his tongue is impeded in speech.  But if its dislocation is slight, it often returns of its own accord very easily.
But if the dislocation is complete, it should be reduced quickly and not delayed at all.  A servant should hold the head of the patient, and the physician should insert the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside the mouth if the jaw is dislocated on one side.
or he should insert both thumbs if the jaw is dislocated on both sides, and the rest of his fingers from outside to adjust them. He should instruct the patient to relax and release his jaw to go to every side, while the physician adjusts the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its place.
If its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are involved, apply warm water and oil compresses until their reduction is facilitated. Do not delay their reduction at all, as we said. When they have returned and are straight, and the patient's mouth is closed and they are not relaxed, then apply compresses of cloth with a poultice made of wax, oil, and rose water.
Then bind it gently with a loose bandage, and let the patient sleep on his back with his head raised between two pillows, lest he move it to the right or left, and let him not attempt to chew anything, but let his food be soft gruel.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw is firmly set, then let him eat whatever he wishes, but let him do so gently and not strain to open his mouth when eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw is firmly set and healed.
If the reduction of the jaws is difficult, if they become dislocated at some time and do not return to their places, fevers and persistent headaches often result from this, and perhaps the patient's abdomen will become distended, and perhaps he will vomit pure bile. If you see this, then know that it is damaged.
And many die from this condition within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-five.
On the reduction of a dislocated clavicle and shoulder.
As for the clavicle, it does not dislocate inwards because of its connection to the chest; it may dislocate outwards, and this is apparent to the touch.  Its reduction is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms.
Then press the area firmly with your hand, and it will return. Then apply a bandage and supports and tighten them. As for its end which is next to the shoulder and connected to it, it rarely dislocates; but if it does dislocate, it should be reduced and set as we have described and as seems best to you.
Then apply a bandage and supports and tighten them, and instruct the patient to remain quiet and still until it heals. By this same treatment, the head of the humerus is also reduced if it leaves its place.
Chapter Twenty-six.
On the reduction of the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three ways: one, towards the armpit and downwards; the second, towards the chest; and it may also dislocate upwards towards the shoulder, but this is rare.
It does not dislocate backwards because of the shoulder blade, nor forwards because of the nerve; most often it dislocates and goes downwards towards the armpit, especially in those with little flesh, because it quickly exits and quickly enters in them.
But as for those whose flesh is abundant, it is otherwise, that is, it exits with difficulty and enters with difficulty.  And sometimes a blow or a fall happens to some people, so the shoulder swells with a hot swelling, and they think it has dislocated. It is necessary to examine this until you ascertain its truth.
Then you proceed to its treatment.  And the dislocation is recognized if it is downwards towards the armpit, for if you compare the dislocated shoulder with the sound shoulder, you will find a clear difference between them; and you will find the head of the shoulder in it twisted, and under the armpit, on palpation, the head of the shoulder like an egg.
And the patient cannot raise his hand to his ear nor move it in all its movements.  Similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, you will find that clearly evident on palpation, not hidden.
And this dislocation is easily reduced if it is recent or if the patient is a child.  Its reduction is that a servant raises his hand upwards, then you place your thumbs under his armpit and raise the joint forcefully upwards to its place, and the servant raises his hand and extends it upwards.
Then he lowers it downwards, and it returns quickly. If it does not return by what we have mentioned, and the dislocation has been for many days, then the patient should bathe in hot water and use things that relax and soften, such as cooking the root of marshmallow, fenugreek, and rue in water and using it.
Then the patient lies on his back, and you place under his armpit a ball of wool which is moderate between soft and hard. Then the physician places his heel on the ball and grasps the patient's hand with his hand and extends it downwards.
And he pushes the ball with his foot, then it returns to its place. And if you wish, reduce it in this way: you bring a taller man than the patient and position him on the side, and he puts his shoulder under the patient's armpit and raises his armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. And another servant pulls the patient's hand downwards towards his belly. If the patient is light, then something else should be hung on him to weigh him down, then the jaw returns immediately.  It may also be reduced in another way.
And that is to fix in the ground a long piece of wood, its head round in shape like the pestle of a mortar, neither thick nor thin. Then it is placed under the patient's armpit after placing a soft rag on the head of the wood, and the patient stands along the length of the wood.
Then you extend his hand downwards from the other side, and he also extends his body from the other side forcefully, then the joint returns to its place quickly. If it is difficult to reduce it by all that we have mentioned, then use this treatment: you take a piece of wood about two cubits long. And its width is about four fingers, and its thickness is about two fingers. It has a round head to facilitate its entry into the depth of the armpit in this manner:
Then a soft rag is tied to the round head so that the wood does not hurt the patient. Then it is placed under the armpit, and the whole hand and arm are extended on the wood downwards, and the wood is tied to the upper arm, forearm, and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a horizontal beam. The arm is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is left hanging from the other side. Then the joint enters immediately.  If it enters in any way possible, then a moderately sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then the prepared bandage of mill dust, frankincense, and egg white is applied to the whole shoulder as it rotates from above. Then the ball is firmly tied under the armpit, and the bandage is rotated over it from above, and his hand is suspended to his neck.
He is left without moving his hand for seven days.  The patient's food should be limited so that the limb may strengthen, for this speeds its healing. Then, after seven or five days, it is untied and exercised. If it is stable and does not relax, then it is healed.
If the joint dislocates repeatedly due to moisture or another cause, cauterization with three cautery irons should be used as previously described in the chapter on cauterization. If all this is done and the bandage is removed after seven days and the joint is not stable,
the bandage and binding were repeated many times, but it did not stabilize, and it fell and relaxed, and you could not raise it upwards, then know that the nerve in the head of the shoulder has been severed, stretched, or relaxed. Then know that the joint will never stabilize in its place.
As for the fracture that occurs towards the chest and breast and towards the back, its reduction is by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns.  All the binding and treatment are used until it heals. If after healing there is stiffness in the limb and slowness in movement,
the patient should use the bath repeatedly until that stiffness softens and returns to its original nature.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the treatment of a fracture of the elbow.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty also. It dislocates in all directions, especially forwards or backwards. Its dislocation is not hidden from you, because it is under sight and touch, to whatever form it dislocates.
And if you compare the dislocated elbow with the sound one, that will be clearly apparent to you by the deformity of the joint, and he cannot bend the arm nor touch his shoulder with it.  And you should hasten and reduce the dislocation at once before a hot inflammation occurs to it.
For if a hot inflammation occurs to it, its reduction is difficult, and perhaps it will never heal at all, especially if the dislocation is backwards, for that is the worst of all types of dislocation, and the most painful, and often death follows it.
And its reduction, if it is something that can be reduced, is that a servant extends his hand with both his hands and his arm extended, and the physician's hands are above and below the elbow, and he pushes the joint with the thumbs of both his hands, or with the base of his palm, until it returns to its place.
And as for if the dislocation is forward, it may be reduced by bending the hand once until the base of its palm strikes the corresponding shoulder.  If the dislocation does not return to its place, then use very strong traction, which is that two servants extend the arm.
And two servants also hold the patient so that he does not move during the traction, then the arm is rotated in every direction after wrapping a folded cloth or wide bandage around his hand. And when the physician touches the joint with his hands, he should lubricate them with oil so that it will help in easily sliding the joint.
Then he pushes the joint strongly until it returns. After its return, a bandage should be applied to it, which contains constriction and drying, along with egg white, and it is tied tightly. The arm is then suspended from the patient's neck and left for several days.
Then it is untied. If the joint is stable in its place, then untie the bandage and leave it. And if you see that the joint is not yet firm, then reapply the bandage and leave it for some days until it becomes firm, then untie it. If swelling occurs after the return of the joint and its unbinding,
and difficulty in movement, then use moist applications in the bath and gentle massage and manipulation until it softens, or place a fat ram’s testicle on the joint, then bind it and leave it there for a day and a night, then remove it and have him enter the bath. When he sweats, massage the joint moderately.
Then reapply the testicle once, twice, and three times with the bath until it softens. And if you wish to apply a moist, light ox-bladder with fat and bind it, do so repeatedly, for it will soften and return to its former state.
Chapter Twenty-eight.
On the treatment of a dislocated wrist.
The wrist often dislocates, and its reduction is easier than that of other joints, except that it is necessary to hasten its reduction at the time it dislocates, before the area becomes inflamed or a hot inflammation appears.
To reduce it, place the patient's wrist on a board, and have an attendant extend his hand; the physician should place his hand on the prominence of the joint and push it until it returns. However, it is necessary to observe whether the dislocation has moved towards the palm of the hand.
Then let the patient place the back of his hand on the board during extension and reduction. And if the dislocation is prominent towards the back of the hand, let the palm of the hand be placed on the board so that the physician's hand falls on the prominence of the joint itself. If it returns immediately, well and good; otherwise, bind it with a bandage that is soothing to the inflammation.
And leave it; do not manipulate it again, for it will not tolerate it, and you will not be able to reduce it after several days have passed, except that the joint will remain crooked, and it will not harm the patient except if the hand becomes weak and unable to grasp anything.
Then you will know that the nerve has been severed or bruised; there is no remedy for it except cauterization, which may or may not help. When the reduction of the wrist is completed, apply the bandage that we described, then tighten it and leave it for five days.
Then unbind it and exercise the hand. If its movement is difficult and there is any stiffness in it, soften it with warm water and massage repeatedly until it softens.
Chapter twenty-nine.
In the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may be dislocated in any direction. If a finger is dislocated towards the back or palm of the hand, extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns. Then bind the tip of the finger and suspend it towards the direction in which it was dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it is straight that day. If it is at night, bind it in the same way, but continue to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night. Do this for several days until it strengthens.
Do the same if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand; bind it towards that same direction and do as you did before until it heals. Do likewise if it is dislocated in any other direction.
Chapter Thirty.
In the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
If a vertebra of the back or neck is completely dislocated, or if many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, there is no treatment, for death quickly overtakes the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's stool is involuntarily passed; he is unable to retain it, and often some of his limbs, either his legs or his arms, or one of them, become relaxed. But if only one vertebra is displaced, it often returns to its place.
And its displacement is in four directions; that which is displaced backward is called a hump. As for its treatment, if the hump has occurred from infancy, there is no treatment for it, nor is there any recovery at all. But that which has occurred from a fall or blow, or the like…
The ancients have mentioned it with various treatments in lengthy descriptions, most of which are not beneficial. I have abridged this, taking what is sufficient from their many approaches, drawing closer to the meaning and explaining it, and depicting the instrument differently from what they have shown and described.
I say that a hump which occurs in the front of the chest is incurable, and so is one on the sides. Only that which occurs in the back is treatable, in the manner I shall describe. It is to extend the patient on his face.
on a level bench near a wall, and spread a wet cloth beneath him so that his chest is not hurt. Then place a vertical wooden post fixed in a hole in the ground at one end of the bench, towards his head, and another wooden post towards his feet at the other end of the bench.
And a servant holds the post—let it not be firmly fixed in the hole—and another servant holds the other post in that manner. Then wrap around the patient's chest and under his armpits with a soft, strong bandage, and extend the end of the bandage to the post at his head and tie it there.
Then tighten with another bandage above his hips and above his knees and at his ankles. Then gather all the bandages and tie them to the other post at his feet. Then each servant pulls his post with the bandage, and the two posts do not move from their fixed positions.
except that they are not firmly fixed, as we said. And the physician places his hands on the hump forcefully until it returns, or he places a board on it, then leans on the board with his feet until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, then take a board about three cubits long.
and make a hole in the wall, which we said should be near the patient, into which the end of the board is inserted. Then place the middle of the board on the hump, and the physician places his feet on the other end and pulls strongly until he presses the hump.
and it returns to its place. And if you wish to make it with a hand-turned screw, then you drive two posts into the ground at the head of the patient at the end of the shop, a cubit long, and at his feet at the end of the shop also two posts.
Let the distance between each post be a span. And a hole is made in each post through which the screw passes, and all the wood is firmly fixed in the ground so that it does not move at all. And insert a round stick, which is the screw in which the bandage is twisted, into the holes of the two posts.
And at its end is a hole in which a stick a span long is firmly fixed with what is twisted, and in the other two posts the same. Then you tighten the bandage which you tightened on the chest of the patient in the screw at his head, and the bandages which you tightened on his legs in the screw at his feet.
Then a servant stands at each screw, turning his hand with the wrench with which he turns the screw, and the physician straightens the hump as we have explained. And this is an image of the screw, the shop, and the patient:
Then after the vertebra returns and the place is straightened, it is necessary to apply a drying bandage with egg white, then lint, then place on top of the bandage a splint of wood whose width is three fingers or so, and its length is as much as the hump takes up.
And on some sound beads, then it is bound with the appropriate bandage, and the patient uses light food until he is healed. If some protrusion remains in the place at the end of healing, then it is necessary to use medicines that relax and soften, along with using the splint we described for a long time.
And a lead plate may be used for that. And a protrusion may appear at the end of the vertebrae of the back, and it may be thought to be a dislocation, and it may be an extra bone that has protruded. Do not treat it with this treatment, for death may result.
Chapter thirty-one.
In the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and they are not subject to what happens to other joints of slight displacement and depression. The hip joint dislocates in four ways.
That is, it dislocates inwards, outwards, forwards and backwards. Most often it dislocates inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards. The sign of its dislocation inwards is that if you compare the sound leg of the patient with the diseased one, it will be longer.
And the knee will be more bent than the sound one, and the patient will not be able to bend his leg at the groin, and the area near the groin will be noticeably swollen, because the head of the femur has gone there.
And the sign of that which is subject to dislocation outwards is that its symptoms are opposite to these symptoms. And the sign of that which is subject to dislocation forwards is that he extends his leg completely, but he cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he wants to walk, he cannot do so forwards.
And he retains his urine, and his groin swells, and when walking, his weight is on his heel. And the sign of that which is subject to dislocation backwards is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the groin, and his leg is also shorter than the other.
And the groin is relaxed, and the head of the femur is clearly at the position of the loin. As for the reduction of this type of dislocation, you should look; if the dislocation is old, it has become chronic with its owner and he has not attempted to reduce it, and it remains in its state, then there is no treatment at all.
Then you should not attempt it.  As for one whose dislocation is recent, and whatever its type may be from among the four types of dislocation, hasten to twist the joint, and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left; perhaps...
it returned and did not need other treatment. If it does not return in this way, then you should prepare a strong, capable assistant who will extend his leg from below, either with his hands or with a bandage tied around his leg above the knee, and another assistant who will extend it from above.
so that he inserts his hands under his armpits, then tightens a soft bandage on the root of the thigh, and another third assistant holds the end of the bandage, and the extension will be either from the front, from the side of the groin to the side of the clavicle, or from behind to the side of the back.
And their extension will all be at once, until the patient is raised from the ground with his body and remains suspended.  For this type of extension is a common type for the four types. If the dislocation returns as we said, otherwise, each type needs the special treatment that I will mention.
As for its special reduction, if the dislocation is inwards, then the patient should lie on his sound side, then a bandage is applied to the root of the thigh between the head of the thigh and the place under the groin.
Then the bandage is extended upwards from the groin towards the upper body to the clavicle, then another strong, capable assistant takes it with his arms and embraces the thick part of the affected thigh and extends it outwards with a strong pull, then it returns to its place.
And this type is easier than the other types of treatment by which this member is reduced. If it is difficult for you and it does not respond to this type of treatment at all, then you should bind both legs of the patient with a strong, soft bandage on the heels and on the knees.
And the distance between each one and its neighbor will be about four fingers, and the affected leg will be extended more than the other by about two fingers. Then the patient will be suspended by the head from a wooden beam in the house, and his distance from the ground will be about two cubits.
Then you order a strong boy to embrace the head of the thigh, and another boy will hang onto the patient, and the other boy embracing the thigh pushes forcefully, then the joint returns to its place quickly. As for its special reduction, if the dislocation is outwards.
The patient should lie on the bench as we described for the humpbacked person, and the bandage is tightened on his affected leg especially, and on his chest. Then the two pieces of wood are placed, one at his feet and the other at his head. Then an extra piece of wood is placed in the middle of the bench, firmly secured.
A wet cloth is wrapped around it so that it does not injure the patient. The wood is placed between his thighs so that he does not slip downwards when pulled. Then each servant pulls towards his side, and the physician adjusts the hip with his hands; if it responds to reduction,
otherwise, place the board on him and compress it as we mentioned in the case of humpback, except that the patient should lie on his healthy side. And if the dislocation is forward, then the patient's leg should be extended once while he is in this same position on the bench.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected hip, then presses it with the other hand. With that, he makes the pressure extended downwards towards the knee. If the dislocation is backwards, the patient should not be extended downwards.
He is raised off the ground, but rather should be placed on something solid, just as it should also be if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we mentioned of his lying on the bench on his face, with the bandages tightened.
as we said before.  It is also necessary to use compression with the board on the place to which the joint has gone. When the hip joint is completely reduced as it should be—and the sign of its reduction will not be hidden from you; it is that you extend the patient's legs—
If you see them even, and the patient bends and straightens his leg without difficulty, then know that the limb has returned as it should. Then bring the thighs together, apply the bandage, and bind it firmly with a turban so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain still for three or four days. Then loosen the bandage and dressing, and compare the leg with the other. If you see them equal in size, know that the reduction is secure, and allow the patient to walk. If you see some laxness in it,
then re-bandage and tighten it as you did before, and leave it also for three days. Then unbind it, and let him walk slowly on it for days until it is strong, God willing.
Chapter thirty-two.
On the treatment of dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways: inwards, outwards, and downwards, that is, backwards; it is never dislocated forwards.  The sign of its dislocation is that you order the patient to bring his leg to his thigh.
If it does not adhere to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated. The treatment for all types of its dislocation is that you seat the patient sitting with his legs extended, if he is able to do so, and a servant sits behind him to hold his waist and bend him slightly backwards; then you sit on his thighs.
And you place your back against his face and put his leg between your legs, then you grasp his knee with your hands, interlacing your fingers on his knee; then you press with your hands strongly on both sides of his knee, and another servant extends his leg until the knee returns to its place.
The sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh softly and without force. Then bandage it and bind the leg to the thigh, and bind them both together with a bandage for three or four days, then release them.  He should only walk a little for a few days until it strengthens.
If you are unable to return it by the method we described, then use strong traction with the bandages that I previously described in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
In the treatment of a dislocated heel.
The heel may be slightly dislocated, or it may be completely dislocated.  Its dislocation is either inwards or outwards. The sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen and prominent towards the side to which it is dislocated.
As for the treatment of its slight dislocation, it is easy to reduce it; this is done by gently stretching it with the hands and straightening it until it returns. As for its treatment if it is completely dislocated, then the patient should sit, and a strong servant should hold him from behind his back at his waist.
Then you grasp his foot with your right hand from above and with your left hand from below the foot at the place of the heel. Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand and then with your left hand twice, then stretch it with your left hand and push the front of the foot with your right hand towards the leg without force.
Do this twice as we have described, then push the front of the foot to the leg on the third occasion while you pull at the heel. If it returns in one or two times in this way and you see the foot is straight, otherwise repeat the procedure, for it will return.
If it refuses to return by what we have described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground and plant a strong, firmly fixed peg in the ground so that it lies between his thighs, having wrapped a cloth around it so that it does not hurt the patient, then a servant should hold his thigh.
Then another servant stretches the leg, either with his hands or with a rope tied around the leg's neck. Then each servant stretches in the opposite direction to his companion, with the peg standing between the patient's thighs, holding it so that his body is not pulled downwards when stretched, then the physician straightens the joint with his hands.
And another servant holds the healthy leg downwards, for the joint returns quickly. When the joint returns and you are sure that it has returned correctly, then apply the bandage and splint, and tighten it with firm bandages, and tie the foot with the bandage below; and you should be wary of the nerve which is above the heel from behind.
lest the bandage be too tight and hurt him. Then leave it for two or three days; if the bandage loosens, tighten it, then release it on the third or fourth day, and the patient should abstain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this time, you cannot be certain that the joint will not become dislocated and spoiled and will not then accept treatment. If a hot swelling appears, you should use what we have previously described in other places of treatment and poultices to soothe it until it disappears.
Chapter thirty-four.
On the treatment of a dislocated toe.
You should set what is dislocated with a gentle pull without force; this is not difficult, but easy. If the dislocation is in one of the joints of the instep, the patient should place his foot on a level surface.
Or on a board while he is standing as if walking; then you should place your foot on the dislocated joint and press down firmly with your foot until it returns and you see that it is straight and there is no prominence in the area.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot that encompasses the whole foot and has two ends, then bind it tightly for three days, then unbind it and keep him from walking for many days until it strengthens and the return is prevented, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On the types of dislocation which occurs with a wound, or with a fracture, or with both together.
When something like that happens and you attempt its treatment and setting, it frequently results in death.
Therefore, no one should undertake the treatment of such a thing except one who is skillful in the art, long experienced, gentle, compassionate, deliberate, not rash nor impetuous, and that he should use in the beginning only medicines which calm hot swellings, and leave the patient alone.
To the extent that God wills, except that which you hope for safety from harm, with mildness of the disease and you see in it some hope, then restore it at once at the first, before the hot swelling occurs.
If the limb returns to what you intended, then use the treatment that calms hot swellings and treat the wound with what is suitable for it of drying ointments.  If the dislocation is with a fracture and splinters are detached in the bone, then remove them. And follow in that what we mentioned in the simple diseases, which preceded in their places.
And strive your best and refrain from entering the path of risk, according to my previous advice to you; that is more secure for your reputation and safer for your honor, God willing.
